The List of Contradictories for Simple Modal Propositions
Author lists contradictories of six simple modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of each of the six types of simple modal propositions from the list given in the text.

Core Idea
Every simple modal proposition has a unique contradictory based on its modality and quantification.

In this passage, the author provides a clear list of contradictories for six types of simple modal propositions: absolute necessary, general conditional, absolute temporal, absolute widespread, absolute perpetual, and general customary. Mastering this list is essential for handling compound propositions later. Let's unpack each pair and see the pattern.

Guided Path
1
Locate the six pairs in the text
The author lists six contradictory pairs for simple propositions. Find each pair in the Arabic text. For example: 'الضرورية المطلقة تناقضها الممكنة العامة' (absolute necessary is contradicted by general possible).
There are six pairs total; the pattern is that the contradictory often swaps the modality (e.g., necessary becomes possible) and changes quantifier.
2
Identify the modality of each proposition
Each proposition in the list has a specific modal term: ضرورية (necessary), مشروطة (conditional), وقتية (temporal), منتشرة (widespread), دائمة (perpetual), عرفية (customary). Note the quantifier (مطلقة general/absolute, etc.).
The contradictory is always a 'possible' or 'absolute' form, based on the original.
3
Analyze the contradictory pattern
Observe that the contradictory is often a 'possible' proposition (ممكنة) when the original is a 'necessary' or 'conditional' type. For example, the contradictory of the absolute necessary is the general possible. This reflects the logical opposition: if something is necessarily true, its opposite is possibly false.
The terms 'general', 'temporal', 'perpetual' etc. modify the possible to match the original's time scope.
4
Memorise the pairs
These pairs are foundational for further logical operations. Create a quick reference or mnemonic to remember which contradictory goes with which modal proposition.
The list is authoritative within this logical tradition; slight variations exist in other texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقائض البَسَائط ؛ لا يخفى عليه طَريقُ أَخذ نَقِيض المركَّباتِ . وإن غُمَّ عليه فلينظر إلى المشروطة الخاصَّة المركَّبة من مشروطة عامة ومطلقة عامة . فَنَقَائض البسائط: أنَّ الضَّروريّة المطلقة تناقضها الممكنة العامة، وأنَّ المشروطة العامَّةَ تُناقضها الممكنة الحينيَّةُ، وأنَّ الوقتية المطلقة تُناقضها الممكنة الوقتية، وأنَّ المنتشرة المطلقة تُناقضها الممكنة الدائمة، وأنَّ الدَّائمة المطلقة تُناقضها المطلقةُ العامَّةُ، وأنَّ العُرفيَّةَ العامَّةَ تُناقضُها المطلقه الحينيَّة . فالمشروطة الخاصَّة مركَّبة من مشروطة عامة موافقة لأصل القضيَّة في الكيف، ومن مطلقة عامة مخالفة له في الكيف أيضاً . فإِنَّ نقيضها : إمَّا الحينيَّة الممكنة المخالِفَةُ ، أو الدائمة الموافقة؛ لأنَّ نقيض الجزء الأول - أي: المشروطة العامة الموافقة - هو الحينيَّةُ الممكنة المخالفة، ونقيض الجزء الثَّاني - أي: المطلقة العامة المخالفة - هو الدائمة الموافقة .
The contradictories of simple propositions: the method of taking the contradictory of compound ones is not hidden. And if it is obscure for him, let him look at the special conditional composed of a general conditional and a general absolute. The contradictories of simple propositions: that the absolute necessary is contradicted by the general possible, and that the general conditional is contradicted by the temporal possible, and that the absolute temporal is contradicted by the temporal possible, and that the absolute widespread is contradicted by the perpetual possible, and that the absolute perpetual is contradicted by the general absolute, and that the general customary is contradicted by the temporal absolute. So the special conditional is composed of a general conditional agreeing with the original proposition in quality, and a general absolute disagreeing with it in quality as well. Indeed, its contradictory is either the temporal possible that disagrees, or the perpetual that agrees; because the contradictory of the first part—i.e., the agreeing general conditional—is the disagreeing temporal possible, and the contradictory of the second part—i.e., the disagreeing general absolute—is the agreeing perpetual.
Source pages p. 672

Commentary
This passage explains the contradictories (naqa'id) of both simple (basit) and compound (murakkab) propositions in Aristotelian logic as interpreted in the Islamic tradition. The author first assures that the method for finding the contradictory of a compound proposition is not difficult if one knows the contradictories of the simple ones. Then he lists the contradictories of six types of simple modal propositions: 1) The absolute necessary is contradicted by the general possible. 2) The general conditional is contradicted by the temporal possible. 3) The absolute temporal is contradicted by the temporal possible. 4) The absolute widespread is contradicted by the perpetual possible. 5) The absolute perpetual is contradicted by the general absolute. 6) The general customary is contradicted by the temporal absolute. These pairings are based on the definitions of these modalities in terms of existence and non-existence. After establishing these, he provides an example: the special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa) is a compound proposition whose parts are a general conditional (agreeing in quality with the original) and a general absolute (disagreeing in quality). To find its contradictory, one takes the contradictory of each part: the contradictory of the first part is the temporal possible (disagreeing), and the contradictory of the second part is the perpetual (agreeing). Since the whole compound functions like a conjunction in terms of contradictory? Actually, the contradictory of a compound of two parts is the disjunction of the contradictories of the parts (De Morgan). So the contradictory of the special conditional is 'either the temporal possible or the perpetual' — an 'or' statement. This is standard procedure: for a compound proposition of the form 'P and Q', its contradictory is 'not-P or not-Q'. Here the parts are considered as a kind of conjunction in the original proposition (though modal logic nuances apply). The example illustrates how to apply the rule to a specific modal compound.

Key Terms
نقائض البسائط
مشروطة خاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the contradictory of the 'absolute necessary' (الضرورية المطلقة)?
A) The general possible (الممكنة العامة)
B) The temporal possible (الممكنة الحينية)
C) The perpetual possible (الممكنة الدائمة)
D) The general absolute (المطلقة العامة)

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a simple modal proposition is determined by its modality and quantification, as listed in the six pairs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the contradictory of the proposition 'All prophets are truthful necessarily' (daruriyya mutlaqa) using the list from this lesson.
?
Given the proposition 'All believers are saved with temporal conditioned necessity' (mashruta 'amma), what is its contradictory? Explain your answer using the pattern from the text.

Finding the Contradictory of a Compound Proposition: The Special Conditional
Author explains the method for the special conditional as an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method for finding the contradictory of a compound modal proposition using the special conditional as a model.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a compound proposition is the disjunction of the contradictories of its parts.

After listing the contradictories of simple propositions, the author introduces a method for compound propositions. He uses the 'special conditional' (al-mashruta al-khassa) as an example. This compound is composed of two parts: a general conditional and a general absolute. By finding the contradictory of each part and combining them with 'or', we obtain the contradictory of the whole. Let's walk through this process step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parts of the compound
The special conditional is composed of two propositions: a general conditional that agrees with the original in quality, and a general absolute that disagrees in quality. In the text, these are: 'مشروطة عامة موافقة' (general conditional agreeing) and 'مطلقة عامة مخالفة' (general absolute disagreeing).
The 'original' refers to the proposition whose contradictory we are seeking; here it is the special conditional itself.
2
Find the contradictory of each part
Using the list of simple contradictories from earlier, the contradictory of the first part (general conditional) is the 'temporal possible' (الحينية الممكنة المخالفة). The contradictory of the second part (general absolute) is the 'perpetual' (الدائمة الموافقة). These are given directly in the text.
The text explicitly states: 'نقيض الجزء الأول ... هو الحينية الممكنة المخالفة، ونقيض الجزء الثاني ... هو الدائمة الموافقة'.
3
Combine with 'or'
The contradictory of a compound proposition is not the conjunction of the contradictories, but the disjunction. So the contradictory of the special conditional is: 'either the temporal possible that disagrees, or the perpetual that agrees' (إما الحينية الممكنة المخالفة أو الدائمة الموافقة).
This is analogous to De Morgan's law: ¬(P ∧ Q) ≡ ¬P ∨ ¬Q. Ensure you don't combine with 'and'.
4
Generalize the method
For any compound proposition composed of two parts (like a conjunction), its contradictory is the disjunction of the contradictories of those parts. The same method applies to longer compounds: break into parts, find each contradictory, and connect them with 'or'.
The author implies this method is general: if you know the contradictories of the simple parts, you can handle any compound.

The Contradictory of Composite Modal Propositions: The Rule
Dassuqi explains the method of deriving the contradictory of the particular conditional proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of a particular conditional (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa) as an exclusive disjunction of the contradictories of its two parts.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a composite modal is a disjunction, not a single proposition.

In Islamic logic, composite modal propositions like 'Necessarily every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes, not always' require a special method to find their contradictory. Unlike simple propositions, the contradictory is not a single statement but an exclusive disjunction of the contradictories of each component. This lesson teaches that method using the particular conditional as a foundation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the composite
A composite modal proposition consists of a general conditional (e.g., 'every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes') and a general absolute (e.g., 'not always'). Recognize that the proposition is an exclusive conjunction of these two parts.
2
Find the contradictory of the first part
The first part is a general conditional. Its contradictory is a possible-conditional (ḥīniyya mumkina) that differs in quality: if the first part is affirmative, the contradictory is negative, and vice versa. In the example, 'every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes' is contradicted by 'some writer does not move his fingers possibly while writing'.
3
Find the contradictory of the second part
The second part is a general absolute. Its contradictory is a perpetual (dāʾima) that agrees in quality. For 'not always' (a negative), the contradictory is 'some writer moves his fingers always' (affirmative, perpetual).
4
Combine into an exclusive disjunction
The contradictory of the whole composite is the exclusive disjunction (munfaṣila māniʿa al-khulūw) of the two contradictories: 'Either the contradictory of part 1 OR the contradictory of part 2'. This ensures at least one is true, and they cannot both be false.
This disjunction preserves the truth conditions of contradiction: it is always true and the original is always false.
5
Verify with the example
For 'Necessarily every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes, not always', the contradictory is 'Either some writer does not move his fingers possibly while writing, or some writer moves his fingers always'.
The disjunction uses the same subject and predicate but modally shifted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الثَّنَاقُضِ. فإِنَّ نقيضها : إمَّا الحينيَّة الممكنة المخالِفَةُ ، أو الدائمة الموافقة؛ لأنَّ نقيض الجزء الأول - أي: المشروطة العامة الموافقة - هو الحينيَّةُ الممكنة المخالفة، ونقيض الجزء الثَّاني - أي: المطلقة العامة المخالفة - هو الدائمة الموافقة. فإذا قلنا : بالضَّرورةِ كلُّ كاتب متحرّك الأصابع ما دام كاتباً لا دائماً»، فنقيضُها : إمَّا «ليس بعضُ الكاتب بمتحرك الأصابع بالإمكانِ الحيني»، وإما «بعض الكاتب متحرك الأصابع دائماً». وهذه هي المنفصلة المانعة الخلوّ المركبة من نقيضي الجزأين. ونذكر نقيض بقيَّةِ المركَّبَاتِ لِلتَّمرين، فنقولُ : أَمَّا العُرفيَّةُ الخاصَّةُ : - وهي كما تقدَّمَ - المركَّبةُ مِن عُرفيَّةٍ عامَّةٍ، ومُطلقة عامَّةٍ ، والعُرفيَّةُ العامَّةُ يُناقضُهَا المطلقه الحينيَّة . والمطلقة العامةُ يُناقضُها الدائمةُ المطلقة؛ نحو: كلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً لا دائماً ، فَنَقيضُها هكذا : دائماً إمَّا بعضُ الكاتب ليس بمتحرك الأصابع بالإطلاق حين هو كاتب، وإمَّا بعضُ الكاتب متحرك الأصابع دائماً . وأمَّا الوقتيَّةُ ؛ وتقدَّمَ أنَّها مُركَّبَةٌ مِن وقتتَيَّةٍ مُطلقةٍ، وَمِن مُطلقة عامة، ونقيضُ الوقتية المطلقة؛ الممكنة الوقتيَّةُ، والمطلقة العامَّةُ نقيضُها الدائمةُ المطلقةُ ؛ نحو: بالضَّرورةِ كلُّ قمرٍ مُنخسفٌ وقتَ الحيلولة لا دائماً ، فنقيضُها هكذا : دائماً إما بعضُ القمر ليسَ يمنخسف بالإمكان العام وقت الحيلولة، وإمَّا بعضُ القمر منخسف دائماً . وأما المنتشرة، وقد تقدَّمَ أنَّها مُركَّبةٌ من مُنتشرةٌ مُطلقة، وَمِن مُطلقة عامة، ونقيضُ المنتشرة المطلقة الممكنة الدائمة، ونقيضُ المطلقة العامة؛ الدائمة المطلقه؛ نحو: كلُّ قمر مُنخسف بالضَّرورةِ وقتاً ما لا دائماً، فنقيضُها هكذا دائماً : إمَّا بعضُ القمر ليس بمنخسف بالإمكان دائماً ، وإما بعضُ القمر منخسف دائماً .
Chapter on Contradiction. For its contradictory is either the possible-conditional that differs, or the perpetual that agrees; because the contradictory of the first part – i.e., the general conditional that agrees – is the possible-conditional that differs, and the contradictory of the second part – i.e., the general absolute that differs – is the perpetual that agrees. So if we say: 'Necessarily every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes, not always', then its contradictory is either 'Some writer does not move his fingers with possible-conditional' or 'Some writer moves his fingers always'. And this is the exclusive disjunction composed of the contradictories of the two parts. And we mention the contradictory of the remaining composite propositions for practice. As for the particular customary: – it is as previously – composed of a general customary and a general absolute; the general customary is contradicted by the absolute-conditional. And the general absolute is contradicted by the perpetual absolute; e.g.: 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes, not always', so its contradictory is thus: always either some writer is not moving his fingers absolutely while he is a writer, or some writer moves his fingers always. As for the particular temporal: – it is as previously – composed of a general temporal and a general absolute; the contradictory of the general temporal is the possible-temporal, and the general absolute's contradictory is the perpetual absolute; e.g.: 'Necessarily every moon is eclipsed at the time of opposition, not always', so its contradictory is thus: always either some moon is not eclipsed with general possibility at the time of opposition, or some moon is eclipsed always. As for the particular permissive: – it is as previously – composed of a general permissive and a general absolute; the contradictory of the general permissive is the possible-perpetual, and the contradictory of the general absolute is the perpetual absolute; e.g.: 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at some time, not always', so its contradictory is thus: always either some moon is not eclipsed with perpetual possibility, or some moon is eclipsed always.
Source pages p. 673

Commentary
This passage defines the method for constructing the contradictory (naqīḍ) of composite modal propositions (qadāyā murakkaba) in logic. The author focuses on four types: the particular conditional (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa), the particular customary (ʿurfiyya khāṣṣa), the particular temporal (waqtiyya), and the particular permissive (muntashira). Each composite proposition is made of two parts: a general proposition (e.g., general conditional) and a general absolute (muṭlaqa ʿāmma). The contradictory of the whole is not a single proposition but a disjunction (munfaṣila) of two propositions, each being the contradictory of one part. The disjunction is of the exclusive type (māniʿa al-khulūw), meaning at least one of the disjuncts is true. The author explains that the contradictory of the first part (general conditional) in the conditional particular is a possible-conditional (ḥīniyya mumkina) that differs in quality (if the original is affirmative, the contradictory is negative), while the contradictory of the second part (general absolute) is a perpetual (dāʾima) that agrees in quality. An example illustrates: 'Every writer moves his fingers while writing, not always' has two potential contradictories: 'Some writer does not move his fingers possibly while writing' and 'Some writer moves his fingers always'. The method is then applied to the other three composite propositions, each with its own modal specificities. For instance, the customary particular uses a general customary contradicted by an absolute-conditional (muṭlaqa ḥīniyya), and the temporal particular uses a general temporal contradicted by a possible-temporal (mumkina waqtiyya). This systematic approach helps students practice deriving contradictories for various modal combinations, a key skill in logical analysis.

Key Terms
نقيض
مشروطة خاصة
حينية ممكنة
دائمة
منفصلة مانعة الخلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the contradictory of the proposition 'Necessarily every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes, not always'?
A) Either some writer does not move his fingers possibly while writing, or some writer moves his fingers always.
B) Either every writer moves his fingers always, or some writer does not move his fingers while writing.
C) Some writer moves his fingers possibly while writing, and some writer does not move his fingers always.
D) Either some writer moves his fingers always, or every writer does not move his fingers while writing.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a composite modal proposition is an exclusive disjunction of the contradictories of its two parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct the contradictory of the proposition: 'Necessarily every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes, not always' by following the steps above.
?
Given the composite proposition 'Every fasting person breaks his fast at sunset, not always' (an example from fiqh), write its contradictory as an exclusive disjunction.

Contradicting Other Composite Modal Propositions
Dassuqi applies the method to three additional composite modal propositions for practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of contradiction to the particular customary, particular temporal, and particular permissive propositions.

Core Idea
Each composite type has different modal components, but the method remains: contradict each part and join with an exclusive disjunction.

In the previous lesson, we learned the rule for contradicting a particular conditional. Now we extend that method to three other types of composite modal propositions: the particular customary (ʿurfiyya khāṣṣa), particular temporal (waqtiyya), and particular permissive (muntashira). Each has a different modal structure for its first part, but the second part remains a general absolute. By mastering these variations, you will be able to handle any composite modal proposition in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Review the general method
For any composite modal proposition composed of a general first part and a general absolute second part, the contradictory is an exclusive disjunction of the contradictory of the first part and the contradictory of the second part.
The second part is always a general absolute, so its contradictory is always a perpetual absolute (dāʾima muṭlaqa) that agrees in quality.
2
Understand the first part of the particular customary
The particular customary (ʿurfiyya khāṣṣa) has a general customary (ʿurfiyya ʿāmma) as its first part. The contradictory of a general customary is an absolute-conditional (muṭlaqa ḥīniyya). In the example, 'every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes' (customary) is contradicted by 'some writer is not moving his fingers absolutely while he is a writer'.
Note the shift from customary to absolute modality.
3
Understand the first part of the particular temporal
The particular temporal (waqtiyya) has a general temporal (waqtiyya ʿāmma) as its first part. Its contradictory is a possible-temporal (mumkina waqtiyya). For 'every moon is eclipsed at the time of opposition', the contradictory is 'some moon is not eclipsed with general possibility at the time of opposition'.
4
Understand the first part of the particular permissive
The particular permissive (muntashira) has a general permissive (muntashira ʿāmma) as its first part. Its contradictory is a possible-perpetual (mumkina dāʾima). For 'every moon is eclipsed necessarily at some time', the contradictory is 'some moon is not eclipsed with perpetual possibility'.
5
Construct the full contradictory disjunctions
For each type, combine the contradictory of the first part with the contradictory of the second part (always 'some... always' or 'some... not always' as needed) using an exclusive 'or'. For example: 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes, not always' (customary) → 'Either some writer is not moving his fingers absolutely while he is a writer, or some writer moves his fingers always'.
The disjunction must be exclusive (māniʿa al-khulūw): at least one is true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الثَّنَاقُضِ. فإِنَّ نقيضها : إمَّا الحينيَّة الممكنة المخالِفَةُ ، أو الدائمة الموافقة؛ لأنَّ نقيض الجزء الأول - أي: المشروطة العامة الموافقة - هو الحينيَّةُ الممكنة المخالفة، ونقيض الجزء الثَّاني - أي: المطلقة العامة المخالفة - هو الدائمة الموافقة. فإذا قلنا : بالضَّرورةِ كلُّ كاتب متحرّك الأصابع ما دام كاتباً لا دائماً»، فنقيضُها : إمَّا «ليس بعضُ الكاتب بمتحرك الأصابع بالإمكانِ الحيني»، وإما «بعض الكاتب متحرك الأصابع دائماً». وهذه هي المنفصلة المانعة الخلوّ المركبة من نقيضي الجزأين. ونذكر نقيض بقيَّةِ المركَّبَاتِ لِلتَّمرين، فنقولُ : أَمَّا العُرفيَّةُ الخاصَّةُ : - وهي كما تقدَّمَ - المركَّبةُ مِن عُرفيَّةٍ عامَّةٍ، ومُطلقة عامَّةٍ ، والعُرفيَّةُ العامَّةُ يُناقضُهَا المطلقه الحينيَّة . والمطلقة العامةُ يُناقضُها الدائمةُ المطلقة؛ نحو: كلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً لا دائماً ، فَنَقيضُها هكذا : دائماً إمَّا بعضُ الكاتب ليس بمتحرك الأصابع بالإطلاق حين هو كاتب، وإمَّا بعضُ الكاتب متحرك الأصابع دائماً . وأمَّا الوقتيَّةُ ؛ وتقدَّمَ أنَّها مُركَّبَةٌ مِن وقتتَيَّةٍ مُطلقةٍ، وَمِن مُطلقة عامة، ونقيضُ الوقتية المطلقة؛ الممكنة الوقتيَّةُ، والمطلقة العامَّةُ نقيضُها الدائمةُ المطلقةُ ؛ نحو: بالضَّرورةِ كلُّ قمرٍ مُنخسفٌ وقتَ الحيلولة لا دائماً ، فنقيضُها هكذا : دائماً إما بعضُ القمر ليسَ يمنخسف بالإمكان العام وقت الحيلولة، وإمَّا بعضُ القمر منخسف دائماً . وأما المنتشرة، وقد تقدَّمَ أنَّها مُركَّبةٌ من مُنتشرةٌ مُطلقة، وَمِن مُطلقة عامة، ونقيضُ المنتشرة المطلقة الممكنة الدائمة، ونقيضُ المطلقة العامة؛ الدائمة المطلقه؛ نحو: كلُّ قمر مُنخسف بالضَّرورةِ وقتاً ما لا دائماً، فنقيضُها هكذا دائماً : إمَّا بعضُ القمر ليس بمنخسف بالإمكان دائماً ، وإما بعضُ القمر منخسف دائماً .
Chapter on Contradiction. For its contradictory is either the possible-conditional that differs, or the perpetual that agrees; because the contradictory of the first part – i.e., the general conditional that agrees – is the possible-conditional that differs, and the contradictory of the second part – i.e., the general absolute that differs – is the perpetual that agrees. So if we say: 'Necessarily every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes, not always', then its contradictory is either 'Some writer does not move his fingers with possible-conditional' or 'Some writer moves his fingers always'. And this is the exclusive disjunction composed of the contradictories of the two parts. And we mention the contradictory of the remaining composite propositions for practice. As for the particular customary: – it is as previously – composed of a general customary and a general absolute; the general customary is contradicted by the absolute-conditional. And the general absolute is contradicted by the perpetual absolute; e.g.: 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes, not always', so its contradictory is thus: always either some writer is not moving his fingers absolutely while he is a writer, or some writer moves his fingers always. As for the particular temporal: – it is as previously – composed of a general temporal and a general absolute; the contradictory of the general temporal is the possible-temporal, and the general absolute's contradictory is the perpetual absolute; e.g.: 'Necessarily every moon is eclipsed at the time of opposition, not always', so its contradictory is thus: always either some moon is not eclipsed with general possibility at the time of opposition, or some moon is eclipsed always. As for the particular permissive: – it is as previously – composed of a general permissive and a general absolute; the contradictory of the general permissive is the possible-perpetual, and the contradictory of the general absolute is the perpetual absolute; e.g.: 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at some time, not always', so its contradictory is thus: always either some moon is not eclipsed with perpetual possibility, or some moon is eclipsed always.
Source pages p. 673

Commentary
This passage defines the method for constructing the contradictory (naqīḍ) of composite modal propositions (qadāyā murakkaba) in logic. The author focuses on four types: the particular conditional (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa), the particular customary (ʿurfiyya khāṣṣa), the particular temporal (waqtiyya), and the particular permissive (muntashira). Each composite proposition is made of two parts: a general proposition (e.g., general conditional) and a general absolute (muṭlaqa ʿāmma). The contradictory of the whole is not a single proposition but a disjunction (munfaṣila) of two propositions, each being the contradictory of one part. The disjunction is of the exclusive type (māniʿa al-khulūw), meaning at least one of the disjuncts is true. The author explains that the contradictory of the first part (general conditional) in the conditional particular is a possible-conditional (ḥīniyya mumkina) that differs in quality (if the original is affirmative, the contradictory is negative), while the contradictory of the second part (general absolute) is a perpetual (dāʾima) that agrees in quality. An example illustrates: 'Every writer moves his fingers while writing, not always' has two potential contradictories: 'Some writer does not move his fingers possibly while writing' and 'Some writer moves his fingers always'. The method is then applied to the other three composite propositions, each with its own modal specificities. For instance, the customary particular uses a general customary contradicted by an absolute-conditional (muṭlaqa ḥīniyya), and the temporal particular uses a general temporal contradicted by a possible-temporal (mumkina waqtiyya). This systematic approach helps students practice deriving contradictories for various modal combinations, a key skill in logical analysis.

C) Either some moon is not eclipsed with perpetual possibility, or some moon is eclipsed always.
D) Always either every moon is eclipsed with possibility, or no moon is eclipsed always.

Key Takeaway
For every composite modal proposition, the contradictory is an exclusive disjunction of the contradictories of its two parts, with specific modal adjustments for the first part.

Constructing the Contradictory of Compound Modal Propositions
Author demonstrates constructing contradictories of six compound modal propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of any compound modal proposition by identifying its component propositions, finding their contradictories, and forming an exclusive disjunction.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a compound modal proposition is always an exclusive disjunction of the contradictories of its parts.

Compound modal propositions like 'every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes, not always' have a specific logical structure. To find their contradictory, we cannot simply negate the whole; we must break it into its component modal propositions, find the contradictory of each, and combine them with 'either...or...'. This passage demonstrates the method across six types of compound propositions, providing a systematic procedure for immediate inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two component propositions
Each compound modal proposition is composed of two simpler modal propositions (e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes' and 'not always'). Determine the exact phrasing and modality of each part.
The compound is a conjunction of these two parts, even if not explicitly marked.
2
Determine the modal type of each component
Classify each component as one of the modal types: general customary ('urfiyya ʿāmma), temporal absolute (waqtiyya muṭlaqa), widespread absolute (muntashira muṭlaqa), general absolute (muṭlaqa ʿāmma), general possible (mumkina ʿāmma), etc. This is necessary to apply the correct contradictory rule.
The passage lists the types for each compound; refer to earlier definitions if needed.
3
Find the contradictory of each component
Apply the rules of modal opposition: e.g., the contradictory of a general absolute (muṭlaqa ʿāmma) is an absolute perpetual (dā'ima muṭlaqa); the contradictory of a temporal absolute is a temporal possible (mumkina waqtiyya); the contradictory of a general possible is an absolute necessary (ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa); the contradictory of a general customary is a temporal absolute (muṭlaqa ḥīniyya); the contradictory of a widespread absolute is a perpetual possible (mumkina dā'ima).
The passage provides these pairings explicitly.
4
Form the disjunction (manfāṣila māni'at al-khuluw)
Combine the two contradictories using 'imma... wa-imma...' (either...or...). This disjunction is an exclusive disjunction that allows both disjuncts to be false (inclusive OR), which correctly negates the original conjunction.
The disjunction is true if at least one disjunct is true; the original compound is true when both parts are true, so its contradictory must be false when both parts are true, i.e., at least one part false.
5
Verify by example
Check the disjunction against a concrete instance: e.g., for 'Every writer moves fingers as long as he writes, not always', the contradictory 'Either some writer does not move fingers by temporal possibility or some writer moves fingers always' is indeed the correct contradictory because both disjuncts are false (as noted in the commentary), making the disjunction false when the original is true.
The commentary explicitly states both disjuncts are false in the given example, confirming the construction.

(قَوْلُهُ : إِمَّا لَيْسَ بَعْضُ ... إلخ) هذا كاذب، وقوله: (وإمّا بعضُ . . . إلخ)؛ هذا كاذب. So if we say: 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes, not always,' then its contradictory is either 'Some writer does not move his fingers by temporal possibility' or 'Some writer moves his fingers always.' And this is the exclusive disjunction (māniʿat al-khuluw) composed of the contradictories of the two parts. (His statement: 'Either not some... etc.') This is false, and his statement: 'Or some... etc.'; this is false. (His statement: 'And this') meaning the mentioned contradictory, and he used the feminine pointer considering it as a proposition, and his statement: 'from the contradictories of the two parts' meaning the two parts of the conditional, and we mention the contradictory of the remaining compounds for practice, and we say: As for the special customary (al-ʿurfiyya al-khāṣṣa) – and it is, as previously, composed of a general customary and a general absolute, and the contradictory of the general customary is the temporal absolute, and the contradictory of the general absolute is the absolute perpetual; e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes, not always,' so its contradictory is thus: 'Always either some writer does not move his fingers absolutely while he is writing, or some writer moves his fingers always.' As for the temporal (al-waqtiyya); it was previously that it is composed of a temporal absolute and a general absolute, and the contradictory of the temporal absolute is the temporal possible, and the contradictory of the general absolute is the absolute perpetual; e.g., 'Necessarily, every moon is eclipsed at the time of opposition, not always,' so its contradictory is thus: 'Always either some moon is not eclipsed by general possibility at the time of opposition, or some moon is eclipsed always.' As for the widespread (al-muntashira), it was previously that it is composed of a widespread absolute and a general absolute, and the contradictory of the widespread absolute is the perpetual possible, and the contradictory of the general absolute is the absolute perpetual; e.g., 'Every moon is necessarily eclipsed at some time, not always,' so its contradictory is thus: 'Always either some moon is not eclipsed by perpetual possibility, or some moon is eclipsed always.' As for the non-perpetual existential (al-wujūdiyya al-lā-dāʾima), it was previously that it is composed of two general absolutes, and the contradictory of the general absolute is the absolute perpetual; e.g., 'Every human is actually asleep, not always,' so its contradictory is thus: 'Either some human is not asleep always, or some human is asleep always.' As for the non-necessary existential (al-wujūdiyya al-lā-ḍarūriyya), it was previously that it is composed of a general absolute and a general possible, and the contradictory of the general absolute is the absolute perpetual, and the contradictory of the general possible is the absolute necessary; e.g., 'Every human is asleep not necessarily,' so its contradictory is thus: 'Either some human is not asleep always, or some human is asleep necessarily.' As for the special possible (al-mumkina al-khāṣṣa), it was previously that it is composed of two general possibles, and that the contradictory of the general possible is the absolute necessary; e.g., 'Every human is asleep by special possibility,' so its contradictory is thus: 'Either some human is not asleep necessarily, or some human is asleep necessarily.'
Source pages p. 674

Commentary
This passage is a practical exercise in constructing the contradictory (naqīḍ) of compound modal propositions. The author begins with an example: the proposition 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he writes, not always' is a general customary conditional (ʿurfiyya ʿāmma). Its contradictory is a disjunction (manfāṣila māniʿat al-khuluw) composed of the contradictories of its two parts: either 'Some writer does not move his fingers by temporal possibility' or 'Some writer moves his fingers always.' The commentary immediately notes that both disjuncts are false in this case, which is fine because the contradictory as a disjunction is true if at least one part is true. The author then lists several types of compound propositions, each composed of two simpler modal propositions. For each, he states the contradictory of each component and then gives an example with its contradictory disjunction. The types covered are: (1) the special customary (ʿurfiyya khāṣṣa), composed of a general customary and a general absolute; (2) the temporal (waqtiyya), composed of a temporal absolute and a general absolute; (3) the widespread (muntashira), composed of a widespread absolute and a general absolute; (4) the non-perpetual existential (wujūdiyya lā-dāʾima), composed of two general absolutes; (5) the non-necessary existential (wujūdiyya lā-ḍarūriyya), composed of a general absolute and a general possible; (6) the special possible (mumkina khāṣṣa), composed of two general possibles. For each, the contradictory of the compound is given as a disjunction of the contradictories of its parts, structured as a māniʿat al-khuluw (exclusive disjunction allowing both false) because the compound is true if both parts are true, so its contradictory is the negation of the conjunction, which is a disjunction of the negations. The examples use concrete natural and human phenomena illustrating the modal distinctions.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of any compound modal proposition is an exclusive disjunction of the contradictories of its two component propositions.

The Contradictory of a Conjunction is a Disjunction
Author defends the disjunctive formulation of the contradictory of a compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the contradictory of a compound (conjunctive) proposition is expressed as a disjunction, using the concept of necessary coimplicate (lāzim musāwī).

Core Idea
Negating a conjunction requires a disjunction, not another conjunction.

Many students mistakenly think that to negate a compound proposition like 'A and B', you must say 'not A and not B'. This passage clarifies that the correct negation is a disjunction: 'not A or not B'. The author shows that the negation of the whole (rafʿ al-majmūʿ) is achieved by negating any one part. This equivalence is called a necessary coimplicate (lāzim musāwī), meaning the disjunction and the negation of the conjunction imply each other.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound proposition
Recognize that a compound proposition (qadiyya murakkaba) in this context is a conjunction (sum of two propositions). For example, in fiqh: 'The prayer is valid and the wudu is complete.'
2
Understand the objection
The objection is: if the compound is a conjunction, its contradictory should be the negation of both parts together, i.e., 'The prayer is not valid and the wudu is not complete.' But the author proposes a disjunction: 'Either the prayer is not valid or the wudu is not complete.' Why?
3
Apply the rule of negating a conjunction
The negation of a conjunction (A and B) is (not A) or (not B). This is because the conjunction is false if at least one part is false. So the contradictory (naqiḍ) of 'A and B' is 'not A or not B'. This is called 'rafʿ al-majmūʿ' (removal of the sum) achieved by 'rafʿ aḥad al-juz'ayn' (removing one part).
This is De Morgan's law in classical logic.
4
Recognize the necessary coimplicate
The disjunction is logically equivalent (lāzim musāwī) to the negation of the conjunction. So the disjunction correctly expresses the contradictory, even though it does not explicitly negate both parts together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإطلاق النَّقيض على هذا المفهوم المردَّدِ باعتبار أنَّه لازم مساو الدسوقي وأمَّا الوجوديَّةُ اللادائمة ... (قَوْلُهُ : وَإِطْلَاقُ النَّقِيض ... إِلَحْ) جواب سؤال تقديرُهُ : إِنَّ القضيَّةِ المركَّبَةَ مُركَّبَةٌ من قضيَّتين، وحينئذ؛ فيكون نقيضُها رفع كلِّ مِنَ القضيَّتين، بأنْ يُقالَ : إِنَّهُما لَيْسَا كذلك، والمفهوم المردَّدُ الَّذي هو منفصلةٌ؛ مُشتمل على رَفْعِ أَحدِ النَّقيضَيْنِ؛ لأنَّ قولنا : النَّقيضُ إِمَّا كَذَا وإِمَّا كَذَا؛ رَفْع أحد الجزئين فقط ؛ أي: رفع لواحدٍ منهما غير معين، وحينئذ ؛ فَلَا يكون المفهوم المذكور نقيضاً ، فأجابَ بِمَا ذكر . وحاصلهُ أنَّ المركَّبةَ لَمَّا كانَتْ عبارة عن مجموع قضيَّتَيْنِ ؛ فَنقيضُها رفعُ المجموع ، ورفعه يحصل برفع أحد الجزئين. (قَوْلُهُ : لَازِمٌ مُسَاءٍ لِلنَّقِيْضِ ) أي : فيلزم من وجودِ أحدهما وجود الآخر، فإذا وجد رفع أحد الجزئين ؛ وجد رفع المجموع ؛ لأنَّ الكلّ يرتفعُ برفع جزيْهِ كَمَا لا يخفى.
And applying the term 'contradictory' to this disjunctive concept (the indefinite concept) is because it is a necessary coimplicate (lāzim musāwī). Ad-Dasūqī: As for the non-perpetual existential... (His statement: 'And applying the term contradictory ... etc.) is an answer to an implied question: that the compound proposition is composed of two propositions, and then its contradictory would be the removal of both propositions, by saying 'they are not so,' while the disjunctive concept (the indefinite concept) which is a disjunction includes the removal of one of the two contradictories; because our saying 'the contradictory is either this or that' is the removal of one of the two parts only; i.e., the removal of one of them unspecified. Then the mentioned concept would not be the contradictory. So he answered with what he mentioned. Its gist is that since the compound is an expression of the sum of two propositions, its contradictory is the removal of the sum, and its removal occurs by removing one of the two parts. (His statement: 'a necessary coimplicate of the contradictory') i.e., it follows from the existence of one the existence of the other. So if the removal of one of the two parts exists, then the removal of the sum exists, because the whole is removed by the removal of its part, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 675

Commentary
This passage addresses a theoretical objection: if a compound proposition consists of two conjuncts, then its contradictory should negate both conjuncts simultaneously (i.e., say 'both are false'). But the author's formulation of the contradictory is a disjunction: 'Either not A or not B.' This seems to negate only one conjunct, not both. The response explains that the contradictory of a conjunction (which is a compound true when both parts are true) is the negation of the whole conjunction. By De Morgan's law, the negation of (A and B) is (not A) or (not B). So removing the whole (the sum) is achieved by removing one part, because if even one part is false, the conjunction is false. Therefore, the disjunction is a necessary coimplicate (lāzim musāwī) of the contradictory: whenever the contradictory (the negation of the conjunction) holds, the disjunction holds, and vice versa. Thus, the disjunction is a correct expression of the contradictory, even though it doesn't explicitly negate both parts simultaneously. The commentary stresses that 'رفع أحد الجزئين' is sufficient to negate the conjunction. This is a standard logical point, often confusing for students who think the contradictory must be a simple negation of each part.

رفع المجموع

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the contradictory of a compound proposition expressed as a disjunction rather than a conjunction of negations?
A) Because the compound is a disjunction, so its negation is a conjunction.
B) Because the compound is a conjunction, and its negation is a disjunction of the negations of its parts.
C) Because the compound is a conditional, so its negation is a conjunction.
D) Because the compound is indefinite, so its negation is indefinite.

Key Takeaway
The negation of a conjunction is logically equivalent to a disjunction of the negations of its parts, so the contradictory of a compound proposition is correctly expressed as a disjunction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Finding the Contradictory of Compound Modal Propositions
Explains how to form the contradictory of three types of compound modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of a compound modal proposition by forming the appropriate disjunctive concept.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a compound modal proposition is a disjunction of the contradictories of its parts, not a conjunction.

In this passage, the commentators explain a subtle point: when a proposition is composed of two modal components, its contradictory is not simply the negation of each component together. Rather, it is a disjunctive concept that negates the whole by negating just one part. This is because the whole is false if any part is false. Understanding this clarifies how contradictory relations work in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound modal proposition
Look for propositions that are explicitly composed of two simpler modal propositions, such as 'Every human is actually sleeping, not permanently' (al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima).
These are often marked by compound qualifiers like 'not permanently' or 'not necessarily'.
2
Extract the two component propositions
For the non-permanent existential, the components are a general absolute ('Every human is actually sleeping') and its negation in the form of a permanent absolute ('Not every human is sleeping permanently').
The components are general modal propositions.
3
Find the contradictory of each component
Use the standard rules: the contradictory of a general absolute is a permanent absolute, the contradictory of a general possible is a necessary absolute, etc.
The passage provides the specific contradictories for each case.
4
Form the disjunction
The contradictory of the whole compound is a disjunction (either ... or ...) of the two component contradictories. For the non-permanent existential: 'Either some human is not permanently sleeping, or some human is permanently sleeping.'
The disjunction is exclusive (man'at al-khuluw) because the two negations cannot both be absent.
5
Understand the justification
The contradictory of the whole is the negation of the whole. Negating one part suffices to negate the whole. The disjunctive concept is not the negation itself but its necessary equivalent.
This is why the term 'contradictory' is applied leniently.

فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَافُض
وإطلاق النَّقيض على هذا المفهوم المردَّدِ باعتبار أنَّه لازم مساو
الدسوقي
وأمَّا الوجوديَّةُ اللادائمة، وقد سلفَ أنَّها مُركَّبةٌ من مطلقتين عامتين، ونقيضُ المطلقة العامة؛ الدائمة المطلقة، نحو: كلُّ إنسان نائم بالفعل لا دائماً، فَنقيضُها: هكذا إمَّا بعضُ الإنسان ليس بنائم دائماً، وإمَّا بعضُ الإنسانِ نائم دائماً . وأمَّا الوجوديَّةُ اللَّاضروريّة؛ وقد سبق أنّها مُركَّبةٌ مِن مُطلقةٍ عامَّةٍ وَمِن مُمكنةٍ عامة، ونقيضُ المطلقة العامَّةِ؛ الدَّائمةُ المطلقة، ونقيضُ الممكنة العامَّةِ؛ الضَّروريَّةُ المطلقه؛ نحو: كلُّ إنسان نائمٌ لا بالضَّرورةِ، فَنقيضُها هكذا: إمَّا بعضُ الإنسانِ ليس بنائم دائماً، وإمَّا بعضُ الإنسانِ نائمُ بالضَّرورةِ. وأما الممكنة الخاصة، وقد مَضى أنَّها مُركَّبَةٌ مِن مُمكنتين عامتين، وأنَّ الممكنةَ العامَّةَ نقيضُها الضَّروريّةُ المطلقة نحو: كلُّ إنسان نائم بالإمكان الخاص، فنقيضُها هكذا: إمَّا بعضُ الإنسان ليس بنائم بالضرورة، وإمَّا بعضُ الإنسانِ نائم بالضَّرورة. (قَوْلُهُ : وَإِطْلَاقُ النَّقِيض ... إِلَحْ) جواب سؤال تقديرُهُ : إِنَّ القضيَّةِ المركَّبَةَ مُركَّبَةٌ من قضيَّتين، وحينئذ؛ فيكون نقيضُها رفع كلِّ مِنَ القضيَّتين، بأنْ يُقالَ : إِنَّهُما لَيْسَا كذلك، والمفهوم المردَّدُ الَّذي هو منفصلةٌ؛ مُشتمل على رَفْعِ أَحدِ النَّقيضَيْنِ؛ لأنَّ قولنا : النَّقيضُ إِمَّا كَذَا وإِمَّا كَذَا؛ رَفْع أحد الجزئين فقط ؛ أي: رفع لواحدٍ منهما غير معين، وحينئذ؛ فَلَا يكون المفهوم المذكور نقيضاً، فأجابَ بِمَا ذكر . وحاصلهُ أنَّ المركَّبةَ لَمَّا كانَتْ عبارة عن مجموع قضيَّتَيْنِ ؛ فَنقيضُها رفعُ المجموع ، ورفعه يحصل برفع أحد الجزئين. (قَوْلُهُ : لَازِمٌ مُسَاءٍ لِلنَّقِيْضِ ) أي : فيلزم من وجودِ أحدهما وجود الآخر، فإذا وجد رفع أحد الجزئين ؛ وجد رفع المجموع ؛ لأنَّ الكلّ يرتفعُ برفع جزيْهِ كَمَا لا يخفى.
العطار
(1)
ذلك
(قَوْلُهُ: وَإِطْلَاقُ النَّقِيْضِ ... إِلَخ) هذا يوهمُ أنَّ إطلاقَهُ على ما تقدَّمَ كُلِّهِ ليسَ بهذا الاعتبار، وقد عَلِمْتَ مَا فيهِ سابقاً، ثم بقوله : (وَإِطلاقُ النَّقيض ... إلخ)
(1) (قَوْلُهُ : أي : رفع لواحد ... إلخ) فيه أن رفع الأحد للدائر بينهما في مانعة الخلو المجوزة للجمع هو عين النقيض للمركب، فالأولى تصوير الاعتراض بما قاله العطار من أنَّه لا اختلاف بين المفهوم وبين القضية المركبة في الإيجاب والسلب ولا في نوع القضية ولا في جهتها، فكيف يجعل المفهوم نقيضاً؟ والجواب : أنَّه لازم مساو للنقيضين لأنه مجموعهما . ا . هـ. الشرنوبي.
للنَّقيض لا باعتبار أنَّه نقيضُ حقيقةً؛ إذ نقيضُ الشَّيء بالحقيقة هو رفعُ ذلك الشيء .
والقضيَّة المركّبة لمَّا كانت عبارة عن مجموع قضيَّتين مختلفتين بالإيجاب والسَّلبِ؛ فَنقيضُها رفعُ ذلك المجموع، والمفهوم المردَّدُ ليس نفسَ الرَّفع، لكنَّه لازم مساو له تأمَّلْ .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ : إِذْ نَقِيضُ الشَّيْء... إلخ) علَّةٌ للنَّفي، وهو قوله: (لا باعتبار). (قَوْلُهُ : لَكِنَّهُ لَازِمٌ مُسَاءٍ لَهُ أي : لأنَّ المفهوم المردَّد رفع لأحد الجزئين لا على التَّعيين، وارتفاع ذلك الأحدِ لا يتحقق إلا بارتفاع المجموع الذي هو النقيض .
(قَوْلُهُ : تَأَمَّلْ أمر بالتأمل ؛ لكون المقام دقيقاً .
فإنْ قُلْتَ : مَا وجه تخصيص هذه بالتسامح ؟! ، فإنَّ جميع ما تقدَّمَ مِن الحمليَّاتِ أيضاً ليست بنقائض حقيقة، فإطلاق النقيض عليها تسامح، وذلك لأنَّ نقيض الشَّيء
العطار
يندفعُ مَا يُقالُ: إِنَّهُ لَا اختلاف بين المفهوم المردَّدِ والقضيَّةِ المركَّبة في الإيجاب والسَّلْبِ، ولا اتحادَ في النَّوعِ لِكون إحدَاهُمَا حَمليَّةٌ والأُخرَى مُنفصلةٌ، وَلَا في الجهةِ؛ لأنَّ المنفصلة لَيْسَتْ مِنَ الموجّهات في شيءٍ وإِنْ كانَ طرفَاهَا
اختلاف
هنا منها ؛ تَأَمَّلْ.
(قَوْلُهُ: فَنَقِيضُهَا ) أي : نقيضُ القضيَّةِ المركَّبةِ رفعُ ذلك المجموع، وبيانه أنَّ نقيض الجزء الأوَّلِ مِنَ القضيَّةِ المركَّبة، ونقيض الجزء الثاني منها لَمَّا كانَ عبارةً عَن رفعِ مَجموعِهِمَا ؛ لزم اجتماعُ الرَّفْعَيْنِ، وَلَمَّا لم يمكن اجتماعُ الرَّفْعَيْنِ في النَّقيض ؛ لزمَ مِن ذلكَ الرَّفْعَيْن حصول القضيَّةِ المنفصلة المانعة الخلو؛ لأنَّ الجزأين في المانعة الخلوّ يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، فيكون رفع الجزأين ملزوماً، والمفهوم المردَّدُ لازماً مُساوياً، فَإطلاق اسم النقيض على المفهوم المردَّدِ باعتبارِ أنَّه لازم مُساءٍ لِذينك الرفعينِ . قَوْلُهُ: تَأَمَّلْ) أي : فى المثالِ المذكور لتقيس البقيَّةَ عليه.
Section: On Contradiction. And the application of the term 'contradictory' to this disjunctive concept is by virtue of its being a necessary equivalent. Al-Dasuqi: As for the non-permanent existential, and it has been mentioned that it is composed of two absolute propositions, and the contradictory of the absolute general is the permanent absolute; for example: Every human is actually sleeping, not permanently, so its contradictory is thus: either some human is not permanently sleeping, or some human is permanently sleeping. And as for the non-necessary existential; and it has been mentioned that it is composed of an absolute general and a possible general, and the contradictory of the absolute general is the permanent absolute, and the contradictory of the possible general is the necessary absolute; for example: Every human is sleeping, not necessarily, so its contradictory is thus: either some human is not permanently sleeping, or some human is sleeping necessarily. And as for the particular possible, and it has been mentioned that it is composed of two general possibilities, and that the contradictory of the general possible is the necessary absolute; for example: Every human is sleeping by particular possibility, so its contradictory is thus: either some human is not sleeping by necessity, or some human is sleeping by necessity. (His saying: and the application of the contradictory ... etc.) is the answer to a presumed question: That the compound proposition is composed of two propositions, and then its contradictory would be the negation of each of the two propositions, by saying: They are not so, and the disjunctive concept which is a disjunctive proposition includes the negation of one of the two contradictories; because our saying: The contradictory is either such or such removes only one of the two parts; i.e., the removal of one of them unspecified. And then the mentioned concept would not be a contradictory. So he answered with what was mentioned. Its summary: Since the compound proposition is an expression of the sum of two propositions, its contradictory is the negation of the whole, and its negation occurs by the negation of one of the parts. (His saying: a necessary equivalent for the contradictory) i.e., from the existence of one follows the existence of the other. So if the negation of one of the parts exists, the negation of the whole exists; because the whole rises by the removal of its part, as is not hidden. Al-Attar: (His saying: and the application of the contradictory ... etc.) This gives the impression that its application to all that preceded is not by this consideration, and you have known what is in it previously, then by his saying: (and the application of the contradictory ... etc.) (1) (His saying: i.e., the removal of one ... etc.) In it: that the removal of one of the two that alternate in the exclusive disjunction that permits conjunction is exactly the contradictory of the compound. So the better way to portray the objection is with what al-Attar said: that there is no difference between the concept and the compound proposition in affirmation and negation, nor in the type of proposition, nor in its modality. So how could the concept be made a contradictory? And the answer: It is a necessary equivalent for the two contradictories because it is their sum. End. Al-Sharnubi. For the contradictory not by virtue of it being a contradictory in reality; for the contradictory of a thing in reality is the negation of that thing. And the compound proposition, since it is an expression of the sum of two propositions differing in affirmation and negation, its contradictory is the negation of that whole, and the disjunctive concept is not the negation itself, but it is a necessary equivalent for it. Reflect. Al-Dasuqi: (His saying: for the contradictory of a thing... etc.) is a cause for the negation, namely his saying: not by virtue. (His saying: but it is a necessary equivalent for it i.e., because the disjunctive concept is a negation of one of the two parts indeterminately, and the lifting of that one does not occur except by the lifting of the whole, which is the contradictory. (His saying: Reflect commands reflection because the position is subtle. If you say: What is the reason for restricting this to leniency? For all that preceded of categoricals are also not true contradictories, so the application of contradictory to them is leniency. And that is because the contradictory of a thing... Al-Attar: Repels what is said: that there is no difference between the disjunctive concept and the compound proposition in affirmation and negation, and no unity in type because one of them is categorical and the other is disjunctive, and not in modality because the disjunctive has nothing to do with modal propositions even if its terms are modal; reflect. (His saying: so its contradictory) i.e., the contradictory of the compound proposition is the negation of that whole, and its explanation: that the contradictory of the first part of the compound proposition and the contradictory of the second part of it, since they are an expression of the negation of both of them, the conjunction of the two negations is necessary. And since it is not possible for the two negations to be combined in the contradictory, from those two negations follows the production of the disjunctive proposition of exclusive disjunction because the two parts in exclusive disjunction can be combined but cannot both be removed. So the negation of both parts is a necessary condition, and the disjunctive concept is a necessary equivalent. So the application of the name 'contradictory' to the disjunctive concept is by virtue of it being a necessary equivalent for those two negations. His saying: Reflect i.e., on the example mentioned to analogize the rest to it.
Source pages p. 675

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the contradictory (naqid) of compound modal propositions. The commentator begins by stating that the term 'contradictory' is applied to the disjunctive concept only because it is a necessary equivalent (lazim musawin) of the actual contradictory. He then gives three examples: al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima (non-permanent existential), al-wujudiyya al-la-daruriyya (non-necessary existential), and al-mumkina al-khassa (particular possible). For each, he shows how its contradictory is a disjunctive proposition composed of the contradictories of its components. For instance, 'Every human is actually sleeping, not permanently' is a compound of a general absolute and its negation. Its contradictory is: either some human is not permanently sleeping (negation of the first part) or some human is permanently sleeping (negation of the second part). The commentator then addresses an objection: since a compound proposition consists of two propositions, its contradictory should be the conjunction of the negations of both parts, but the disjunctive concept negates only one part. The answer is that the contradictory of the whole is the negation of the whole, which is achieved by negating just one part because if one part is false, the whole compound is false. The disjunctive concept is not the contradictory itself but a necessary concomitant. Al-Attar refines this by noting that the disjunctive concept is actually the sum of the two contradictories, making it a necessary equivalent. The passage concludes by explaining why exclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluw) arises: the two negations cannot both be true simultaneously, so they entail a disjunction. The commentator urges reflection because the topic is subtle.

Key Terms
النَّقِيض
القضيَّة المركَّبة
المفهوم المردَّد
لازم مساو
مانعة الخلو

What is the contradictory of the proposition 'Every human is sleeping by particular possibility' (كل إنسان نائم بالإمكان الخاص)?
A) Either some human is not sleeping by necessity, or some human is sleeping by necessity.
B) Some human is sleeping not by necessity.
C) No human is sleeping by particular possibility.
D) Every human is sleeping by necessity.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a compound modal proposition is a necessary equivalent disjunctive concept, not the direct negation of both parts.

Three Methods for the Contradictory of Particular Compound Propositions
Dasuqi presents three ways to form the contradictory of a particular compound proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the three methods for forming the contradictory of a particular compound proposition as presented by Dasuqi.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a particular compound is not a simple disjunction — it requires a three-part disjunction or per-subject negation.

In the previous lesson, you learned how to form the contradictory of a universal compound proposition using the disjunctive concept. However, that method does not work for particular compound propositions. Here, Dasuqi presents three alternative methods, each ensuring that the contradictory is a single quantified proposition suitable for logical inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound type
Determine whether the compound proposition is universal or particular. The disjunctive concept suffices for universal compounds like 'every body is always an animal', but not for particular ones like 'some body is always an animal'.
Particular compounds have the form 'some S is P and Q'.
2
Recognize the insufficiency for particulars
Note that the contradictory of a particular compound cannot be expressed as a simple two-part disjunction of the contradictories of its parts. That would leave out the case where some individuals are affirmative and some negative.
Dasuqi states: 'for the particular compound, what we mentioned of the disjunctive concept is not sufficient.'
3
Learn Method 1: Simple negation (raf')
The simplest method is to negate the entire proposition, saying 'it is not so'. This yields a single proposition like 'not (some body is always an animal)'.
This is the first of the three methods mentioned.
4
Learn Method 2: Three-part disjunction
Add a third part to the disjunction. For the example, the contradictory becomes: 'either every body is always an animal, or no body is always an animal, or some body is always an animal and some body is not always an animal'.
The disjunction now has three parts, covering all possibilities.
5
Learn Method 3: Per-subject disjunction
Apply the disjunction between the contradictories of the two parts to each individual of the subject. Formulate as: 'every individual of the subject is not free from the contradictory of the two parts'.
This avoids adding a third part by iterating over individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ
ثمَّ هذا المفهومُ المردَّدُ إنَّما هو نقيضُ المركبة الكلِّيَّةِ، لكن في المركَّبة الجزئيَّةِ لا يكفي في نقيضها ما ذكرنا من المفهوم المردَّدِ، بل الحقُّ في نقيضها رفْعُهُ بأنَّهُ ليس كذلك. وهذا أحد طرق ثلاثة؛ ثانيها: أن يُؤخذ المفهوم المردَّدُ على أصلِهِ منفصلةً، ولكن يُضم إليها جزء آخَرُ فَيُقالُ في المثالِ الآتي دائماً : إمَّا كُلُّ جسم حيوان دائماً، وإمَّا لا شيء من الجسم بحيوان دائماً، وإمَّا بعضُ الجسم حيوان دائماً، وبعضُ الجسم ليس بحيوانٍ دائماً، فتكونُ المنفصلةُ مُركَّبَةٌ مِنَ أجزاء ثلاثة. ثالثها: أن يؤخذ المفهوم المردَّدُ كذلك، ولكن أن يردد بين نقيضي الجزأين بالنسبة إلى كل فرد من أفراد الموضوع، فيقال في نقيضها : كل فرد من أفراد الموضوع، لا يخلو عن نقيض الجزأين.
Section on Contradiction. Then this disjunctive concept is only the contradictory of the universal compound. But for the particular compound, what we mentioned of the disjunctive concept is not sufficient. Rather, the truth for its contradictory is to negate it by saying 'it is not so'. And this is one of three methods. The second method: that the disjunctive concept is taken as it is, as a disjunction, but another part is added to it, so one says in the following example always: either every body is always an animal, or no body is always an animal, or some body is always an animal and some body is not always an animal; thus the disjunction is composed of three parts. The third method: that the disjunctive concept is taken similarly, but one makes a disjunction between the contradictories of the two parts with respect to every individual of the subject, so one says in its contradictory: every individual of the subject is not free from the contradictory of the two parts.
Source pages p. 677

Commentary
This passage addresses the difficulty of forming the contradictory of a particular compound proposition (e.g., 'some body is always an animal') as opposed to a universal compound. The author first states that the disjunctive concept (i.e., the idea of disjoining the contradictories of the parts) suffices for universal compounds but not for particular ones. For a particular compound, the simple negation of the entire proposition is one valid method (the first of three). The second method constructs a three-part disjunction, adding an extra clause to account for the particularity. For example, instead of a two-part disjunction like 'either every body is always an animal or no body is always an animal', a third part is added: 'or some body is always an animal and some body is not always an animal'. This covers the case where the subject is partly affirmative and partly negative. The third method avoids adding a third part by applying the disjunction to each individual subject separately, stating that for every individual, one of the contradictories holds. This yields a statement like 'every individual of the subject is either always an animal or not always an animal'. The author emphasizes that these methods are designed to produce a single quantified proposition that serves as the proper contradictory in logical operations like conversion and syllogism.

Key Terms
تناقض
مفهوم مردد
مركبة كلية/جزئية

Checkpoint
What is the third method for forming the contradictory of a particular compound proposition?
A) Take the contradictory as a single negated proposition.
B) Take a disjunction of three parts.
C) Apply the disjunction between the contradictories of the two parts to each individual subject.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a particular compound proposition requires either simple negation, a three-part disjunction, or a per-subject disjunction, because a two-part disjunction is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct the contradictory of 'some animals are always rational and laughing' using each of the three methods described by Dasuqi.
?
Given the proposition 'some humans are not always sinful and guilty', determine which method a logician would use to form its contradictory and explain why the simple disjunction fails.

The Contradictory of Universal Compound Propositions
Author defines the contradictory of universal compound propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the correct contradictory of a universal compound proposition by vacillating between the contradictories of its two parts for each individual subject.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a universal compound is a vacillation for each individual, not a simple disjunction.

This lesson unpacks the definition of the contradictory for universal compound propositions such as 'Every A is B or C.' The correct method, as refined by commentators, requires vacillating between the contradictories of the two parts for every individual subject, not applying them to the subject as a whole. We will walk through the definition and its logical grounding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound proposition and its two parts
Take a universal compound like 'Every human is either male or female.' The two parts are 'male' and 'female' (the predicates in the disjunction).
The compound's parts are the disjuncts or the subject and predicate if it is a conditional.
2
Understand the definition: 'vacillation between the two contradictories of the two parts'
The contradictory is formed by saying: For each individual of the subject, the individual is not free from being either not-A or not-B (where A and B are the original predicates). This is a vacillation between the two contradictories for each individual.
Al-Dasooqi clarifies that this vacillation is disjunctive (بدلية) because the two contradictories cannot both hold.
3
Recognize the refinement: the vacillation is on the predicates, not whole propositions
Commentators like al-Isam emphasize that we vacillate between the predicates of the two contradictories of the parts, not between the two full contradictory propositions. This avoids attributing a universal contradictory to each individual.
This resolves the objection that a full proposition cannot be attributed to a single individual.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider the compound proposition from Usul al-Fiqh: 'Every transaction is either permissible or prohibited.' Its contradictory is: 'For every transaction, it is not the case that it is both not-permissible and not-prohibited' (i.e., vacillating between the contradictories of the two parts for each transaction).
This correctly ensures that the original and its contradictory cannot both be true or both false.

Source Text

As for compounds that include non-necessity, its face is that it is possible that the predicate is necessary for some, and its negation is necessary for some other, so the particular non-necessary and the two universal necessary or the permanent and the necessary are obtained, so if it were said with the possibility that the predicate is true for the contradictories of the individuals of the subject by necessity and negated from some by necessity, the explanation would be inclusive of all. End quote.
Source pages p. 678

Commentary
This passage defines the contradictory of compound categorical propositions and addresses a subtle technical point. The author first gives the standard definition: the contradictory of a compound proposition (like 'Every A is B or C') is formed by vacillating between the contradictories of its two parts for every individual subject. Al-Dasooqi clarifies that this vacillation is disjunctive (بدلية) because the two contradictories cannot both be true. He also explains that 'between the two contradictories' refers to the predicates of those contradictories. Al-Attar then elaborates: when forming the contradictory, the subject is restricted by the predicate of the first part, and the process continues until affirmation and negation apply to one thing. He gives an example: the contradictory of 'Every body is always an animal' is 'Either every body is always an animal or nothing that is a body is an animal'. The gloss of al-Isam is then quoted, which argues that a simpler 'vacillated concept' suffices if applied to each individual singly, making it a single categorical proposition similar to a disjunctive. This view is preferred because it requires nullifying only one proposition. The author then responds to an objection: since the two contradictories are two full propositions, vacillating between them for each individual is meaningless. The intended meaning is vacillation between the affirmations and negations of their predicates. The passage then moves to a new section on contradiction (فصل في التناقض). Here, it explains why the mere 'vacillated concept' (مفهوم مردد) is insufficient for the contradictory of a particular compound proposition: both the particular and the vacillated concept could be false together. To demonstrate, the author uses a non-permanent existential proposition (مادة الوجودية اللادائمة). He shows that it is possible for the predicate to be permanently true for some individuals of the subject, leading to the scenario where both are false. Al-Dasooqi adds glosses on specific phrases. Al-Attar then notes that the contradictory must be restricted by the two modalities (جهتي) of the two contradictories to yield a universal proposition. This resolves an error in some glosses that thought the two contradictories are complete propositions; rather, they are the predicates with their modalities. Finally, al-Isam's commentary explains that the example works for non-permanent compounds; for non-necessary compounds, the predicate and its negation may be necessary for different parts, so a more general explanation is needed. The entire passage guards against misconstruing the contradictory of compound propositions as a simple disjunction of two complete propositions, emphasizing the need to consider the subject and modalities.

المفهوم المردد
المركبة الجزئية
الوجودية اللادائمة

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a universal compound proposition is formed by vacillating between the contradictories of its parts for each individual subject, not by a simple disjunction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Immediate Inference

10 min
Why the Simple Contradictory Fails for Particular Compounds
Author explains the insufficiency of the simple vacillated concept for particular compound contradictories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the mere 'vacillated concept' is insufficient for the contradictory of a particular compound proposition and how the modal resolution works.

Core Idea
A particular compound and its simple vacillated contradictory can both be false, necessitating a nuanced modal contradictory.

This lesson addresses a critical refinement in the theory of contradiction. For particular compound propositions, the straightforward vacillated concept (مفهوم مردد) used for universals does not guarantee a proper contradictory because both can be false simultaneously. The passage uses a non-permanent existential example to illustrate this and then presents the correct contradictory as a universal proposition restricted by the two modalities of the contradictories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim: Simple vacillated concept insufficient for particular compounds
The author states that the vacillated concept alone is not sufficient for the contradictory of a particular compound proposition.
This is a departure from the universal case where it works.
2
Understand the reason: Joint falsity is possible
Both the original particular compound and the simple vacillated contradictory can be false together. For example, in a non-permanent existential mode, it is possible that the predicate is permanently true for some individuals, making the particular false (if no individual has it non-permanently?) and the universal negative false (because some individuals have the predicate permanently).
The key is that the simple vacillated contradictory is a universal negative, which can be false while the particular is also false.
3
Analyze the example: Non-permanent existential mode
Take a particular compound like 'Some body is animal at some time' (وجودية غير دائمة). It is possible that no body is animal at any time (so particular false) and also that every body is animal permanently (so universal negative false). Thus both are false.
The example shows the vacillated concept (here, a universal negative) does not properly contradict.
4
Learn the resolution: Universal proposition with two modalities
The correct contradictory must be a universal proposition that attributes to each individual either the negation of the first part with its modality or the negation of the second part with its modality. Al-Attar explains that this yields a single universal proposition that truly contradicts the particular.
This resolves the joint falsity problem by ensuring that for each individual, exactly one of the modalized negations holds.

Source Text
