أن يردد بين نقيضي الجزأين (بالنسبة إلى كل فرد) من أفراد الموضوع، فيقال في نقيضها: كل فرد من أفراد الموضوع، لا يخلو عن نقيض الجزأين. قال الدسوقي: (قوله: أن يردد بين ... إلخ) أي: على البدلية؛ لأنهما لا يجتمعان. (قوله: بين نقيضي ... إلخ) أي: بين محمولي نقيضي الجزئين. (قوله: بالنسبة) متعلق بيردد. (قوله: فيقال) مفرع على قوله: (أن يردد ... إلخ). (قوله: لا يخلو) أي: لا يخلو عن واحد من محمولي نقيضي الجزأين. قال العطار: المركبات أخذ نقيض جميع في يقيّد موضوع عجز الجزئية المركبة بمحمول صدرها، ثم إذا أخذ النقيض لجزئها؛ يصنع كذلك حتى يرد الإيجاب والسلب على شيء واحد، فيقال في المثال المذكور دائما: إما كل: إما كل جسم حيوان دائما ولا شيء من الجسم الذي هو حيوان يحيوان دائما.اهـ. وفي حاشية العصام على القطب: أنه يكفي المفهوم المردد بين نقيض الجزأين لكل واحد واحد قال: ولو تأملت؛ استغنيت عن بيانه، فلو اعتبر في الجميع كذلك؛ لكان أقرب إلى الضبط، وكان استعماله الخلوّ أسهل؛ لأنه لا يحتاج حينئذ إلا إلى إبطال قضية واحدة، بخلاف ما إذا جعلت منفصلة، فإنه يوجب الحاجة إلى إبطال قضيتين.اهـ. يريد أن المفهوم المردد بالنسبة إلى كل واحد واحد؛ يكون من قبيل الحملية الشبيهة بالمنفصلة، قضية واحدة، هذا معنى قوله: لأنه لا يحتاج ... إلخ. (قوله: أن يردد بين نقيضي الجزأين لا يخفى أن نقيضي الجزأين قضيتان، ولا معنى للترديد بينهما لكل واحد واحد، إذ القضية لا تثبت لشيء، فالمراد أن يردد بين نقيضي محموليهما بمعنى السلب بأن يردد كل واحد بين ثبوت المحمول وسلبه وهي (قوله: من محمولي ... إلخ) قدر هذا المضاف وهو (محمولي) لتصحيح عبارة الشارح، فاندفع ما أورد عليها من لزوم حمل أحد النقيضين الكليين على كل فرد، وهو باطل كما يوضحه المقال الآتي، ويأتي للمحشي التنبيه عليه صراحة. فصل: في التناقض وإنما لم يكف المفهوم المردد في نقيض المركبة الجزئية؛ لجواز كذب الجزئية، والمفهوم المردد معا. فلنبيته في مادة الوجودية اللادائمة؛ ليقاس سائر القضايا عليها، فنقول: من الجائز أن يكون المحمول ثابتا دائما لبعض أفراد الموضوع، قال الدسوقي: (قوله: لجواز) علة لقوله: (وإنما لا يكفي) (قوله: معا) أي: يكذبا معا. (قوله: فلنبيته) أي: وإن أردت بيان كذبهما معا؛ فلبينه... إلخ. (قوله: سائر) أي: باقي. (قوله: المحمول كالحيوان في مثال الآتي. (قوله: لبعض أفراد ... إلخ) أي: كالفرس. (قوله: الموضوع) أي: كالجسم. قال العطار: و مقيدا بجهتي نقيض الجزأين، فتحصل قضية كلية ينسب محمولها إلى كل واحد واحد من أفراد موضوعها إيجابا أو سلبا بجهتي نقيضي الجزأين؛ أفاد في شرح المطالع، وبه تعلم أن ما في بعض الحواشي هنا، حيث قال عند قول الشارح: لا يخلو عن نقيضي الجزأين؛ فيه نظر بين؛ لأن نقيضي الجزئين؛ قضيتان ذاتا كم وكيف وجهة، وليس كل فرد يردد فيه بين أن يثبت له القضية الأولى بتمامها أو القضية الثانية بتمامها.اهـ. سلخ ونسخ لما في شرح المطالع. (قوله: من الجائز أن يكون المحمول ثابتا ... إلخ) قال العصام: هذا في المركبات من اللادوام، وأما المركبات المشتملة على اللاضرورة؛ فوجهه أنه يجوز أن يكون المحمول ضروريا لبعض، وسلبه ضروريا لبعض آخر، فتكون الجزئية اللاضرورية والكليتان الضروريتان أو الدائمة والضرورية، فلو قيل بجواز أن يكون المحمول ثابتا لنقيض أفراد الموضوع بالضرورة ومسلوبا عن البعض بالضرورة؛ لكان البيان شاملا للجميع.اهـ.
That it be vacillated between the two contradictories of the two parts (with respect to every individual) of the individuals of the subject, so it is said in its contradictory: Every individual of the individuals of the subject is not free from the two contradictories of the two parts. Al-Dasooqi said: (His saying: 'that it be vacillated between... etc.') i.e., on the basis of alternation; because they do not both occur together. (His saying: 'between the two contradictories of... etc.') i.e., between the two predicates of the two contradictories of the two parts. (His saying: 'with respect to') is attached to 'be vacillated'. (His saying: 'so it is said') is a subordination to his saying: 'that it be vacillated... etc.'. (His saying: 'is not free') i.e., is not free from one of the two predicates of the two contradictories of the two parts. Al-Attar said: For compound [propositions], taking the contradictory of all is restricted by the subject of the deficient particular compound by the predicate of its first part, then when the contradictory is taken for its part, one does likewise until affirmation and negation fall on one thing, so it is said in the mentioned example always: Either every: Either every body is always an animal and nothing of the body that is an animal is always an animal. End quote. And in the gloss of al-Isam on al-Qutb: That the vacillated concept between the two contradictories of the two parts suffices for each single one. He said: And if you reflect, you will dispense with its explanation, so if it were considered in all similarly, it would be closer to the rule, and its use of 'free' would be easier; because then one does not need except to nullify one proposition, unlike if it is made a disjunction, it necessitates the need to nullify two propositions. End quote. He means that the vacillated concept with respect to each single one is of the type of the categorical similar to the disjunctive, one proposition, this is the meaning of his saying: 'because one does not need... etc.' (His saying: 'that it be vacillated between the two contradictories of the two parts' it is not hidden that the two contradictories of the two parts are two propositions, and there is no meaning for vacillation between them for each single one, since the proposition is not established for a thing, so the intended meaning is that it be vacillated between the two contradictories of their two predicates in the sense of negation, so that each one is vacillated between the affirmation of the predicate and its negation. And it is (his saying: 'from the two predicates of... etc.') he estimated this annexed [noun] which is 'two predicates' to correct the wording of the commentator, so what was objected to it of the necessity of attributing one of the two universal contradictories to every individual is repelled, and it is false as the following discussion clarifies, and the glossator will explicitly alert to it. Section: On Contradiction. And the vacillated concept alone is not sufficient for the contradictory of the particular compound; because of the possibility of falsity of the particular and the vacillated concept together. So we shall explain it in the matter of the non-permanent existential, so that other propositions may be measured against it. We say: It is possible that the predicate is permanently true for some individuals of the subject. Al-Dasooqi said: (His saying: 'because of the possibility') is a reason for his saying: ('And it is not sufficient') (His saying: 'together') i.e., they both become false together. (His saying: 'so we shall explain it') i.e., and if you want to explain their falsity together, so we explain it... etc. (His saying: 'other') i.e., the remaining. (His saying: 'the predicate like 'animal' in its coming example. (His saying: 'for some individuals... etc.') i.e., like the horse. (His saying: 'the subject') i.e., like body. Al-Attar said: And restricted by the two modalities of the two contradictories of the two parts, so a universal proposition results, its predicate is attributed to each single individual of its subjects with affirmation or negation with the two modalities of the two contradictories of the two parts; he informed in Sharh al-Matali', and by it you learn that what is in some glosses here, where he said at the commentator's saying: 'is not free from the two contradictories of the two parts' is an evident oversight; because the two contradictories of the two parts are two propositions in essence, quantity, quality and modality, and not every individual is vacillated in whether to affirm for it the first proposition in its entirety or the second proposition in its entirety. End quote. It is plagiarism and copying from Sharh al-Matali'. (His saying: 'It is possible that the predicate is true... etc.') Al-Isam said: This is about compounds from non-permanent [propositions]. As for compounds that include non-necessity, its face is that it is possible that the predicate is necessary for some, and its negation is necessary for some other, so the particular non-necessary and the two universal necessary or the permanent and the necessary are obtained, so if it were said with the possibility that the predicate is true for the contradictories of the individuals of the subject by necessity and negated from some by necessity, the explanation would be inclusive of all. End quote.
Source pages p. 678

Commentary
This passage defines the contradictory of compound categorical propositions and addresses a subtle technical point. The author first gives the standard definition: the contradictory of a compound proposition (like 'Every A is B or C') is formed by vacillating between the contradictories of its two parts for every individual subject. Al-Dasooqi clarifies that this vacillation is disjunctive (بدلية) because the two contradictories cannot both be true. He also explains that 'between the two contradictories' refers to the predicates of those contradictories. Al-Attar then elaborates: when forming the contradictory, the subject is restricted by the predicate of the first part, and the process continues until affirmation and negation apply to one thing. He gives an example: the contradictory of 'Every body is always an animal' is 'Either every body is always an animal or nothing that is a body is an animal'. The gloss of al-Isam is then quoted, which argues that a simpler 'vacillated concept' suffices if applied to each individual singly, making it a single categorical proposition similar to a disjunctive. This view is preferred because it requires nullifying only one proposition. The author then responds to an objection: since the two contradictories are two full propositions, vacillating between them for each individual is meaningless. The intended meaning is vacillation between the affirmations and negations of their predicates. The passage then moves to a new section on contradiction (فصل في التناقض). Here, it explains why the mere 'vacillated concept' (مفهوم مردد) is insufficient for the contradictory of a particular compound proposition: both the particular and the vacillated concept could be false together. To demonstrate, the author uses a non-permanent existential proposition (مادة الوجودية اللادائمة). He shows that it is possible for the predicate to be permanently true for some individuals of the subject, leading to the scenario where both are false. Al-Dasooqi adds glosses on specific phrases. Al-Attar then notes that the contradictory must be restricted by the two modalities (جهتي) of the two contradictories to yield a universal proposition. This resolves an error in some glosses that thought the two contradictories are complete propositions; rather, they are the predicates with their modalities. Finally, al-Isam's commentary explains that the example works for non-permanent compounds; for non-necessary compounds, the predicate and its negation may be necessary for different parts, so a more general explanation is needed. The entire passage guards against misconstruing the contradictory of compound propositions as a simple disjunction of two complete propositions, emphasizing the need to consider the subject and modalities.

نقيض
الجزأين
المفهوم المردد
المركبة الجزئية
الوجودية اللادائمة

C) Because the vacillated concept is always true.

Key Takeaway
For particular compounds, the contradictory cannot be a simple universal negation; it must be a universal proposition incorporating the two modalities of the contradictories.

Understanding the Falsity of the Non-Permanent Particular
Commentary explains why the non-permanent particular proposition is false when predicate is always present in some and always absent in others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of a non-permanent particular proposition in a given matter and explain why it is false when the predicate is always present in some individuals and always absent in others.

Core Idea
A non-permanent particular proposition is false if no individual subject ever alternates in possessing the predicate.

In this lesson, we explore a key insight from the commentary on Tadhheeb: the non-permanent particular proposition (e.g., 'Some body is animal not always') is false when the predicate is always present in some members of the subject and always absent in others. This scenario demonstrates the importance of temporal modality in judging propositional truth. We'll learn to analyze such propositions step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matter (māddah)
Determine the relationship between subject and predicate: Is the predicate always present in some individuals of the subject and always absent in others? In our example, 'animal' is always present in humans and always absent in stones.
2
Understand the meaning of non-permanent particular
The non-permanent particular means there is at least one individual of the subject that has the predicate at one time and lacks it at another. It requires a single subject that alternates.
The same individual must both have and lack the predicate at different times.
3
Check for an alternating individual
In the given matter, does any individual body ever change from being animal to not being animal? If not, no individual fulfills the condition.
Humans always have animality; stones never have it.
4
Conclude falsity
Since no individual alternates, the non-permanent particular is false. The compound proposition 'Some body is animal not always' is false as a whole.
Note that decomposing into 'Some body is animal' and 'Some body is not animal' yields true simple propositions because the subject reference changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسلوباً دائماً عن بعض الأفرادِ الأُخرِ كالحيوان مثلاً، فإنَّه ثابت دائماً، لبعض أفرادِ الجسم مسلوب دائماً عن بعض آخر . ففي هذه المادة تكذبُ الجزئيَّةُ اللادائمة والمفهوم المردَّدُ معاً . أمَّا كَذِبُ الجزئيَّةِ اللادائمةِ ؛ أي: كَقولنا : «بعضُ الجسم حيوان لا دائماً»؛ فَلِأنَّ مفهوم الجزئيّة اللادائمة، هو أن يكون بعض أفراد الموضوع، بحيث يثبت له المحمول تارةً، ويُسلَبُ عنه أُخرى، ولا شيء من أفراد الموضوع في المادَّة المفروضة كذلك؛ أي: ليس شيءٌ من أفراد الجسم بحيثُ يثبت له الحيوان تارةً، ويُسلَبُ عنه أُخرى؛ فتكذب الجزئيَّةُ اللادائمة. (قَوْلُهُ : وَيُسْلَبُ عَنْهُ أُخْرَى) فيكونُ الموضوعُ مُتَحِداً في الجزئيَّةِ، فَلِهذا كذبَتْ، فإنْ تحلَّلَتْ إلى قضيَّتَيْن ؛ كانَتْ هاتَانِ القضيَّتَانِ صَادقتَين؛ لأنَّه بزوالِ التَّركيب يتعدَّدُ الموضوع، ويصير موضوع هذهِ غير موضوع تلكَ، فَبعض الجسم حيوان لا دائماً ؛ كاذبةٌ ؛ لأنَّ معناها : البعضُ الذَّي نُسب له الحيوانيَّةُ بالفعل سُلِبَتْ عنه بالفعل أيضاً، وليسَ شيءٌ مِنَ الأفرادِ تثبتُ لهُ الحيوانية وتُسلَبُ عنه، وأمَّا بعضُ الجسم حيوان بالفعل، بعضُ الجسم ليس بحيوان بالفعل، إذا اعتُبِرَتْ كُلُّ واحدةٍ منهما على حِدَتِهَا ؛ كانَتْ صادقةً لاختلافِ موضوعَيْهما ؛ إذ البعضُ المحكوم عليه بالحيوانية غير المحكوم عليه بسلبها
Always negated from some other individuals, like 'animal' for example, for it is always established for some individuals of body, always negated from some others. So in this matter, the non-permanent particular and the equivocal concept are both false. As for the falsity of the non-permanent particular, i.e., our statement: 'Some body is animal not always' - because the concept of the non-permanent particular is that some individuals of the subject are such that the predicate is affirmed for it at one time and negated from it at another, and nothing from the individuals of the subject in the supposed matter is like that; i.e., no individual of body is such that animal is affirmed for it at one time and negated from it at another; so the non-permanent particular is false. (His statement: 'and negated from it at another') so the subject is unified in the particular, thus it is false. If it is analyzed into two propositions, those two propositions would be true, because with the removal of composition the subject becomes multiple, and the subject of this becomes other than the subject of that. So 'some body is animal not always' is false; because its meaning is: the individual to which animality was actually attributed is also actually negated from it, and there is no individual for which animality is affirmed and negated. As for 'some body is animal in actuality, some body is not animal in actuality', if each one of them is considered on its own, they would be true due to the difference of their two subjects; for the individual judged with animality is other than the individual judged with its negation.
Source pages p. 680

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary on Tadhheeb, explains the falsity of the non-permanent particular proposition (al-juz'iyya al-lā dā'ima) in a specific material (māddah). The material in question is one where the predicate is always present in some individuals of the subject and always absent in others. The example given is 'animal' predicated of 'body': animal is always present in some bodies (e.g., humans) and always absent in others (e.g., stones). In such a case, the proposition 'Some body is animal not always' is false. Why? Because the non-permanent particular means that there is some individual of the subject that has the predicate at one time and lacks it at another. But in this material, no individual body ever changes with respect to animality: if it is a human, it always has animality; if it is a stone, it never has it. Thus, no individual fits the description of having animality at one time and not at another. Hence the proposition is false. The commentary further clarifies that if we break the compound proposition into two separate simple propositions – 'Some body is animal (actually)' and 'Some body is not animal (actually)' – then each is true. The reason is that the subject of the first ('some body') is not the same as the subject of the second in actual reference: the 'some body' that is animal refers to a human; the 'some body' that is not animal refers to a stone. So when considered independently, each is true because the predicate holds (or not) for that specific part. But the original compound proposition ('some body is animal not always') requires the same individual to both have and lack animality, which never occurs. This is a subtle point about composition and the unity of the subject in modal propositions.

Key Terms
الجزئية اللادائمة
المفهوم المردد
المادة

B) Because no single individual body ever changes with respect to animality.
C) Because the predicate 'animal' is not a universal.
D) Because the subject 'body' is not a genus.

Key Takeaway
A non-permanent particular proposition is true only if there exists at least one individual of the subject that alternates in possessing the predicate; if the predicate is always present in some and always absent in others, the proposition is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Understanding Contradiction of the Non-Perpetual Particular
Author explains the falsity of non-perpetual particular and its universal contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of a non-perpetual particular proposition and its universal contradictories based on the predicate's permanence in the subject.

Core Idea
A non-perpetual particular is false if the predicate is essential and perpetual.

In this lesson, we explore a key modal logical concept: the non-perpetual particular. The author explains why such a proposition cannot be true when the predicate is a permanent attribute of the subject. We'll also see why its universal contradictories (the 'composite concept') are also false. This analysis sharpens our understanding of contradiction in modal logic, which is essential for precise reasoning in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Recognize the non-perpetual particular: it asserts existence of the predicate for some subject at some time, not always. The example is 'some body is an animal actually, not perpetually'.
This is a particular affirmative with temporal modality (non-perpetual).
2
Check the predicate's modal status
Determine if the predicate is a permanent (perpetual) or temporary attribute of the subject. Here, 'animal' is an essential accident of body: if a body is an animal, it is always an animal; if not, it never is.
Permanent attributes do not change over time for a given individual.
3
Evaluate the particular's truth value
Because animality is permanent, no individual body can have it at one time and lack it at another. Therefore, the non-perpetual particular is false.
The proposition claims temporal variation, but none exists.
4
Analyze the universal contradictories
The composite concept (mafhūm muraddad) consists of the universal affirmative 'Every body is an animal perpetually' and the universal negative 'No body is an animal perpetually'. Both are false: the affirmative because some bodies (stones) are never animals; the negative because some bodies (humans) are always animals.
The universal propositions are too strong; they fail due to counterexamples.
5
Draw the logical lesson
This shows that when the predicate is permanent, the non-perpetual particular is false, and its universal contradictories are also false. This is a special modal case where the standard contradiction relations do not hold symmetrically.
Contradictories must have opposite truth values; here both are false, so they are not true contradictories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي التَّنَاقُضِ
الموضوع، بحيث يثبت له المحمول تارةً، ويُسلَبُ عنه أُخرى، ولا شيء من أفراد الموضوع في المادَّة المفروضة كذلك؛ أي: ليس شيءٌ من أفراد الجسم بحيثُ يثبت له الحيوان تارةً، ويُسلَبُ عنه أُخرى؛ فتكذب الجزئيَّةُ اللادائمة.
(قَوْلُهُ: وَيُسْلَبُ) أي: ينتفي. (قَوْلُهُ: كَذَلِكَ) أي: يثبتُ لهُ المحمول تارةٌ، وينتفي عنهُ أُخرى.
بعضُ الجسم حيوان بالفعل لا دائماً؛ لأنَّ هذا مثال للوجوديَّةِ اللادائمةِ وَجِهَتِهَا بالفعل.
وأمَّا كذبُ المفهوم المردَّدِ؛ فلِكَذِبِ الموجبة والسالبة الكلِّيَّتَينِ اللَّتَينِ تركَّبَ المفهوم المردَّدُ منهما.
أمَّا كذب الموجبة الكلية؛ أي: كقولنا: «كل جسم حيوان دائماً»؛ فلأنَّ المحمول مسلوب دائماً عن بعض أفراد الجسم، فكيف يكون ثابتاً لجميعها؟!.
وأمَّا كذبُ السَّالبة الكلِّيَّة؛ أي: كقولنا: «لا شيء من الجسم بحيوان دائماً»؛ فلأنَّ المحمول ثابت دائماً لبعض أفراد الجسم، فكيف يكونُ مسلوباً دائماً عن جميعها؟!.
Section: On Contradiction. The subject, such that the predicate is affirmed of it at one time and negated of it at another, and no individual of the subject in the supposed matter is like that; i.e., no individual of body is such that animal is affirmed of it at one time and negated of it at another; thus the non-perpetual particular is false. (His saying: 'and is negated') i.e., is denied. (His saying: 'like that') i.e., the predicate is affirmed of it at one time and denied of it at another. Some body is an animal actually, not perpetually; because this is an example of the non-perpetual existential whose modality is actual. As for the falsity of the composite concept, it is due to the falsity of the universal affirmative and universal negative from which the composite concept is composed. As for the falsity of the universal affirmative, i.e., our saying: 'Every body is an animal perpetually'; because the predicate is perpetually negated of some individuals of body, so how can it be affirmed of all of them?! As for the falsity of the universal negative, i.e., our saying: 'No body is an animal perpetually'; because the predicate is perpetually affirmed of some individuals of body, so how can it be perpetually negated of all of them?!.
Source pages p. 681

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of contradiction for a non-perpetual particular proposition and explains its falsity. The author begins by describing the condition for a contradictory pair: the subject must be such that the predicate is sometimes affirmed and sometimes negated of it. For the example 'some body is an animal non-perpetually', no individual body fits this description because animality is a permanent essential attribute – it cannot be true of a body at one time and false at another. Therefore, the particular proposition is false. The commentator al-ʿAṭṭār clarifies that this particular is existential (wujūdiyyah) with actual modality, meaning it asserts actual existence at some time. Next, the author explains why the 'composite concept' – meaning the contradictory pair of universal propositions that would be the contradictories of the non-perpetual particular – is false. The composite concept consists of the universal affirmative 'Every body is an animal perpetually' and the universal negative 'No body is an animal perpetually'. Both are false because the affirmative is contradicted by the fact that some bodies (like stones) are never animals, and the negative is contradicted by the fact that some bodies (like humans) are always animals. Thus, the non-perpetual particular is false because it cannot be true, and its universal contradictories are also false because they are too strong. This analysis guards against confusing a genuine contradiction with a mere opposite, and illustrates that a proposition's modality affects its contradictory relationships. In Islamic logic, this is crucial for evaluating arguments in jurisprudence (fiqh) and theology (kalam), where premises must be carefully checked for modal consistency.

الجزئية اللادائمة
الدائمة
المفهوم المردد

In modal logic, a non-perpetual particular is false if the predicate is a permanent attribute of the subject, and its universal contradictories are also false because they are too strong.

Truth Conditions of the Disjunctive Concept and Its Inadequacy for Negation
Author defines truth conditions and correct negation for composite particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of a disjunctive concept and construct the correct negation of a composite particular proposition.

Core Idea
A simple disjunctive concept cannot negate a composite particular; correct negation distributes over each individual.

In this passage, the author defines the disjunctive concept (al-mutlaq al-murdad) and shows why it is insufficient as the negation of a composite particular proposition (al-murakkaba al-juz'iyya). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly handling modal propositions in Islamic logic, especially when dealing with temporal modifiers like 'always' (da'iman). We will walk through the truth conditions and derive the correct negation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive concept
Recognize that the disjunctive concept is a compound proposition composed of a universal affirmative and a universal negative joined by 'or'.
Example: 'Either every body is always an animal or no body is always an animal.'
2
Determine its truth condition
The disjunctive concept is true when at least one of its parts is true, and false only when both parts are false.
This is the standard truth condition for disjunction.
3
Apply the condition to the given example
Show why both the universal affirmative ('Every body is always an animal') and the universal negative ('No body is always an animal') are false: some bodies (stones) are never animals, and some bodies (humans) are always animals.
Thus the disjunctive concept is false.
4
Understand the composite particular
The composite particular 'Some body is always an animal' is true because some bodies (humans) are always animals.
The word 'always' adds a temporal modality.
5
Realize the inadequacy of the simple disjunctive
Since the disjunctive concept is false while the composite particular is true, it cannot serve as its negation (which must be true when the original is false).
The simple disjunctive does not contradict the particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا كذبُ المفهوم المردَّدِ ؛ فلِكَذِبِ الموجبة والسالبة الكلِّيَّتَينِ اللَّتَينِ تركَّبَ المفهوم المردَّدُ منهما .
أمَّا كذب الموجبة الكلية؛ أي: كقولنا : «كل جسم حيوان دائماً»؛ فلأنَّ المحمول مسلوب دائماً عن بعض أفراد الجسم، فكيف يكون ثابتاً لجميعها ؟! .
وأمَّا كذبُ السَّالبة الكلِّيَّة ؛ أي : كَقولنا : « لا شيء من الجسم بحيوان دائماً»؛ فلأنَّ المحمول ثابت دائماً لبعض أفراد الجسم، فكيف يكونُ مسلوباً دائماً عن جميعها ؟! .
فصل: في التناقض
وإذا كذبَتِ الموجبة والسالبة الكلِّيَّتان ؛ كذب المفهوم المردَّدُ لَا محالة؛ لأنَّه مركَّب منهما
فتبيَّنَ أنَّ المفهوم المردَّدَ لا يكفي في نقيض المركبة الجزئية، بل الحق في نقيضها أن يردَّدَ بين نقيضي الجزأين لكلّ واحدٍ واحدٍ من أفرادِ الموضوع، فيقال في المادَّة المذكورة: «كل فرد من أفراد الجسم إمَّا حيوان دائماً، أو ليس بحيوان دائماً».
وهذا نقيضُ المركَّبةِ الجزئية؛ أي قولنا : «بعضُ الجسم حيوان لا دائماً»
As for the falsity of the disjunctive concept: it is due to the falsity of the universal affirmative and the universal negative from which the disjunctive concept is composed. As for the falsity of the universal affirmative, i.e. our saying: 'Every body is always an animal' — because the predicate is always negated of some individuals of body, so how can it be affirmed of all of them?! As for the falsity of the universal negative, i.e. our saying: 'No body is always an animal' — because the predicate is always affirmed of some individuals of body, so how can it be always negated of all of them?! Chapter on Contradiction. And when the universal affirmative and universal negative are false, the disjunctive concept is necessarily false, because it is composed of them. Then it becomes clear that the disjunctive concept is not sufficient for the negation of the composite particular. Rather, the truth regarding its negation is that it be disjoined between the negations of the two parts for each single individual of the subject. So it is said in the mentioned matter: 'Every individual of body is either always an animal or not always an animal.' And this is the negation of the composite particular, i.e. our saying: 'Some body is always an animal.'
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage defines the truth conditions of the disjunctive concept (al-mutlaq al-murdad) and explains why it fails as the negation of a composite particular proposition (al-murakkaba al-juz'iyya). The author begins by stating that the disjunctive concept is false when both its constituent universal propositions—affirmative and negative—are false. He illustrates with the example of 'body' as subject and 'animal' as predicate under the modality 'always' (da'iman). The universal affirmative 'Every body is always an animal' is false because some bodies (like stones) are never animals. The universal negative 'No body is always an animal' is false because some bodies (like humans) are always animals. Thus both are false, making the disjunctive 'Either every body is always an animal or no body is always an animal' false as well. Then the author turns to the negation (naqid) of a composite particular proposition, such as 'Some body is always an animal' (which is true because humans are bodies). The simple disjunctive concept is inadequate because it does not capture the distribution per individual. The correct negation must be a disjunction for each individual: 'Every individual of body is either always an animal or not always an animal.' This proposition is true for each body (since for any body, it is either always an animal or not), and it directly contradicts the original composite particular. This analysis highlights the careful logical treatment of temporal modalities in the tradition.

المركبة الجزئية
نقيض

C) 'Some body is not always an animal'

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive concept is false when both universal components are false, and it cannot serve as the negation of a composite particular; the correct negation distributes the disjunction over each individual.

Definition of Simple Conversion
Author defines conversion and clarifies the nature of term exchange.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define simple conversion and identify its key characteristic: exchanging the two terms in wording, not in meaning.

Core Idea
Conversion swaps subject and predicate, but only in wording — the meaning stays anchored to the same concepts.

In this passage, the author defines conversion (al-'aks) as the exchange of the two terms of a proposition. But as the commentator Dasuqi clarifies, this exchange is only in wording, not in meaning. Understanding this nuance is crucial because conversion is a tool used in Islamic sciences to transform propositions without changing their truth value.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence where conversion is defined. It says 'exchanging the two terms of a proposition'.
The verbal noun sense (المعنى المصدري) indicates the operation itself.
2
Distinguish verbal from real exchange
Note Dasuqi's clarification: the exchange is in wording (لفظ) not in meaning (مراد). In a categorical proposition, before conversion subject refers to individuals, predicate to intension; after conversion they reverse.
This is specific to categorical (حملیة) propositions.
3
Apply to conditional propositions
The passage also mentions conditional propositions: the antecedent (necessary condition) becomes the consequent after conversion.
In conditional conversion, the roles of ملزوم and لازم are swapped.
4
Summarize the definition
Simple conversion is the operation of swapping subject and predicate while keeping the same terms (no negation) and preserving truth through a verbal exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي
والعكسُ يُطلَقُ على المعنى المصدري ؛ أي : تبديل طرفَي القضية .
الدسوقي
(قَوْلُهُ: الْمُسْتَوِي :) أي: خرج عكسُ النَّقيض المخالف، وعكس النقيض الموافق ، فَالعكوس ثلاثة، والأوَّلُ هو الذي ينصرفُ لهُ اللَّفْظُ عند الإطلاق. (قَوْلُهُ : يُطْلَقُ عَلَى الْمَعْنَى الْمَصْدَرِي) أي : حقيقة .
(قَوْلُهُ : تَبْدِيلُ ... إلخ) المراد بتبديل الطَّرفين : التَّبديل في اللفظ لا في المراد ؛ لأنَّ الموضوع يُراد منه الأفراد قبل العكس، والمفهوم في العكس، والمحمولُ يُرادُ منه قبل العكس المفهوم، وفي العكس الأفراد، وهذا في الحملية.
وأمَّا في المتّصلة ؛ فالمقدَّمُ قبل العكس ملزومٌ ، وفي العكس لازم .
المطار
فَصْلٌ: في العكس المستوي
الظَّاهِرُ أَنَّهُ يُقالُ بالاشتراك على مَعنيين، ويخصُّ بالتَّقييد بالمستوي والإضافة إلى النّقيض، وإنّما وُصِفَ بِالمستوي ؛ لأنَّهُ طريقٌ مُستوِ لَا أَمتَ فِيهِ وَلَا اعوجاج؛ بخلافِ عكس النقيض، فإنَّهُ ليس طريقاً واضحاً .ا.هـ. عصام؛ أي: لعدم استعماله في العلوم والإنتاجاتِ لِمَا قالُوا مِنْ أنَّ الإنتاج بواسطة عكس نقيض القضية لا يُسمَّى قياساً ؛ بخلاف الإنتاج بالعكس المستوي، فإِنَّهُ مُعتبر في العلوم، وذلكَ لرعاية أطراف القضيَّة فيه، حيثُ أُخِذَ عينُ أطرافها ولمْ يُؤخذ نَقيضُها، وأَمَّا عكس النَّقيض فإِنَّهُ يؤخذُ فِيهِ نَقيضُ طَرفَي القضيَّةِ أو نقيضُ أحدهما، وفي عبد الحكيم: أنَّ لفظ العكس ليسَ مُشتركاً لفظيًا بين العكس المستوي وعكس النَّقيض؛ إذْ لَا دليل على وضعِهِ لِلمعنيين ؛ بل بعد تخصيص العكس اللغوي بالصفة وبالإضافة؛ استعمِلَ كُلٌّ مِنَ المقيَّدَيْنِ في المعنى الاصطلاحي.
Chapter: On Simple Conversion. Conversion is used in the verbal noun sense, i.e., exchanging the two terms of a proposition. Dasuqi: (His saying: 'al-mustawi') i.e., excluding contraposition of the opposite and contraposition of the equivalent; thus conversions are three, and the first is what the word refers to when unqualified. (His saying: 'It is used in the verbal noun sense') i.e., real meaning. (His saying: 'Exchanging... etc.') What is meant by exchanging the two terms is exchange in wording not in meaning; because the subject is intended for individuals before conversion, and for the intension after conversion, and the predicate is intended for the intension before conversion, and for the individuals after conversion, and this is in categorical propositions. As for conditional ones, the antecedent before conversion is a necessary condition, and after conversion it is a necessary consequent. al-Matar: Chapter: On Simple Conversion. It is apparent that it is said by homonymy for two meanings, and it is specified by the qualification 'simple' and the addition to 'contrapositive'. And it is described as 'simple' because it is a straight path with no curvature or crookedness, unlike contraposition, for it is not a clear path. End of 'Isam; i.e., because it is not used in the sciences and productions for what they said: that production by means of contraposition of a proposition is not called a syllogism, unlike production by simple conversion, for it is considered in the sciences, and that is because of consideration of the terms of the proposition in it, where the exact terms are taken and their negations are not taken. As for contraposition, the negation of the two terms or the negation of one of them is taken in it. And from Abd al-Hakim: that the word 'conversion' is not homonymous between simple conversion and contraposition; since there is no evidence for its being coined for two meanings; rather, after specifying the linguistic conversion by the attribute and by addition, each of the two qualified terms is used for the technical meaning.
Source pages p. 686

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory text on the logical operation of conversion (al-'aks). It begins with the chapter heading 'On Simple Conversion', then gives a compact definition: 'Conversion, in its verbal-noun sense, is the exchange of the two terms of a proposition.' This is the standard definition. The commentator Dasuqi then expands on the text: the term 'simple' (mustawi) here excludes two other types of conversion: 'contraposition of the opposite' and 'contraposition of the equivalent' (both involve negation). Thus 'aks mustawi is the first of three types of conversion, and the unqualified term 'conversion' normally refers to simple conversion. Dasuqi clarifies that 'exchanging the two terms' is a verbal exchange only, not a change in meaning. In categorical propositions, before conversion the subject refers to individuals (extension) and the predicate refers to the intension (meaning), but after conversion they reverse roles. In conditional propositions, the antecedent and consequent exchange roles: the antecedent which was a necessary condition (lazim) becomes the consequence (malzum). Then another commentator, al-Matar (possibly a misreading), repeats the chapter heading and adds that the term 'simple conversion' is used homonymously for two meanings: simple conversion and contraposition, but it is specified by the qualifier 'simple' or the addition 'to the contrapositive'. The term 'simple' is used because it is a straight path (mustawin) with no curvature, unlike contraposition which is not a clear path. Isam's comment explains that contraposition is not used in the sciences because it does not produce a syllogism properly; only simple conversion is used because it keeps the exact terms without negation. Finally, 'Abd al-Hakim argues that the word 'conversion' is not genuinely homonymous between simple conversion and contraposition; rather, after specifying the linguistic word with the adjectives 'simple' and 'contrapositive', each becomes a distinct technical term. The passage thus defines the technical term 'simple conversion', distinguishes it from contraposition, and explains its importance in scientific reasoning.

العكس المستوي
تبديل الطرفين

Simple conversion swaps the subject and predicate in wording only, preserving the original terms without negation; this operation is foundational for valid immediate inference in Islamic logic.

Simple Conversion vs Contraposition
Author distinguishes types of conversion and discusses their technical usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) from contraposition (aks al-naqid) and explain why simple conversion is preferred in scientific reasoning.

Core Idea
Simple conversion uses the same terms; contraposition adds negation — that is why the sciences rely on simple conversion alone.

The passage distinguishes three types of conversion: simple conversion, contraposition of the opposite, and contraposition of the equivalent. Contraposition involves negation of one or both terms, making it less straightforward. Commentators like 'Isam and 'Abd al-Hakim debate whether the term 'conversion' is homonymous. This lesson unpacks these distinctions and their implications for Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of 'aks
The passage mentions three: al-mustawi (simple), aks al-naqid al-mukhalif (contraposition of opposite), and aks al-naqid al-muwafiq (contraposition of equivalent).
Simple conversion is the first type; the other two involve negation.
2
Understand the difference via negation
Simple conversion uses exact terms; contraposition negates one or both terms. 'Isam says that contraposition takes the negation of both terms or one of them.
This affects the truth conditions and validity in syllogisms.
3
Examine why simple conversion is used in sciences
'Isam explains that production by contraposition is not called a syllogism, so simple conversion is the only one considered in the sciences.
The reason is that simple conversion keeps the exact terms, making it a clear path.
4
Analyze the homonymy debate
Al-Matar suggests the word 'aks is homonymous between simple and contrapositive. 'Abd al-Hakim rejects this, arguing that after qualification, each is a distinct technical term.
This debate shows the importance of precise terminology in logic.

المطار
فَصْلٌ: في العكس المستوي
الظَّاهِرُ أَنَّهُ يُقالُ بالاشتراك على مَعنيين، ويخصُّ بالتَّقييد بالمستوي والإضافة إلى النّقيض، وإنّما وُصِفَ بِالمستوي ؛ لأنَّهُ طريقٌ مُستوِ لَا أَمتَ فِيهِ وَلَا اعوجاج؛ بخلافِ عكس النقيض، فإنَّهُ ليس طريقاً واضحاً .ا.هـ. عصام؛ أي: لعدم استعماله في العلوم والإنتاجاتِ لِمَا قالُوا مِنْ أنَّ الإنتاج بواسطة عكس نقيض القضية لا يُسمَّى قياساً ؛ بخلاف الإنتاج بالعكس المستوي، فإِنَّهُ مُعتبر في العلوم، وذلكَ لرعاية أطراف القضيَّة فيه، حيثُ أُخِذَ عينُ أطرافها ولمْ يُؤخذ نَقيضُها، وأَمَّا عكس النَّقيض فإِنَّهُ يؤخذُ فِيهِ نَقيضُ طَرفَي القضيَّةِ أو نقيضُ أحدهما، وفي عبد الحكيم: أنَّ لفظ العكس ليسَ مُشتركاً لفظيًا بين العكس المستوي وعكس النَّقيض؛ إذْ لَا دليل على وضعِهِ لِلمعنيين ؛ بل بعد تخصيص العكس اللغوي بالصفة وبالإضافة؛ استعمِلَ كُلٌّ مِنَ المقيَّدَيْنِ في المعنى الاصطلاحي.
Chapter: On Simple Conversion. Conversion is used in the verbal noun sense, i.e., exchanging the two terms of a proposition. Dasuqi: (His saying: 'al-mustawi') i.e., excluding contraposition of the opposite and contraposition of the equivalent; thus conversions are three, and the first is what the word refers to when unqualified. (His saying: 'It is used in the verbal noun sense') i.e., real meaning. (His saying: 'Exchanging... etc.') What is meant by exchanging the two terms is exchange in wording not in meaning; because the subject is intended for individuals before conversion, and for the intension after conversion, and the predicate is intended for the intension before conversion, and for the individuals after conversion, and this is in categorical propositions. As for conditional ones, the antecedent before conversion is a necessary condition, and after conversion it is a necessary consequent. al-Matar: Chapter: On Simple Conversion. It is apparent that it is said by homonymy for two meanings, and it is specified by the qualification 'simple' and the addition to 'contrapositive'. And it is described as 'simple' because it is a straight path with no curvature or crookedness, unlike contraposition, for it is not a clear path. End of 'Isam; i.e., because it is not used in the sciences and productions for what they said: that production by means of contraposition of a proposition is not called a syllogism, unlike production by simple conversion, for it is considered in the sciences, and that is because of consideration of the terms of the proposition in it, where the exact terms are taken and their negations are not taken. As for contraposition, the negation of the two terms or the negation of one of them is taken in it. And from Abd al-Hakim: that the word 'conversion' is not homonymous between simple conversion and contraposition; since there is no evidence for its being coined for two meanings; rather, after specifying the linguistic conversion by the attribute and by addition, each of the two qualified terms is used for the technical meaning.
Source pages p. 686

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory text on the logical operation of conversion (al-'aks). It begins with the chapter heading 'On Simple Conversion', then gives a compact definition: 'Conversion, in its verbal-noun sense, is the exchange of the two terms of a proposition.' This is the standard definition. The commentator Dasuqi then expands on the text: the term 'simple' (mustawi) here excludes two other types of conversion: 'contraposition of the opposite' and 'contraposition of the equivalent' (both involve negation). Thus 'aks mustawi is the first of three types of conversion, and the unqualified term 'conversion' normally refers to simple conversion. Dasuqi clarifies that 'exchanging the two terms' is a verbal exchange only, not a change in meaning. In categorical propositions, before conversion the subject refers to individuals (extension) and the predicate refers to the intension (meaning), but after conversion they reverse roles. In conditional propositions, the antecedent and consequent exchange roles: the antecedent which was a necessary condition (lazim) becomes the consequence (malzum). Then another commentator, al-Matar (possibly a misreading), repeats the chapter heading and adds that the term 'simple conversion' is used homonymously for two meanings: simple conversion and contraposition, but it is specified by the qualifier 'simple' or the addition 'to the contrapositive'. The term 'simple' is used because it is a straight path (mustawin) with no curvature, unlike contraposition which is not a clear path. Isam's comment explains that contraposition is not used in the sciences because it does not produce a syllogism properly; only simple conversion is used because it keeps the exact terms without negation. Finally, 'Abd al-Hakim argues that the word 'conversion' is not genuinely homonymous between simple conversion and contraposition; rather, after specifying the linguistic word with the adjectives 'simple' and 'contrapositive', each becomes a distinct technical term. The passage thus defines the technical term 'simple conversion', distinguishes it from contraposition, and explains its importance in scientific reasoning.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
عكس النقيض
المخالف
الموافق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion is the only type of conversion used in Islamic sciences because it keeps the terms identical; contraposition introduces negation and is not recognized as a proper syllogistic tool.

Simple Conversion and the Condition of Truth Preservation
Defines simple conversion and clarifies that preservation of truth is conditional, not actual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition of preservation of truth to convert categorical and conditional propositions and verify the validity of the conversion.

Core Idea
Simple conversion preserves truth hypothetically: if the original is true, the converse follows necessarily.

This lesson introduces the fundamental operation of simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) in Islamic logic. You will learn how to swap the terms of a proposition while ensuring that truth is conditionally preserved. The passage from the Tadhheeb al-Sharh clarifies a common misunderstanding: conversion does not require actual truth of the original, only that if it were true, the converse would be true.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of propositions covered
The passage describes conversion in categorical propositions (subject-predicate) and conditional propositions (antecedent-consequent). Recognize which type each example belongs to.
Categorical: 'Every human is an animal'; Conditional: 'Fire exists; heat exists'.
2
Examine the conversion examples
For the categorical example, the subject 'human' becomes predicate and predicate 'animal' becomes subject, with a change from universal to particular. For the conditional, the antecedent 'fire exists' becomes consequent and consequent 'heat exists' becomes antecedent.
Note the quantifier change: every → some.
3
Understand the condition of preservation of truth
The text states that preservation of truth means 'if the original were true, the converse would be true.' This is a conditional (hypothetical) condition, not a requirement that the original be actually true.
The converse is a necessary consequence (lazim) of the original proposition.
4
Apply to an Islamic legal example
Take the proposition 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (khamr haram). Its simple conversion yields 'Some forbidden thing is an intoxicant'. Test: if the original is true (all intoxicants are indeed forbidden), then the converse must be true (there exists at least one forbidden thing that is an intoxicant).
This shows how conversion is used in usul al-fiqh to generate new propositions from established rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلما كانتِ محمولاً وتالياً، وجعل المحمول والتّالي موضوعاً ومقدماً، كقولنا في عكس كلُّ إنسان حيوان : بعض الحيوان إنسان»، وفي : النَّارُ موجودةٌ ؛ كانت الحرارة موجودة»: «قد يكونُ إذا كانت الحرارة موجودةً كانتِ النَّارُ موجودة». فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي. والمراد ببقاءِ الصِّدْقِ : أنَّ الأصلَ لو كان صادقاً كان العكس صادقاً ; لأنَّ العكس لازم القضيَّةِ، فلو فُرِضَ صدقُ القضيَّة لَزِمَ صدقُ العكس، وإلا لزم صدقُ الملزومِ بدونِ اللازم. ولم يعتبر بقاءُ الكَذِبِ;
Whenever [the term] was a predicate and a consequent, and making the predicate and the consequent a subject and an antecedent, like our saying in the conversion of 'Every human is an animal': 'Some animal is a human', and in 'Fire exists; heat exists': 'It may be that when heat exists, fire exists.' Chapter: On Simple Conversion. And what is meant by 'preservation of truth' is that if the original were true, the converse would be true, because the converse is a necessary consequence of the proposition. So if the truth of the proposition is assumed, the truth of the converse would follow; otherwise, the truth of the entailing [proposition] would follow without the necessary consequence. And preservation of falsehood is not considered.
Source pages p. 690

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) and explains its key condition. The author first describes conversion in categorical and conditional propositions: in categoricals, the subject becomes predicate and vice versa; in conditionals, the antecedent and consequent swap. Examples are given: 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is a human'; 'Fire exists; heat exists' (a conditional) converts to 'When heat exists, fire exists' (note the order swap). Then the chapter heading explicitly introduces simple conversion. The crucial point is the definition of 'preservation of truth' (baqa' al-sidq). The author clarifies that this does not mean the original proposition must actually be true. Rather, it means that if the original is assumed true, then the converse must necessarily be true. This is because the converse is a necessary consequence (lazim) of the original. The test is: if the truth of the original is assumed, then the truth of the converse follows. If not, then a true original would not have a necessary consequence, which is impossible. Importantly, preservation of falsehood is not considered — that is, if the original is false, the converse might be false or true; conversion does not require any condition regarding falsehood. This guards against the misunderstanding that conversion only applies to actually true propositions.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي (al-'aks al-mustawi): Simple conversion: swapping the two terms of a proposition (subject-predicate in categorical, antecedent-consequent in conditional) while preserving truth and quality. [Note: In later logic texts, the term is stable, though some distinguish between 'simple' and 'per accidens' conversion.]
بقاء الصدق (baqa' al-sidq): A conditional condition: if the original proposition is true, the converse must be true. It does not require actual truth of the original. [Note: Some early logicians interpreted preservation of truth as requiring actual truth; this text explicitly corrects that.]
لازم (lazim): A necessary consequence: the converse is logically entailed by the original proposition, so that whenever the original is true, the converse is true. [Note: In broader logic, lazim can refer to any necessary conditional, not just conversion.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The converse must be actually true.
B) If the original is true, the converse must be true.
C) The original must be false for conversion to work.
D) The converse must be false.

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion swaps the terms of a proposition, and its validity hinges on the conditional preservation of truth: if the original is true, the converse necessarily follows.

Definition and Conditions of Simple Conversion
Defines simple conversion and its conditions of preserving truth and quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions of simple conversion and apply the rule for converting affirmative categorical propositions.

Core Idea
Simple conversion preserves truth hypothetically, not actually; it preserves quality but not quantity for affirmatives.

Simple conversion is a fundamental immediate inference that swaps subject and predicate. However, not every proposition can be converted validly. This lesson unpacks the two conditions—preservation of truth and preservation of quality—that define valid conversion, and explains why only particular affirmatives result from universal affirmatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions of simple conversion
From the passage, note that simple conversion requires both preservation of truth (baqa' al-sidq) and preservation of quality (baqa' al-kaif). These are the essential criteria.
These conditions are distinct: one deals with truth value, the other with affirmation/negation.
2
Understand 'preservation of truth' as hypothetical
The text clarifies that the original need not be actually true; rather, if we assume the original true, the converse must be true. This is because the converse is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the original.
If the original is false, its converse may still be true—so falsehood is not preserved.
3
Understand why falsehood is not a condition
The author explicitly states that preservation of falsehood is not required, because a false premise can have a true converse. For example, 'Every animal is human' is false, but its converse 'Some human is animal' is true.
This prevents over-restricting conversion to only actually true statements.
4
Understand 'preservation of quality'
The converse must match the original in being affirmative or negative. An affirmative original yields an affirmative converse; a negative original yields a negative converse.
Quality is separate from quantity (universal/particular).
5
Apply the rule for affirmative propositions
The passage states that all affirmative propositions (whether universal or particular) convert only to a particular affirmative. For 'Every A is B', the valid converse is 'Some B is A'. For 'Some A is B', the converse is also 'Some B is A' (same).
This is a key rule: universal affirmatives lose their universality upon conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي. وَالْمُرَادُ بِبَقَاءِ الصِّدْقِ: أَنَّ الْأَصْلَ لَوْ كَانَ صَادِقًا كَانَ الْعَكْسُ صَادِقًا؛ لِأَنَّ الْعَكْسَ لَازِمُ الْقَضِيَّةِ، فَلَوْ فُرِضَ صِدْقُ الْقَضِيَّةِ لَزِمَ صِدْقُ الْعَكْسِ، وَإِلَّا لَزِمَ صِدْقُ الْمَلْزُومِ بِدُونِ اللَّازِمِ. وَلَمْ يُعْتَبَرْ بَقَاءُ الْكَذِبِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ كَذِبِ الْمَلْزُومِ كَذِبُ اللَّازِمِ. وَأَرَادَ بِبَقَاءِ الْكَيْفِ: أَنَّ الْأَصْلَ لَوْ كَانَ مُوجَبًا كَانَ الْعَكْسُ أَيْضًا مُوجَبًا، وَإِنْ كَانَ سَالِبًا فَسَالِبًا. وَالْمُوجَبَةُ كُلِّيَّةً كَانَتْ أَوْ جُزْئِيَّةً إِنَّمَا تَنْعَكِسُ.
Chapter: On Simple Conversion. And what is meant by preservation of truth is that if the original were true, the converse would be true; because the converse is a necessary concomitant of the proposition. So if the truth of the proposition is assumed, the truth of the converse follows; otherwise, the truth of the necessitating entity would occur without the necessary concomitant. And preservation of falsehood is not considered because falsehood of the necessitating entity does not necessitate falsehood of the necessary concomitant. And he intended by preservation of quality that if the original were affirmative, the converse would also be affirmative, and if negative, then negative. And the affirmative proposition, whether universal or particular, is only converted (i.e., it is not converted except as a particular).
Source pages p. 691

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion ('aks mustawi) and its two conditions: preservation of truth (baqa' al-sidq) and preservation of quality (baqa' al-kaif). The author first clarifies that preserving truth does not mean the original must be actually true; rather, if we assume the original to be true, the converse must also be true, because the converse is a logical consequent. If the converse were false while the original true, that would mean a necessary relation (the original implying the converse) is broken—impossible. Falsehood is not preserved because a false original can have a true converse (e.g., 'Every animal is human' is false, but its converse 'Some human is animal' is true). Preservation of quality means the converse must match the original in being affirmative or negative. Then the rule: affirmative propositions, whether universal or particular, convert only to a particular affirmative. For example, 'Every human is animal' converts to 'Some animal is human'. This is a foundational rule in logic for immediate inference.

Key Terms
الْعَكْسُ الْمُسْتَوِي
بَقَاءُ الصِّدْقِ
بَقَاءُ الْكَيْفِ
الْمُوجَبَةُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion is a valid immediate inference only when the original proposition's truth would guarantee the converse's truth, and the quality is preserved.

Conversion of Affirmative Propositions: Only as Particular
Author states the rule that affirmative propositions convert only as particular and explains why with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of conversion for affirmative propositions to both categorical and conditional statements and identify when universal conversion would be invalid.

Core Idea
Affirmative propositions always convert as particular, never as universal.

In logic, conversion is the operation of swapping the subject and predicate (or antecedent and consequent) while preserving truth. This lesson examines the rule for affirmative propositions: they only convert as particular. The author explains the rationale and clarifies that the rule applies to singular and indefinite statements as well.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
The passage says any affirmative proposition (universal or particular) converts only as particular. This applies to both categorical (e.g., 'Some human is animal') and conditional (e.g., 'Whenever fire exists, heat exists') affirmatives.
2
Understand the reason
Universal conversion fails because the predicate or consequent may be more general than the subject or antecedent. For example, 'Every human is animal' does not convert to 'Every animal is human' because animals include non-humans.
3
Test with your own example
Take an affirmative proposition from Islamic law, such as 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. Convert it correctly: 'Some forbidden thing is intoxicant'. Why is universal conversion invalid? Because forbidden things include non-intoxicants.
4
Handle singular and indefinite propositions
The author clarifies that 'universal' includes singular propositions (like 'Zayd is a man') by judgment, and 'particular' includes indefinite propositions (like 'Man is an animal'). So the rule covers these as well.
This prevents objections that the rule might not apply to singular terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمُوجَبَةُ كُلِّيَّةً كَانَتْ أَوْ جُزْئِيَّةً إِنَّمَا تَنْعَكِسُ؛ أَيْ: لَا تَنْعَكِسُ إِلَّا جُزْئِيَّةً، وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ تَنْعَكِسْ كُلِّيَّةً لِجَوَازِ عُمُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ أَوِ التَّالِي فِي بَعْضِ الْمَوَادِّ، كَقَوْلِنَا: بَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ، وَ: كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ النَّارُ مَوْجُودَةً كَانَتِ الْحَرَارَةُ مَوْجُودَةً. وَأُجِيبُ: بِأَنَّ مُرَادَهُ بِالْكُلِّيَّةِ حَقِيقَةً أَوْ حُكْمًا، فَدَخَلَتِ الشَّخْصِيَّةُ؛ لِأَنَّهَا فِي حُكْمِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ، وَكَذَا يُقَالُ فِي الْجُزْئِيَّةِ، فَدَخَلَتِ الْمُهْمَلَةُ؛ لِأَنَّهَا فِي قُوَّةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ كَمَا مَرَّ.
The affirmative proposition, whether universal or particular, only converts; i.e., it only converts as particular. And it only does not convert as universal because of the possibility of the generality of the predicate or the consequent in some materials, like our saying: 'Some human is animal', and: 'Whenever fire exists, heat exists.' And it was answered: that his intention by 'universal' is in reality or by judgment, so singular enters because it is in the ruling of universal, and likewise said for particular, so indefinite enters because it is in the force of particular as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 692

Commentary
This passage defines the rule of conversion for affirmative propositions (both categorical and conditional). It first states the rule: an affirmative proposition, whether originally universal or particular, converts only as a particular. The reason given is that the predicate (in categorical) or consequent (in conditional) may be more general than the subject/antecedent in some cases, so converting universally would yield falsehood. Examples illustrate: 'Some human is animal' (categorical) and 'Whenever fire exists, heat exists' (conditional) show that the predicate 'animal' is more general than 'human', and 'heat' is more general than 'fire'. The passage then addresses a potential objection: does this rule cover singular and indefinite propositions? The answer clarifies that 'universal' includes singular by judgment (since a singular term is treated like a universal in conversion), and 'particular' includes indefinite propositions (since an indefinite is equivalent to a particular in force). Thus, the rule applies to all types of affirmative propositions.

Key Terms
الموجبة (al-mūjaba): affirmative proposition
العكس (al-'aks): conversion
جزئية (juz'iyya): particular proposition
كلية (kulliyya): universal proposition
شخصية (shakhsiyya): singular proposition
مهملة (muhmala): indefinite proposition

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Affirmative propositions, whether categorical or conditional, always convert as particular because the predicate or consequent may be more general than the subject or antecedent.

Why Universal Propositions Convert Only Particularly
Explanation of universal conversion rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a universal proposition (categorical or conditional) converts only particularly, using examples from Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Universal conversion fails because the predicate may be broader than the subject.

In this passage, the commentator explains a key rule of immediate inference: a universal proposition always converts as a particular, never as a universal. The reason is that the predicate (in categoricals) or the consequent (in conditionals) may be more general in scope. We will break down the logical reasoning and apply it to Islamic legal examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The commentator asserts: universal propositions convert only particularly, not universally.
2
Understand the reason
The reason given is the possibility of the predicate or consequent being more general than the subject or antecedent.
Generality means the term applies to more individuals or cases.
3
Examine the categorical example
'Some human is an animal' shows that 'Every human is an animal' converts particularly. If converted universally to 'Every animal is human', it would be false because animal includes non-humans.
The commentator calls this impossibility: predicating the more specific (human) of all instances of the more general (animal).
4
Examine the conditional example
'Whenever fire exists, heat exists' converts particularly to 'Sometimes when heat exists, fire exists'. Universal conversion to 'Whenever heat exists, fire exists' fails because heat can come from other sources like the sun.
This shows the consequent (heat) is more general than the antecedent (fire).
5
Apply to Islamic legal reasoning
Consider 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram)'. It converts particularly to 'Some forbidden thing is an intoxicant'. Universal conversion to 'Every forbidden thing is an intoxicant' fails because forbidden includes carrion, blood, etc.
This rule prevents invalid inferences in legal deductions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي. وَإِنَّمَا تَنْعَكِسُ الْقَضِيَّةُ الْكُلِّيَّةُ جُزْئِيَّةً لَا كُلِّيَّةً، لِجَوَازِ عُمُومِ الْمَحْمُولِ أَوِ التَّالِي فِي بَعْضِ الْمَوَادِّ، كَقَوْلِنَا: بَعْضُ الْإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ، وَ: كُلَّمَا كَانَتِ النَّارُ مَوْجُودَةً كَانَتِ الْحَرَارَةُ مَوْجُودَةً. فَلَوِ انْعَكَسَتَا كُلِّيَّتَيْنِ لَلَزِمَ حَمْلُ الْأَخَصِّ عَلَى كُلِّ أَفْرَادِ الْأَعَمِّ فِي الْحَمْلِيَّةِ، وَاسْتِلْزَامُ الْأَعَمِّ الْأَخَصَّ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ، وَكِلَاهُمَا مُحَالٌ.
Chapter: On Simple Conversion. And the universal proposition converts only as a particular, not as a universal, because of the possibility of the generality of the predicate or the consequent in some materials, like our saying: 'Some human is an animal' and 'Whenever fire exists, heat exists'. For if they both converted as universals, it would necessitate predicating the more specific of all individuals of the more general in the categorical, and the necessitation of the more general by the more specific in the conditional, and both are impossible.
Source pages p. 693

Commentary
This passage defines the rule of simple conversion ('aks mustawi) for universal propositions. The commentator explains why a universal proposition (whether categorical or conditional) does not convert into a universal but only into a particular. The reason is that the predicate (in categorical) or the consequent (in conditional) may be more general in scope than the subject or antecedent. The example given is the categorical 'Some human is an animal' – here 'animal' is more general than 'human'. If we converted the universal 'Every human is an animal' universally to 'Every animal is human', it would be false. Similarly, the conditional 'Whenever fire exists, heat exists' – heat is more general (coming also from the sun) – if converted universally to 'Whenever heat exists, fire exists', it is false. The commentator then gives a proof by contradiction: if they converted universally, it would require the more specific (human) to be predicated of all individuals of the more general (animal) in the categorical, or the more general (heat) to necessitate the more specific (fire) in the conditional. Both are impossible because they would make the specific equal to the general, contradicting the assumption that one is more specific. Thus, universal propositions must convert particularly.

العكس المستوي
القضية الكلية
الجزئية

B) Because the subject 'intoxicant' is more general than 'forbidden'.

Key Takeaway
Universal propositions convert particularly because the predicate/consequent may be more general; otherwise, absurd implications would follow.

Impossibility of Universal Conversion of Universal Affirmative
Author proves universal affirmative does not convert universally using counterexample and logical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to demonstrate why a universal affirmative proposition does not convert universally using a counterexample from Islamic law or theology.

Core Idea
A single counterexample disproves universal conversion; the universal affirmative converts only to particular.

In this lesson, we examine why the universal affirmative proposition 'All intoxicants are haram' does NOT imply 'All haram things are intoxicants'. This is a foundational principle in immediate inference, central to valid reasoning in Islamic logic. The commentator proves this using a proof by contradiction and the concept of the more general and more specific.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal affirmative and its converse
Write the proposition as 'Every S is P'. The converse is 'Every P is S'. Note the subject and predicate swap. Use an Islamic example: S = intoxicant (musakkir), P = haram.
Original: 'Every intoxicant is haram'. Converse: 'Every haram thing is an intoxicant'.
2
Test with a counterexample
Find a specific instance where the original is true but the converse is false. If even one such instance exists, universal conversion fails. For example, theft is haram but not an intoxicant.
Thus the converse is false, proving universal conversion invalid.
3
Generalize the principle
The commentator states: if the conversion fails in one matter, it fails universally. The meaning of 'not converting' is that the converse does not necessarily follow in all cases.
Arabic: إذا ثبت عدم انعكاس الموجبة إلى الكلية في مادة واحدة؛ ثبت عدم انعكاسها إلى الكلية مطلقاً.
4
State the correct conversion rule
While universal affirmative does not convert universally, it does convert to a particular affirmative (I: Some P is S). For example, from 'Every intoxicant is haram' we validly infer 'Some haram thing is an intoxicant'.
This is the standard rule in categorical logic.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَلو انعكستا كليَّتين؛ لزم حمل الأخصّ على كلِّ أفراد الأعم في الحملية، واستلزام الأعمّ الأخصَّ في الشَّرطيَّة؛ وكلاهما محال. أمَّا حمل الأخصّ على كلِّ أفراد الأعمّ فظاهر. وأمَّا استلزام الأعمّ للأخص، فلأنَّه لو استلزم الأخص لزم أن يُوجد الأخصُّ كلَّما وُجِدَ الأعم، وذلك بيّن البطلان. وإذا ثبت عدم انعكاس الموجبة إلى الكلِّيَّةِ في مادَّةٍ واحدةٍ؛ ثبت عدم انعكاسها إلى الكلية مطلقاً؛ لأنَّ معنى عدم انعكاس القضيَّة أن لا يلزمها العكس لزوماً كلّيًّا، وذلك يتحقَّق بالتَّخلُّفَ في صورةٍ واحدةٍ.
If they converted as universals, it would necessitate the predication of the more specific on all individuals of the more general in the categorical, and the implication of the more general for the more specific in the conditional; both are impossible. As for the predication of the more specific on all individuals of the more general, it is obvious. And as for the implication of the more general for the more specific, because if the more general implied the more specific, then the more specific would exist whenever the more general exists, and that is clearly false. And if it is established that the universal affirmative does not convert universally in one matter, then its failure to convert universally in all matters is established; because the meaning of a proposition not converting is that its converse does not necessarily follow it universally, and that is realized by failure in a single instance.
Source pages p. 694

Commentary
This passage is an argument from the commentator explaining why a universal affirmative proposition (e.g., 'Every human is an animal') does not convert to a universal affirmative ('Every animal is a human'). The argument uses a proof by contradiction (burhān al-khalf). The commentator first states the assumption: if it did convert universally, then in categorical propositions, the more specific would be predicated of every individual of the more general (e.g., 'human' would be said of every animal), which is false because there are animals that are not human. Similarly, in conditional propositions (e.g., 'Whenever heat exists, fire exists'), the converse 'Whenever fire exists, heat exists' would require that fire is always present when heat is, which is also false (e.g., heat from other sources). Thus both cases are impossible. The commentator then notes that the impossibility of universal conversion in even a single instance proves that the conversion is not necessary universally; the criterion for a valid conversion is that it holds in all cases (mutṭarid), not merely in some. The glosses clarify that the conversion is actually to a particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some animal is a human'), and this is the standard rule in logic.

Key Terms
العكس (al-‘aks): The operation of transposing subject and predicate of a proposition while preserving truth (in valid conversions). [Note: Stable: always refers to immediate inference by swapping terms.]
الأعم والأخص (al-a‘amm wa al-akhass): The more general (genus) and more specific (species) terms; here used to illustrate impossibility of universal conversion. [Note: Standard usage in logic; no significant shift.]
الموجبة الكلية (al-mūjiba al-kulliyya): A universal affirmative proposition (A: All S are P). [Note: Stable across tradition.]
مادة (mādda): A specific content or instance of a proposition; here used for the example 'Every human is an animal'. [Note: Technical term in logic for the matter (content) of a proposition; stable.]

Conversion of Universal Affirmative Propositions
Author explains why universal affirmative converts to particular, not universal, and when universal conversion is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct simple conversion of any universal affirmative proposition and identify cases where universal conversion is valid.

Core Idea
Universal affirmative converts to particular, except when subject and predicate are coextensive.

Simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) is a fundamental operation in Islamic logic used to derive new propositions from given ones. For universal affirmative propositions like 'Every human is an animal,' the instinctive guess might be that the converse is also universal: 'Every animal is human.' But this is false because not every animal is human. The correct converse is the particular: 'Some animal is human.' However, there is a special case when the subject and predicate are equal in extension, as in 'Every human is rational' – here the universal converse 'Every rational is human' holds. This lesson unpacks why the general rule holds and when the exception applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Recognize the proposition as universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every A is B'). Note the quantity (universal) and quality (affirmative).
2
Apply the general conversion rule
For a universal affirmative, swap subject and predicate and change quantity to particular, preserving truth. The result is a particular affirmative: 'Some B is A.'
This is the default rule, as explained in the passage.
3
Check for coextensiveness
Examine whether the predicate term (B) is equal in extension to the subject term (A). If every B is also A unconditionally, then the universal conversion is valid. The passage gives the example: 'Every human is rational' because 'rational' and 'human' are coextensive in classical logic.
Coextensiveness means the two terms refer to exactly the same set of individuals.
4
Determine the correct converse
If the predicate is broader than the subject (e.g., 'animal' is broader than 'human'), the converse must be particular: 'Some animal is human.' If the predicate is coextensive, the universal converse is valid: 'Every rational is human.'
The passage emphasizes that conversion must hold in all materials (maddah) – a single failure invalidates universal conversion for the general rule.
