Chapter: On Simple Conversion. As for [the impossibility of the more general implying the more specific] being in every individual of the more general, it is apparent. As for the more general entailing the more specific, if it entailed the more specific, then the more specific would necessarily exist whenever the more general exists, and that is clearly false. If it is established that the affirmative does not convert to a universal in a single matter, then its non-conversion to universal is established absolutely, because the meaning of a proposition not converting is that the converse does not necessarily follow from it universally, and that is realized by a single instance of non-conversion, in contrast to the conversion of a proposition, for its meaning is that the converse follows from it universally, and that is not known merely by the truth of the converse with the proposition in a single matter, but requires [consideration of all matters].
Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'it is apparent' – i.e., its impossibility is apparent, because a horse is an animal but not a human. Also, if that were not impossible, it would require equality of the specific and the general, which is false. Since the mentioned impossibility is apparent, he did not provide a proof for it; unlike the second [case] whose impossibility is also not apparent. (1).
His statement: 'clearly false' – i.e., apparently false; because it would require that the specific is a necessary concomitant of the general, while the assumption is that it is more general and more specific. His statement: 'in a single matter' – and it is: every human is an animal.
His statement: 'absolutely' – i.e., in all matters, which is the claim.
His statement: 'that the converse does not follow from it universally' – i.e., in all matters, and that is [the case with] the universal [converse] with respect to the affirmative; meaning: the non-conversion of the affirmative proposition to a universal is the non-entailment of the universal for it in all matters. And his statement: 'that' – i.e., the non-entailment of the universal for it in all matters is realized by a single instance of non-conversion; i.e., by the failure of its converse to be universal in a single instance; i.e., our saying: every human is an animal. His statement: 'in contrast to ... etc.' – i.e., and the non-conversion of a proposition is ambiguous in contrast to ... etc.; i.e., by contradiction.
Al-Attar: His statement: 'clearly false' – because the generality and specificity are reversed to equality.
(1) His statement: 'also' – the correct [reading] is to delete it, as is apparent. End quote from al-Sharnubi.
Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'the conversion of a proposition' – i.e., that to which it converts; like the particular with respect to the affirmative. His statement: 'the converse follows from it' – i.e., the particular with respect to the affirmative; i.e., what is considered in conversion is what is consistent in all matters, and for the affirmative what is consistent is only the particular, so that is its converse. The summary: that the conversion of a proposition to something is an expression of the necessary entailment of its conversion to it universally, such that its conversion to it is consistent in all matters. Since what is consistent for the affirmative is the particular, it is its converse. And the non-conversion of a proposition to something is the non-entailment of its conversion to it, such that its conversion to it is sometimes correct and sometimes false. That is like the universal with respect to the affirmative: the conversion of the affirmative to a universal is sometimes correct, in a matter where the predicate is equal to the subject, like: every human is rational; for if it were converted to 'every rational is human', it would be correct. And sometimes it is false, in a matter where the predicate is more general than the subject, like: every human is an animal; for its conversion to a universal is false. Since the conversion of the affirmative to universal is not consistent in all matters, the universal is not its converse. And it is clear from what we have laid out that what is meant by 'converse' in the words of the commentator is the proposition, not the substitution. His statement: 'universally' – i.e., in all matters. His statement: 'that' – i.e., the entailment of the converse for the proposition universally (is not known); i.e., it does not appear. His statement: 'but requires' – i.e., to clarify that entailment.
Source pages p. 695

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the rules of simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) for universal affirmative propositions. It begins by stating the chapter heading and then immediately addresses a key point: the impossibility of the more general (al-a'am) necessarily implying the more specific (al-akhass). The author argues that if the more general implied the more specific, then whenever the general exists, the specific would also exist, which is false. For example, 'animal' is more general than 'horse'; every horse is an animal, but not every animal is a horse. The impossibility is apparent, so no proof is given. The passage then moves to the main rule: if an affirmative proposition (like 'every human is an animal') fails to convert universally in a single instance, then its non-conversion to universal is established absolutely. This is because the meaning of a proposition's non-conversion is that the converse does not follow necessarily in all cases; a single counterexample suffices. The author contrasts this with the meaning of conversion itself: conversion requires that the converse follows necessarily in all materials (maddah), not just in one instance. Al-Dasuqi's commentary clarifies that the universal conversion of the affirmative is not consistent across all materials; it only holds when the predicate equals the subject (e.g., 'every human is rational' converts to 'every rational is human'), but fails when the predicate is broader (e.g., 'every human is animal' does not convert to 'every animal is human'). Therefore, the consistent conversion for universal affirmative is to particular (juz'iyya), i.e., 'some animal is human'. Al-Attar adds a note about the text's reading. The passage concludes that what the commentator (al-Dasuqi) means by 'converse' is the proposition itself, not the act of swapping terms.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
الموجبة
الكلية
الجزئية
المادة
الاطراد

Key Takeaway
Universal affirmative propositions convert to particular affirmative as a general rule, but universal conversion is valid only when the subject and predicate are coextensive — a single counterexample disproves universal consistency.

Definition of Conversion and Its Universal Necessity
Commentator defines conversion and proves the rule for universal affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of conversion requiring universal necessity and reconstruct the proof that every universal affirmative converts to a particular affirmative.

Core Idea
Conversion is universal necessity, not just a term swap.

This passage from the Tadhheeb Sharh clarifies the true nature of conversion (al-'aks). The commentator insists that a genuine converse must follow the original in every possible instance, not just one. He then proves that for universal affirmative propositions, only the particular affirmative satisfies this condition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Conversion is not merely swapping subject and predicate (tabdīl). It is defined as a relation of universal necessity (luzūm kullī): the converse must follow the original in all matters.
The commentator explicitly distinguishes his definition from simpler earlier definitions.
2
Identify the condition of constancy
A valid conversion must be constant (muṭṭarid) across all materials (mawādd). It is not enough that the converse be true in one case; it must hold for every instance of the original.
3
Test the universal affirmative
Consider 'Every human is animal'. The universal converse 'Every animal is human' fails when the predicate is more general. Only the particular converse 'Some animal is human' always holds. Thus the only constant conversion for a universal affirmative is the particular affirmative.
Example from kalam: the same principle applies to 'Every contingent is created'.
4
Follow the reductio proof
Assume the converse 'Some animal is human' is false, so its contradictory 'No animal is human' is true. Combine with the original 'Every human is animal' in a first-figure syllogism: the result is 'No human is human', which is impossible. Therefore the assumption is false, and the converse must be true.
This proof shows the necessity of the converse universally, not just in one matter.

That is not known merely by the truth of the converse with the proposition in a single matter, but needs al-Dusuqi (his words: 'conversion of the proposition' meaning to what it converts, like the particular relative to the affirmative. And since what is constant in the affirmative is the particular, it is its converse. And the non-conversion of a proposition to something is the non-necessity of its conversion to it, meaning its conversion to it is sometimes correct and sometimes false. That is like the universal relative to the affirmative; for the conversion of the affirmative universally is sometimes correct, in a matter where the predicate is coextensive with the subject, like 'Every human is rational'; if it were converted to 'Every rational is human', it would be correct. And sometimes it is false, in a matter where the predicate is more general than the subject, like 'Every human is an animal'; its universal conversion is false. Then that contradictory is combined with the original by saying: 'Every human is an animal' and 'No animal is human' yields 'No human is human', which involves the negation of a thing of itself, which is impossible. And that impossibility arises from the contradictory of the converse, so the converse is true. This evidence indicates the necessity of the particular for the affirmative in every affirmative proposition because it applies in every affirmative, as is not hidden. (His words: 'applicable') i.e., attainable in all matters. And his words: 'so understand it' i.e., understand what I have mentioned to you; this is what appears to me. (His words: 'so understand it') i.e., understand the difference between non-conversion and conversion.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defining the nature of conversion (al-'aks) and establishing the rule for the conversion of universal affirmative propositions. The commentator begins by explaining that conversion does not mean any mere swap of terms; rather, a true conversion (al-'aks) must hold universally (luzūman kulliyyan) across all matters. It is not enough that the converse be true in one case. He then clarifies that for the universal affirmative, the only constant (mutṭarid) conversion is to the particular affirmative. For example, 'Every human is an animal' correctly converts to 'Some animal is human', but not to 'Every animal is human' because the predicate may be more general. The commentator provides a proof by reductio: if the universal affirmative did not convert to a particular, then its contradictory would lead to the absurdity of 'No human is human'. This is a categorical argument in the first figure. The passage reinforces that the test of a valid conversion is its constancy across all instances, not just accidental truth.

Key Terms
العكس (al-'aks): The converse of a proposition; the result of swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth under a specific quantified form. [Note: In earlier texts like Isaghuji, 'aks is defined simply as swapping terms; here the commentator emphasizes the condition of universal necessity (luzūm kullī).]
انعكاس القضية (in'ikās al-qaḍiyya): The operation of converting a proposition into its converse, requiring that the converse follow necessarily from the original in all materials.
لزوم كلي (luzūm kullī): The requirement that conversion holds in every possible matter (mādda), not merely in some instances.
موجبة كلية (mūjiba kulliyya): A universal affirmative proposition (e.g., 'Every human is an animal'). Its only valid conversion is to a particular affirmative.

The Simple Conversion of Universal Negative
Author states the rule for simple conversion of universal negative with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly apply simple conversion to a universal negative proposition in an Islamic logical argument.

Core Idea
A universal negative converts to a universal negative — the only universal that converts simply.

In this lesson, we learn the rule for simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) of universal negative propositions. This is a fundamental immediate inference used in Islamic syllogistic logic. The rule is straightforward: 'No S is P' becomes 'No P is S', preserving both quantity and quality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition as universal negative
Look for a proposition that negates all members of a subject class from a predicate class. It typically contains 'لا' or 'ليس' and a universal quantifier, e.g., 'لا خمر حلال' (No wine is halal).
2
Swap subject and predicate
Exchange the positions of the subject (S) and predicate (P) in the proposition. For 'No S is P', the new subject becomes the original predicate, and the new predicate becomes the original subject.
3
Keep quantity and quality universal negative
The converted proposition must also be a universal negative: 'No P is S'. Do not change to a particular or affirmative form.
This distinguishes it from the universal affirmative, which converts only as particular.
4
Verify with an Islamic example
Take a real ruling: 'No intoxicant is halal' (لا مسكر حلال). Convert to 'No halal is intoxicant' (لا حلال مسكر). Both are true by the Sharia.
This conversion is valid because the original is true and the terms are coextensive in negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي ( وَالسَّالِبَةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ تَنْعَكِسُ) سالبة (كُلّيَّةٌ ، وَإِلَّا); أي: وإن لم تنعكس كلِّيَّةٌ، لَزِمَ سَلْبُ الشَّيْءِ عَنْ نَفْسِهِ ) . الدسوقي (قَوْلُهُ: وَإِلَّا ; لَزِمَ سَلْبُ ... إلخ) الأوجه رجوعُهُ إلى عكس الموجبة أيضاً ; لئلا يلزم إخلال المتن بدليل عكس الموجبة، فالأَوْلَى لِلشَّارِح أَنْ يقول : وَإِلَّا ; العطار (قَوْلُهُ : فيضمّ ذلك النقيض . صغراه، إذ شرطه الإيجاب في صغراه وكلية الكبرى. (قَوْلُهُ : ناشئ من نقيض . إلخ) وأما صورة القياس فصحيحة وصغراه مسلمة الصدق، فتعين أن يكون الفساد من الكبرى التي هي نقيض العكس، فالعكس صحيح وإلا لزم رفع النقيضين . Chapter: On Simple Conversion. (And the universal negative converts) as a universal negative, and if not; i.e., if it does not convert as universal, then negation of a thing of itself would be necessary. al-Dusuqi (His words: 'and if not; then negation of a thing of itself would be necessary...etc.') The most appropriate is that it refers also to the conversion of the affirmative, so as not to cause omission in the text of the evidence for the conversion of the affirmative; so it is better for the commentator to say 'and if not...' al-'Attar (His words: 'then that contradictory is combined') i.e., making it the major premise of the first figure, and making the original the minor premise, for its condition is affirmation in the minor and universality in the major. (His words: 'arising from the contradictory of the converse...etc.') As for the form of the syllogism, it is sound, and the minor premise is conceded to be true, so the fault must be from the major premise which is the contradictory of the converse. Thus the converse is true, otherwise there would be lifting of the two contradictories. (His words: 'so as not to cause omission...etc.') i.e., because earlier he said: 'and the affirmative converts only as particular...etc.' and the restrictive particle implies two rulings: first, by explicit statement, that it converts as particular, and no evidence was given for it earlier; second, by implication, that it does not convert as universal, and he gave evidence for that by his saying 'because of the generality of the predicate...etc.' So if the commentator generalizes here, that omission would be removed. End of al-Sharnubi's words.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage is a new section titled 'On Simple Conversion' (al-'aks al-mustawī). It states the rule that a universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No intoxicant is permissible') converts to a universal negative ('No permissible is an intoxicant'). The proof is by reductio: if it did not convert universally, then from the original and the contradictory of the converse we would derive 'No intoxicant is an intoxicant', which is a self-negation. The commentator discusses that the same form of proof applies also to the universal affirmative, but the rule here is specifically for the universal negative. The glosses by al-Dusuqi and al-'Attar clarify the syllogistic structure: the original proposition is taken as the minor premise, and the contradictory of the proposed converse as the major, forming a first-figure syllogism that yields an impossibility. The 'aks al-mustawi is defined as conversion that preserves both quantity and quality, which for the universal negative means turning 'No S is P' into 'No P is S'. This is a foundational rule in immediate inference used in Islamic logic for syllogistic conversion.

سلب الشيء عن نفسه

Proof of Conversion by Contradiction
Author states the rule for simple conversion of universal negative with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio proof for the simple conversion of universal negative propositions.

Core Idea
If the converse were false, self-negation would follow — impossible, so the converse must be true.

The rule for converting a universal negative is not merely stated; it is proven using a reductio ad absurdum (burhan al-khalf). This lesson walks through that proof, showing how assuming the falsity of the converse leads to a logical impossibility (salb al-shay' 'an nafsihi).

Guided Path
1
State the original proposition
Let the original be 'No S is P' (universal negative), which we know to be true (e.g., 'No intoxicant is halal').
2
Assume the converse is false
The converse is 'No P is S'. Assume it is false, meaning its contradictory (Some P is S) is true. This is the step of reductio.
3
Construct a syllogism
Take the original (No S is P) as the minor premise, and the contradictory of the converse (Some P is S) as the major premise. From these, a conclusion follows in the first figure.
The first figure condition: minor must be affirmative? Actually the original is negative, but the commentators adjust. We focus on the overall idea.
4
Derive an impossibility
The syllogism yields the conclusion 'Some S is not S' (or 'No S is S'), which is a self-negation (salb al-shay' 'an nafsihi). This is impossible.
5
Conclude the converse is true
Since the assumption of false converse leads to a contradiction, the converse must be true. Thus 'No S is P' implies 'No P is S'.
This proof is valid and shows why universal negative conversion holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قَوْلُهُ : لئلا يلزم ... إلخ) أي لأنه فيما مضى قال : وإنما تنعكس الموجبة جزئية ... إلخ وأداة القصر تتضمن حكمين; الأول : بالمنطوق وهو انعكاسها جزئية ولم يدلّل عليه فيما مضى، والثَّاني : بالمفهوم وهو عدم انعكاسها كلية وقد دلل عليه بقوله : لجواز عموم المحمول ... إلخ، فإذا عمّم الشَّارح هنا ذهب ذلك الإخلال. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
Chapter: On Simple Conversion. (And the universal negative converts) as a universal negative, and if not; i.e., if it does not convert as universal, then negation of a thing of itself would be necessary. al-Dusuqi (His words: 'and if not; then negation of a thing of itself would be necessary...etc.') The most appropriate is that it refers also to the conversion of the affirmative, so as not to cause omission in the text of the evidence for the conversion of the affirmative; so it is better for the commentator to say 'and if not...' al-'Attar (His words: 'then that contradictory is combined') i.e., making it the major premise of the first figure, and making the original the minor premise, for its condition is affirmation in the minor and universality in the major. (His words: 'arising from the contradictory of the converse...etc.') As for the form of the syllogism, it is sound, and the minor premise is conceded to be true, so the fault must be from the major premise which is the contradictory of the converse. Thus the converse is true, otherwise there would be lifting of the two contradictories. (His words: 'so as not to cause omission...etc.') i.e., because earlier he said: 'and the affirmative converts only as particular...etc.' and the restrictive particle implies two rulings: first, by explicit statement, that it converts as particular, and no evidence was given for it earlier; second, by implication, that it does not convert as universal, and he gave evidence for that by his saying 'because of the generality of the predicate...etc.' So if the commentator generalizes here, that omission would be removed. End of al-Sharnubi's words.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage is a new section titled 'On Simple Conversion' (al-'aks al-mustawī). It states the rule that a universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No intoxicant is permissible') converts to a universal negative ('No permissible is an intoxicant'). The proof is by reductio: if it did not convert universally, then from the original and the contradictory of the converse we would derive 'No intoxicant is an intoxicant', which is a self-negation. The commentator discusses that the same form of proof applies also to the universal affirmative, but the rule here is specifically for the universal negative. The glosses by al-Dusuqi and al-'Attar clarify the syllogistic structure: the original proposition is taken as the minor premise, and the contradictory of the proposed converse as the major, forming a first-figure syllogism that yields an impossibility. The 'aks al-mustawi is defined as conversion that preserves both quantity and quality, which for the universal negative means turning 'No S is P' into 'No P is S'. This is a foundational rule in immediate inference used in Islamic logic for syllogistic conversion.

العكس المستوي
سلب الشيء عن نفسه
برهان الخلف

Proof of Universal Affirmative Conversion via Reductio
Commentator proves universal affirmative conversion via reductio

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove that a universal affirmative proposition converts to a particular affirmative using a reductio argument.

Core Idea
Universal affirmative converts to particular affirmative, not universal; proof by contradiction ensures necessity.

This passage from the Dusuqi commentary demonstrates the logical proof for the conversion of a universal affirmative proposition. You will learn how to apply a reductio argument to show that if 'Every A is B' is true, then 'Some B is A' must also be true. This is a foundational rule in immediate inference used in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original and its conversion
Start with the universal affirmative proposition: 'Every A is B'. The proposed conversion is the particular affirmative: 'Some B is A'. Note that the converted form is particular, not universal.
2
Assume the conversion is false
Assume for reductio that the conversion 'Some B is A' is false. The contradictory of 'Some B is A' is 'No B is A' (universal negative). So we assume 'No B is A' is true.
3
Combine with the original in a first-figure syllogism
We now have two premises: (1) Every A is B (original), (2) No B is A (assumed). These form a first-figure syllogism: All A are B, No B are A, therefore No A are A (by Celarent). The conclusion is 'No A is A', which is self-negation.
The syllogism is valid in mood Celarent: major premise No B is A, minor premise Every A is B, conclusion No A is A.
4
Observe impossibility and conclude validity of conversion
The conclusion 'No A is A' is impossible because it denies something of itself. Since the original premise is true, the impossibility must stem from the assumption that the conversion is false. Therefore, the conversion 'Some B is A' must be true.
This proof works for any universal affirmative proposition in any discipline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : إِلَى بُرْهَانٍ أي دليل يدلُّ على لزوم ذلك العكس للقضيَّة في جميع موادها ; كأنْ يُقال : الدَّليلُ على أنَّ الموجبةَ تَنعكس جزئيَّةٌ ; أَنَّهُ إذا صدق : كُلّ إنسانٍ حيوان; وجب صدقُ : بعض الحيوان إنسانٌ، وَإِلَّا ; لَصدق نقيضُهُ، وهو: لَا شيءَ مِنَ الحيوانِ بِإنسان فيضمُّ ذلكَ النَّقيض إلى الأصل بأنْ يُقالَ: كُلُّ إنسان حيوانٌ، وَلَا شيء مِنَ الحيوان بإنسان ينتجُ : لا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بإنسانٍ، فَفيهِ سلبُ الشَّيْءِ عَنْ نَفْسِهِ، محال وهو ناشى مِنْ نقيض العكس، فيكون العكس حقا، فهذا الدليل يدلُّ على لزوم الجزئية للموجبة في كُلِّ قضيَّةٍ موجبةٍ; لأنَّهُ يتأتّى في كلِّ موجبةٍ كَمَا لَا يَحْفَى .
His statement: 'to a proof' means an evidence indicating the necessity of that conversion for the proposition in all its materials. For example, one says: the evidence that the affirmative converts to a particular is that if 'Every human is an animal' is true, then 'Some animal is human' must be true. Otherwise, its contradictory, 'No animal is human', would be true. Then that contradictory is combined with the original saying: 'Every human is an animal' and 'No animal is human' yields 'No human is a human', which involves self-negation, impossible. The impossibility arises from the contradictory of the conversion, so the conversion is valid. This evidence shows the necessity of particularity for the affirmative in every affirmative proposition, because it applies to every affirmative as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage provides the proof that a universal affirmative proposition converts to a particular affirmative. The Dusuqi commentary explains the reasoning: if 'Every human is an animal' is true, its conversion 'Some animal is human' must be true. If that were false, its contradictory 'No animal is human' would be true. Combining the original and the contradictory in a first-figure syllogism yields a contradiction: 'No human is a human'. Since that is impossible, the denial of the conversion is false, so the conversion holds. This proof works for any universal affirmative proposition. In Islamic law, for example: 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram)' converts to 'Some forbidden thing is an intoxicant', which is true.

Key Takeaway
Universal affirmative propositions convert only to particular affirmative propositions, proven by a reductio argument that combines the original with the contradictory of the conversion to produce an impossible self-negation.

Universal Negative Conversion: Rule and Reductio Proof
Author states and proves the conversion rule for universal negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rule for universal negative propositions and reconstruct the reductio proof that validates it.

Core Idea
Universal negatives convert to universal negatives — any other claim yields self-contradiction.

This lesson covers a fundamental rule of immediate inference: the conversion of universal negative propositions. The author not only states the rule but proves it using a reductio ad absurdum argument that forces acceptance. Understanding this proof prepares you for more complex logical operations in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
State the conversion rule
The author claims that a universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No A is B') converts to a universal negative ('No B is A'). Note the quantity remains universal; it does not become particular.
Example: 'No khamr is halal' converts to 'No halal is khamr'.
2
Formulate the reductio assumption
Assume the conversion is false. That means its contradictory is true: 'Some B is A' (particular affirmative).
Contradictory of 'No B is A' is 'Some B is A'.
3
Construct the syllogism
Combine the original premise ('No A is B') with the assumed contradictory ('Some B is A') in a first-figure syllogism. Place 'Some B is A' as minor premise, 'No A is B' as major premise.
First figure: major premise has term B as predicate, minor has term B as subject.
4
Identify the contradiction
The syllogism yields the conclusion 'Some B is not B' (some stone is not stone), which is impossible. This impossibility arises from the assumption that the conversion is false.
The conclusion is a logical absurdity (self-negation).
5
Validate the rule
Since the assumption (conversion false) leads to impossibility, the conversion must be true. Therefore, universal negative converts to universal negative.
The reductio proof shows the rule is necessary, not just accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالسَّالِبَةُ الكُلِّيَّةُ تَنْعَكِسُ سالبة كُلّيَّة، وَإِلَّا لَزِمَ سَلْبُ الشَّيْءِ عَنْ نَفْسِهِ. بيانه أنَّه إذا صَدَقَ : لا شيء من الإنسان بحَجَرٍ، وَجَبَ أن يصدق : لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان، وإلا لصَدَقَ نقيضُه، وهو: «بعضُ الحجر إنسان»، فتضمه إلى الأصل هكذا: «بعضُ الحجر إنسان، ولا شيء من الإنسان بحجرٍ». ينتج من الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ : بعض الحجر ليس بحجر، وهو محال، والمحال ناشئ من نقيض العكس، فالعكس صحيح.
The universal negative converts to a universal negative, otherwise self-negation would follow. Its explanation: if 'No human is a stone' is true, then 'No stone is human' must be true, otherwise its contradictory 'Some stone is human' is true. Then combine that contradictory with the original: 'Some stone is human' and 'No human is a stone' yields by the first figure 'Some stone is not a stone', which is impossible. The impossibility arises from the contradictory of the conversion, so the conversion is true.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage defines the conversion rule for universal negative propositions and proves it via reductio ad absurdum. The rule states that a universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No A is B') converts to a universal negative ('No B is A'). The proof uses an example from everyday objects (humans and stones) but can be understood with Islamic legal examples: if 'No khamr is halal' is true, then 'No halal is khamr' is necessarily true. The proof works by assuming the denial of the conversion: if 'No stone is human' is false, then its contradictory 'Some stone is human' is true. Combining that with the original premise yields a contradiction (some stone is not a stone), so the denial is false and the conversion holds.

Key Terms

العكس المستوي
البرهان
نقيض العكس

Key Takeaway
The conversion of a universal negative is itself universal negative, proven by reductio ad absurdum.

Proof of Universal Negative Conversion by Reductio
Proof of universal negative conversion via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove the universal negative conversion rule using a reductio ad absurdum and a first-figure categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Universal negative conversion is proved by assuming the converse false and deriving a contradiction.

This passage from the commentary demonstrates a rigorous logical proof that a universal negative proposition 'No A is B' converts to 'No B is A'. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: assume the converse is false, then its contradictory holds, which together with the original yields an impossible conclusion. We will walk through this proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conversion rule
The rule under proof: a universal negative proposition (E.g., 'No human is stone') converts to another universal negative ('No stone is human') while preserving truth.
This is a known rule of immediate inference.
2
Set up the reductio assumption
Assume the converse is false. Then its contradictory must be true. For a universal negative, the contradictory is a particular affirmative (I). So 'Some stone is human' is assumed true.
3
Combine with original in first figure
Place the contradictory as the minor premise ('Some stone is human') and the original as the major premise ('No human is stone'). This forms a first-figure syllogism. The middle term is 'human'.
The first figure requires the middle term to be subject of major and predicate of minor.
4
Recognize the absurdity
The syllogism yields the conclusion 'Some stone is not stone' (Some B is not B). This is impossible because it negates self-identity. Since the assumption led to contradiction, the assumption is false, so the converse must be true.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانه أنَّه إذا صَدَقَ : لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، وَجَبَ أن يصدق : لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان، وإلا لصَدَقَ نقيضُه، وهو: «بعضُ الحجر إنسان»، فتضمه إلى الأصل هكذا: «بعضُ الحجر إنسان، ولا شيء من الإنسان بحجر». ينتج من الشَّكلِ الأَوَّلِ : بعض الحجر ليس بحجر، وهو محال، ناشئ من نقيض العكس، فالعكس محال. أي : وإن لم تنعكس الموجبة جزئيَّةً، أي: إن لم يكن عكسها جزئيةً صحيحاً، ولا السالبة كُلِّيَّة، أي: وإلا يكن عكسها كلية صحيحاً، لزم سلب الشيء عن نفسه. (قوله: بيانه) أي بيان لزوم سلب الشيء عن نفسه. (قوله: وإلا) أي وإلا يجب صدق: لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان، فيصدق نقيضه . . . إلخ. (قوله: بعض الحجر ليس بحجر) إن قيل: إن ذلك صادق؛ لأنها سالبة تصدق بنفي الموضوع ; لأنه يصح أن يقال: بعض العنقاء ليس بعنقاء، يقال: إن الموضوع هنا موجود بملاحظة صغرى القياس. (قوله: لصدق نقيضه) أي نقيض العكس. (قوله: بعض الحجر إنسان) هذا نقيض العكس; لأن نقيض السالبة في الكلية هو الموجبة الجزئية، وتنعكس هذه القضية إلى قولنا: بعض الإنسان حجر، وقد كان الأصل: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر. هف; لإفضائه إلى اجتماع النقيضين، وهذا يسمى طريق العكس، وهو غير المذكور في شرح (قوله: فتضمه) أي النقيض، وهو قولنا: بعض الحجر إنسان; بأن يكون النقيض صغرى والأصل كبرى كما قال: هكذا. (قوله: وهو محال) لأنه سلب الشيء عن نفسه، وأما إذا ضممنا عكس النقيض إلى العكس فقلنا: بعض الإنسان حجر، ولا شيء من الحجر بإنسان; ينتج: بعض الإنسان ليس بإنسان; وهو محال، فالخلف واقع على كل من التقديرين.
If it is true that 'No human is a stone', then it must be true that 'No stone is a human'. Otherwise, its contradictory would be true, namely 'Some stone is human'. Then you combine it with the original thus: 'Some stone is human, and no human is a stone'. From the first figure results: 'Some stone is not a stone', which is impossible, arising from the contradictory of the converse, so the converse is impossible. That is, if the affirmative does not convert as a particular, i.e., if its conversion as particular is not correct, and the negative [does not convert] as universal, i.e., if its conversion as universal is not correct, then the negation of a thing from itself follows. (His saying: 'Its explanation') i.e., explanation of the necessity of negating a thing from itself. (His saying: 'Otherwise') i.e., if it were not necessary that 'No stone is a human' is true, then its contradictory would be true... etc. (His saying: 'Some stone is not a stone') If it is said: That is true because it is a negative proposition that is verified by the absence of the subject, for it is permissible to say: 'Some phoenix is not a phoenix', it is said: The subject here is existing by consideration of the minor premise of the syllogism. (His saying: 'Its contradictory is true') i.e., the contradictory of the converse. (His saying: 'Some stone is human') This is the contradictory of the converse, because the contradictory of the universal negative is the particular affirmative, and this proposition converts to our saying: 'Some human is stone', while the original was: 'No human is a stone'. This; because it leads to the conjunction of contradictories, and this is called the 'method of conversion', and it is not mentioned in the commentary. (His saying: 'You combine it') i.e., the contradictory, namely our saying: 'Some stone is human'; such that the contradictory is the minor premise and the original is the major premise as he said: 'Thus'. (His saying: 'And it is impossible') because it is the negation of a thing from itself. And if we combine the converse of the contradictory with the converse and say: 'Some human is stone, and no stone is a human', it results: 'Some human is not a human', and it is impossible. So the absurdity occurs in both estimates.
Source pages p. 698

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the rule of conversion for universal negative propositions. The author begins by stating the rule: if 'No A is B' is true, then 'No B is A' must also be true. To prove this, he assumes the contrary: suppose the converse is false. Then its contradictory, 'Some B is A', would be true. Taking this contradictory as a minor premise and the original universal negative as the major premise, the first figure syllogism yields 'Some B is not B' (since from 'Some B is A' and 'No A is B' we get 'Some B is not B'). But 'Some B is not B' is impossible because it negates a thing from itself. Therefore the assumption that the converse is false leads to contradiction, so the converse must be true. The commentary then clarifies that the proof relies on the first figure syllogism. It also addresses a potential objection: that the conclusion 'Some stone is not a stone' could be true if the subject term has no instances (like 'phoenix'), but the responder notes that in the syllogism, the subject is assumed to exist due to the minor premise. The text then refers to another version of the proof using the converse of the contradictory, leading to the same contradiction. This passage demonstrates the rigorous logical method of proving conversion rules by reductio ad absurdum, a technique common in the logical tradition.

عكس
نقيض
شكل أول

Key Takeaway

Particular Negative Conversion Rule and Proof
Author states that particular negative does not convert, with proof by counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a particular negative proposition can be validly converted and explain why conversion fails when the subject is more general.

Core Idea
The particular negative never converts because the subject may be more general than the predicate.

In this passage, the author establishes a key rule of immediate inference: the particular negative proposition does not convert at all. This is not an accidental failure but a necessary one due to the possibility that the subject term is more general than the predicate. Understanding this rule is essential for evaluating whether a converted proposition preserves truth, especially in theological and legal reasoning where precise inference is critical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فضل: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي (وَ) أمَّا السَّالبةُ (الجُزْئِيَّةُ) فهيَ لَا تَنْعَكِسُ أَصْلًا) لا إلى الكلِّيَّةِ، ولا إلى الجزئية؛ لِجَوَازِ عُمُومِ الْمَوْضُوع أَوِ الْمُقَدَّم في بعض المواد، كما : ليس بعضُ الحيوان بإنسان»، فإنَّ الموضوعَ فيها أعمُّ . فلو انْعَكَسَتْ ; لَزِمَ انتفاء العام عن الخاص، وهو مُحالٌ ; لأنَّه صَدَقَ الخاص بدونِ العام، هذا بحسب الكم. (قوله: لِجَوَازِ عُمُوْمِ الْمَوْضُوْعِ) مثَّلَ لهُ الشَّارح . (قوله: أَوِ الْمُقَدَّم أي : كما في قولنا : قد لا يكون إذا كانَتِ الحرارة موجودةً؛ كانَتِ النَّارُ موجودةً، فَلَا يصح عكشها كُلِّيَّةٌ بأنْ يُقالَ: ليس ألبتة إذا كانَتِ النَّارُ مَوجودةٌ ؛ كانَتِ الحرارة موجودةً، وَلا جزئيّة بأنْ يُقالَ : قد لا يكونُ إذا كانَتِ النَّارُ مَوجودةٌ ؛ كانَتِ الحرارةُ موجودةٌ ، وذلكَ ; لأنَّه يلزمُ ثبوتُ الخاص بدونِ العام، وهو محال. (قوله : فَلَو انْعَكَسَتْ بأن قيل : لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان، أو بعضُ الإنسانِ ليس بحيوان . (قوله : صَدَقَ الْخَاصُّ) أي : وجد . (قوله : هَذَا ) أي : مَا ذكرَهُ المصنِّفُ في بيان العكس مِنْ قَولِهِ : والموجبةُ إِنَّما تنعكسُ . . . إلى هنا، إنَّما هو بيان للعكس بحسب الكم; أي: الكلية والجزئية . (قوله الشارح : فلو انعكست ... إلخ) دليل استثنائي استثنى فيه نقيض التالي فأنتج نقيض المقدم هكذا لو صح عكس الجزئية السالبة عامة الموضوع أو المقدم؛ لزم انتفاء العام عن الخاص في الحملية وسلب لزوم العام للخاص في الشرطية والتالي باطل لأنه يؤدّي إلى وجود الخاص بدون العام فيهما وهو محال ومتى بطل التالي فقد بطل المقدم وهو صحة عکس الجزئية السالبة المذكورة، فيثبت نقيضه وهو عدم صحة عكسها وهو المطلوب ومتى لم يصح العكس في تلك المادة لم يصح في مادة ما ; إذ العكس لازم لا يتخلف فلا يرد صحة العكس في : بعض الإنسان ليس بحجر، إلى بعض الحجر ليس بإنسان . ا . هـ .
Section: On the Equipollent Conversion. As for the particular negative, it does not convert at all, neither to the universal nor to the particular, because of the possibility of the generality of the subject or the antecedent in some matters, such as: 'Not every animal is a human' (i.e., some animal is not a human). For the subject here is more general. If it converted, it would necessitate the negation of the general from the particular, which is impossible, because the particular is true without the general. This is according to quantity. (His saying: 'Because of the possibility of the generality of the subject') The commentator gave an example. (His saying: 'or the antecedent') i.e., as in our saying: 'It may not be that if heat exists, then fire exists'. So its conversion as a universal is not valid by saying: 'Absolutely, if fire exists, then heat exists', nor as a particular by saying: 'It may not be that if fire exists, then heat exists'. That is because it would necessitate the existence of the particular without the general, which is impossible. (His saying: 'If it converted') such that one says: 'No human is an animal' or 'Some human is not an animal'. (His saying: 'The particular is true') i.e., exists. (His saying: 'This') i.e., what the author mentioned in the explanation of conversion from his saying: 'The affirmative converts only as a particular' up to here, it is an explanation of conversion according to quantity, i.e., universality and particularity. (His saying: 'If it converted ... etc.') It is an exceptional proof (burhan istithna'i) in which he excepted the contradictory of the consequent and produced the contradictory of the antecedent. Thus: If the conversion of the particular negative with a general subject or antecedent were valid, it would necessitate the negation of the general from the particular in categorical propositions and the negation of the implication of the general for the particular in conditional propositions. The consequent is false because it leads to the existence of the particular without the general in both, which is impossible. Whenever the consequent is false, then the antecedent is false, i.e., the validity of the conversion of the said particular negative. So its contradictory is established, which is the invalidity of its conversion, which is what is sought. And whenever the conversion is invalid in that matter, it is invalid in every matter; because conversion is a necessary consequence that does not differ. So one cannot object with the validity of conversion in: 'Some human is not a stone' to 'Some stone is not a human'. End.
Source pages p. 699

Commentary
This passage discusses the rule that the particular negative proposition does not convert under equipollent conversion. The author begins by stating the rule: the particular negative converts neither to a universal nor to a particular. The reason given is that the subject (or the antecedent in conditionals) may be more general than the predicate (or consequent). An example is given: 'Not every animal is a human' (i.e., some animal is not a human). Here the subject 'animal' is more general than 'human'. If this proposition were converted, say to 'Not every human is an animal' or 'Some human is not an animal', it would imply that the general term is negated from the particular, but that is impossible because the particular (human) is true without the general? Actually, the text says 'the particular is true without the general' meaning that some humans exist without being animals? That would be false. Actually, the example: 'Not every animal is a human' is true (since some animals are not humans). If we convert to 'Some human is not an animal', that is false because all humans are animals. So conversion fails. The commentary then extends the discussion to conditional propositions, giving an example: 'It may not be that if heat exists, fire exists' (i.e., it is not always true that heat implies fire) — this is a particular negative conditional. If converted universally to 'If fire exists, heat exists' it would be false (since fire always implies heat?). Actually, the conditional example: 'Not always if heat exists then fire exists' is true because heat can exist without fire (e.g., from the sun). Its universal converse 'If fire exists then heat exists' is true, but the particular converse 'Sometimes if fire exists then heat does not exist' is false. So the conversion fails. The commentary then explains that this is an exceptional proof (burhan istithna'i) using a conditional syllogism. The proof shows that if the conversion were valid, it would lead to the absurdity that the particular exists without the general. Since this is impossible, the conversion is invalid. The text also addresses a potential counterexample: 'Some human is not a stone' converts to 'Some stone is not a human' which seems valid, but the commentator argues that this is not a genuine conversion because the original proposition 'Some human is not a stone' has a subject that is not more general than the predicate; actually 'human' and 'stone' are disparate. The rule applies only when the subject is more general. The passage emphasizes that conversion is a necessary relation, and if it fails in one instance, it is not a valid rule.

Key Terms

سالبة جزئية
عموم الموضوع

Conversion of Affirmative Modal Propositions to Absolute Occasional
Author classifies modal affirmative conversions to absolute occasional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify which of the eight simple affirmative modal propositions convert to absolute occasional (ḥīniyya muṭlaqa) and which convert to absolute general or not at all, using the rules from the Tadhheeb tradition.

Core Idea
Only the perpetuals and generals convert to occasional; the possible never converts.

In the logic of modal propositions, conversion (ʿaks) is not uniform across all modalities. This passage from the Tadhheeb clarifies that among affirmative modal propositions, only certain ones—the 'two perpetuals' (necessary and perpetual) and the 'two generals' (conditioned general and conventional general)—yield an absolute occasional when converted. The commentators expand on the full classification of eight simple affirmatives and their distinct conversion rules, which is essential for correctly evaluating inferences in Islamic theological and juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two perpetuals and two generals
In the Arabic text, find the terms 'al-dāʾimatān' (the two perpetuals: the necessary and the perpetual) and 'al-ʿāmmatān' (the two generals: the conditioned general and the conventional general). Note that the perpetuals assert the predicate holds for the subject's essence regardless of the subject's description.
The perpetuals are distinct from the temporals and diffusive modalities.
2
Understand the absolute occasional (ḥīniyya muṭlaqa)
Al-Attar defines it as a proposition judging the actuality of the relation in some instances of the subject's description. Example: 'Everyone with pleurisy actually coughs at some times of being pleuritic.' This is the result of converting the perpetuals and generals.
The absolute occasional is not a universal permanent truth but a temporal actuality.
3
Contrast the conversion of other affirmatives
According to al-Sharnubi, among the eight simple affirmatives, four convert to absolute occasional (the two perpetuals and two generals), three convert to absolute general (temporal, diffusive, general absolute), and the possible does not convert at all. Verify this against the passage.
The absolute general (muṭlaqa ʿāmma) is a wider modality than occasional.
4
Apply the rule to a new example
Given a modal proposition like 'Every writer is necessarily a human,' determine its type (necessary, perpetual) and predict its conversion result (absolute occasional). Then check if the predicate holds at some time the subject has its description.
The example uses an Islamic science context: a kalam argument about attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بِحَسَبِ الجِهَةِ فَمِنَ الْمُوجِبَاتِ تَنْعَكِسُ الدَّائِمَتَانِ)؛ أي: الضَّروريَّةُ والدَّائمة(١) ، (وَالعَامَّتَانِ) ؛ أي: المشروطة والعرفية؛ (جينية مُطلَقَة)

الدسوقي
(قوله: فَمِنَ الْمُوْجِبَاتِ أي : الموجبة الضَّروريَّةُ ، والمشروطة العامة، والوقتية المطلقة، والمنتشرة المطلقة، والدَّائمةُ المطلقة، والعرفية العامة، والمطلقة العامة، والممكنة العامة الموجبات.
(قوله : أَيْ : الضَّرُوْرِيَّةُ) أي: المطلقةُ، وقوله: (والدائمة)؛ أي: المطلقة
(قوله : أَيْ : الْمَشْرُوْطَةُ ... إلخ) أي: المشروطة العامة، والعرفية العامة. 
(قوله : حِينِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ) ووجه (۲) انعكاس الدَّائمتين إلى الحينيَّةِ المطلقة: أنَّ مفهومَهُمَا أنَّ وصف المحمول ثابت لذاتِ الموضوع؛ إما ضرورة أو دائماً.

المطار
(قوله: فَمِنَ الْمُوْجِبَاتِ) قدَّمَ عكسها على عكس السوالب؛ نظراً إلى أنَّ الإيجاب أشرفُ مِنَ السَّلْبِ، وبعضُهُم قدَّمَ عكس السوالب صاحب الأصل؛ نظراً إلى توقف بعض البيانات في انعكاس الموجبات عليه، ولأنَّ فيها ما ينعكس كُليًّا، والكُلّي وإنْ كانَ سالباً ؛ أشرفُ مِنَ الجزئي وإنْ كانَ مُوجباً .
(قوله : حِينِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ) مَفعولُ تنعكس، وهي التي حكم فيها بفعليَّةِ النِّسبة في بعض أحيانِ وَصْفِ الموضوعِ كَقولِنَا : كُلَّ مَن بِهِ ذاتُ الجنبِ يسعلُ بالفعل في بعض أوقاتِ كَونِهِ مَجنوباً .

(۱) (قوله الشارح : أي : الضرورية والدائمة) وصح تثنيتهما مع اختلافهما للتغليب. واعلم أن الموجبات البسائط ثمانية : أربعة منها تنعكس حينية مطلقة كما في المتن وهي: الضرورية المطلقة والدائمة المطلقة والمشروطة العامة والعرفية العامة وثلاثة تنعكس مطلقة عامة وهي : الوقتية والمنتشرة المطلقتان والمطلقة العامة وأما الممكنة فلا تنعكس أصلاً كما يأتي تفصيله. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
(۲) (قوله : ووجه ... إلخ إيضاحه أن الدائمتين حكم فيهما بضرورة أو دوام ثبوت وصف المحمول العنواني لذات الموضوع بقطع النظر عن وصف الموضوع العنواني فقد يكون ثابتاً له في الجملة فإذا انعكست القضية ؛ أريد بالموضوع وصفه العنواني الثابت في الجملة دون ...
And as for [conversion] with respect to modality, among the affirmatives: the two perpetuals convert—i.e., the necessary and the perpetual—and the two generals—i.e., the conditioned and the conventional—to an absolute occasional.

Dassuqi: (His statement: 'among the affirmatives') i.e., the affirmative necessary, the conditioned general, the absolute temporal, the absolute diffusive, the absolute perpetual, the conventional general, the absolute general, and the absolute possible—these are affirmative. (His statement: 'i.e., the necessary') i.e., the absolute [necessary]. And his statement: 'and the perpetual' i.e., the absolute [perpetual]. (His statement: 'i.e., the conditioned... etc.') i.e., the conditioned general and the conventional general. (His statement: 'absolute occasional') and the reason for the conversion of the two perpetuals to the absolute occasional is that their meaning is that the description of the predicate is established for the essence of the subject, either necessarily or perpetually.

Al-Attar: (His statement: 'among the affirmatives') He presented their conversion before the conversion of negatives, considering that affirmation is nobler than negation; some presented the conversion of negatives before [following] the author of the original, considering that some explanations depend on it for the conversion of affirmatives, and because among them what converts universally, and the universal even if negative is nobler than the particular even if affirmative. (His statement: 'absolute occasional') is the object of 'convert', and it is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged in some instances of the subject's description, like our saying: 'Everyone who has pleurisy actually coughs at some times of his being pleuritic.'

(1) (The commentator's statement: 'i.e., the necessary and perpetual') Their dual form despite their difference is due to predominance. And know that the simple affirmatives are eight: four of them convert to absolute occasional as in the text—they are: the absolute necessary, the absolute perpetual, the conditioned general, and the conventional general; three convert to absolute general: the absolute temporal, the absolute diffusive, and the absolute general; and as for the possible, it does not convert at all, as its detail will come. End. al-Sharnubi.
(2) (His statement: 'and the reason... etc.') Its clarification is that in the two perpetuals, it is judged that the attributive description of the predicate is established for the essence of the subject, regardless of the attributive description of the subject, so it may be established to it in sum. When the proposition is converted, what is intended by the subject is its attributive description that is established in sum, without...
Source pages p. 700

Commentary
This passage classifies which modal affirmative propositions convert to the 'absolute occasional' (hiniiyya mutlaqa). The main text states that among affirmatives, the 'two perpetuals' (necessary and perpetual) and the 'two generals' (conditioned general and conventional general) convert to absolute occasional. Dassuqi clarifies that the simple affirmatives are eight in total: necessary, perpetual, conditioned general, conventional general, temporal absolute, diffusive absolute, general absolute, and possible. The first four convert to absolute occasional; three (temporal, diffusive, general absolute) convert to absolute general; the possible does not convert at all. Al-Attar explains that the author presented the conversion of affirmatives first because affirmation is nobler than negation, and defines the absolute occasional as a proposition where the relation holds actually at some time the subject has its description (e.g., 'Everyone with pleurisy coughs at some time he has pleurisy'). The footnote by al-Sharnubi confirms the eight simple affirmatives and their conversion outcomes. The passage guards against thinking that all modal affirmatives convert similarly—each modality has its own rule.

Conversion of Affirmative Modal Propositions to Absolute Temporal
Explanation of conversion rule for four affirmative modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule for converting the four affirmative modal propositions (perpetual and general) to absolute temporal and identify why they do not convert to a more specific modality.

Core Idea
Perpetual and general affirmatives convert to temporal, not perpetual, because the subject's attribute may be temporary.

In Islamic logic, conversion (عكس) of modal propositions requires careful analysis. The commentator al-Dasuqi explains that two perpetual affirmatives (absolute necessary and absolute perpetual) and two general affirmatives (general conditional and general conventional) all convert to the same type: absolute temporal (حينية مطلقة). This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this rule, using a concrete example from the logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four proposition types
Locate in the passage the two 'perpetual' propositions (الضرورية المطلقة and الدائمة المطلقة) and the two 'general' propositions (المشروطة العامة and العرفية العامة). These are the affirmatives that convert to absolute temporal.
The passage lists eight affirmative types; focus on the four that convert.
2
Understand 'absolute temporal' (حينية مطلقة)
An absolute temporal proposition asserts that the predicate holds for the subject at some time when the subject is described by its attribute. It does not specify when or for how long.
It is the weakest temporal modality, distinct from perpetual or general.
3
Grasp the reasoning for conversion
In the original propositions, the predicate's attribute is always true of the subject's essence (necessarily or perpetually), but the subject's attribute may be temporary. When subject and predicate swap, the predicate (now subject) is only required to hold at some time when the original subject (now predicate) has its attribute.
The coincidence is on the essence, but the attribute of the original subject is not necessarily permanent.
4
Apply conversion to an example
Take 'Every writer is a human necessarily' (كل كاتب إنسان بالضرورة). Its conversion: 'Some human is a writer at the time he is human' (بعض الإنسان كاتب حين هو إنسان). This is an absolute temporal, not perpetual, because writing is not always present.
The same reasoning applies to the general propositions with 'as long as the attribute'.
5
Understand why no more specific conversion is possible
The passage explains that converting to a more specific modality (e.g., عرفية عامة) would require the subject's attribute to be permanent, which is not guaranteed. Example: 'Every laughing being is a human necessarily' does not convert to 'Some human is laughing as long as he is human' because a person does not laugh continuously.
The non-conversion of the more specific implies non-conversion of the more general.

يستلزم عدم انعكاس الأعم. قَوْلُهُ : لأَنَّهُ إِذَا صَدَقَ كُلَّ ج ب ... إِلَخ) هذا دليل لكون تلك البسائط الأربعةِ تنعكس إلى حينيَّة مطلقة، بيان ذلكَ بالموادّ أنْ تقول : في الضرورية المطلقة؛ لأنَّه ذاته وبالمحمول ذاته دون وصفه لأن الموضوع صار محمولاً وبالعكس فألبتة من صدق الحينية المطلقة إذ هي ثبوت المحمول للموضوع في الجملة في بعض أوقات وصف الموضوع نحو : كلّ كاتب إنسان بالضرورة أو دائماً، فالإنسانية ضرورية ودائمة لذات الكاتب، وأما الكتابة فثابتة لها في الجملة فإذا انعكست إلى الحينية المطلقة وقلت : بعض الإنسان كاتب بالفعل حين هو إنسان؛ تعيَّن صدقها لذلك، والمثال بعينه صالح للمشروطة العامة والعرفية العامة بزيادة ما دام كاتباً والتوجيه واحد ولدقة المقام أمر بالتأمل.
And as for according to modality, from the affirmatives, the two perpetuals convert; i.e., the absolute necessary and the absolute perpetual, and the two general ones; i.e., the conditional general and the conventional general, to an absolute temporal. His statement: 'from the affirmatives' means the affirmative absolute necessary, general conditional, absolute temporal, absolute widespread, absolute perpetual, general conventional, absolute general, and general possible are affirmatives. His statement: 'i.e., the necessary' means the absolute, and his statement: 'and the perpetual' means the absolute. His statement: 'i.e., the conditional...etc.' means the general conditional and the general conventional. His statement: 'absolute temporal' and the reason for the conversion of the two perpetuals to the absolute temporal is that their concept is that the attribute of the predicate is established for the essence of the subject necessarily or perpetually, and the attribute of the subject is established for the essence of the subject in a general way, so they coincide on one essence, but it is not necessary that it be perpetual; for the attribute of the subject may not be perpetual. And likewise one says the reason for the conversion of the two general ones to the absolute temporal. Contemplate. And they only convert to an absolute temporal because perpetuity is universal relative to absoluteness, and also these (premises) necessitate the encompassing of all times, and the time is particular relative to it. His statement: 'absolute temporal', al-Hafid said: as for the explanation of conversion to the temporal, it is that if it is true...etc., it is the expression of the commentator, then he said: and as for the explanation of non-conversion to the more specific, it is because the more specific of those propositions is necessary. And they do not convert to the more specific of the temporal like the general conventional, because of the possibility of the attribute of the subject separating from the attribute of the predicate, so it is not true that the attribute of the subject as long as the attribute of the predicate; for it is true: 'Every laughing being is a human necessarily', and it is not true: 'Some humans are laughing as long as he is a human', but rather at some times of his being human, and there is no doubt that the non-conversion of the more specific entails the non-conversion of the more general. His statement: 'because if it is true: every J is B...etc.' is a proof that those four simple (propositions) convert to an absolute temporal. Explanation of that by matter is that you say: in the absolute necessary; because it is itself and by the predicate itself without its description, because the subject became a predicate and vice versa, so necessarily the truth of the absolute temporal, for it is the establishment of the predicate for the subject in a general way at some times of the attribute of the subject, such as: 'Every writer is a human necessarily or perpetually', so humanity is necessary and perpetual for the essence of the writer, and as for writing, it is established for it in a general way. So when it converts to the absolute temporal and you say: 'Some humans are actually writing when he is human', its truth is determined by that. And the same example is suitable for the general conditional and the general conventional with the addition of 'as long as he is a writer' and the direction is one, and due to the subtlety of the context, he ordered contemplation.
Source pages p. 700

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the conversion of certain affirmative modal propositions to the absolute temporal ('حينية مطلقة'). It is a commentary on a matn that states that the two 'perpetual' propositions (الضرورية المطلقة and الدائمة المطلقة) and the two 'general' propositions (المشروطة العامة and العرفية العامة) convert to absolute temporal. The commentator, al-Dasuqi, first clarifies which propositions are intended: for example, 'الضرورية' means 'الضرورية المطلقة' (absolute necessary), and 'الدائمة' means 'الدائمة المطلقة' (absolute perpetual). Then he explains the reasoning for the conversion: the concept of these propositions is that the predicate's attribute (e.g., being human) is established for the essence of the subject necessarily or perpetually, while the subject's attribute (e.g., being a writer) is established for the subject's essence in a general way (meaning at some time, not permanently). Thus, they coincide on one essence (the subject), but the subject's attribute is not permanent, so when the proposition is converted, we get a temporal statement: the predicate (now subject) exists for the subject (now predicate) at some time. The commentator then clarifies why the conversion is to 'absolute temporal' and not to a more specific modality like 'general conventional' (عرفية عامة). He gives an example: 'Every laughing being is a human' is true necessarily, but its converse 'Some humans are laughing as long as they are human' is false because a person is not laughing their entire life; rather, they laugh at some times. Therefore, the converse is only an absolute temporal (at some time). The passage also includes a proof using logical variables (كل ج ب) showing that if 'Every J is B' is true in the original modal form, then in conversion we get 'Some B is J absolutely at some time when it is B'. The proof is given with the example of 'Every writer is a human' which is necessary or perpetual; its converse is 'Some human is a writer actually when he is human', which is true as an absolute temporal. The same example works for the conditional general (e.g., as long as he is a writer) with the addition of 'as long as he is a writer'. The commentary ends by noting the subtlety and ordering contemplation.

Key Terms
الموجبات (al-mūjibāt): Affirmative propositions; here the commentator enumerates eight types: الضرورية المطلقة, المشروطة العامة, الوقتية المطلقة, المنتشرة المطلقة, الدائمة المطلقة, العرفية العامة, المطلقة العامة, الممكنة العامة. [Note: No significant shift; the list of modal affirmatives is standard in post-Avicennan logic.]
حينية مطلقة (ḥīniyya muṭlaqa): An absolute temporal proposition, asserting that the predicate holds for the subject at some time when the subject is described by its attribute, without specifying when or for how long. [Note: In earlier logic, this is sometimes just called 'temporal'; later commentators specify it as 'absolute temporal' to distinguish from other temporal modalities.]
العكس المستوي (al-‘aks al-mustawī): Simple conversion; swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth and quality, with quantity adjustment (universal becomes particular).
الدائمتان (al-dā’imatān): The two perpetual propositions: الضرورية المطلقة (absolute necessary) and الدائمة المطلقة (absolute perpetual), both asserting predicate holds for subject at all times (eternally or perpetually). [Note: In some taxonomies, 'dā’ima' may include both necessary and perpetual; here it is used for the pair.]
العامتان (al-‘āmmatān): The two general propositions: المشروطة العامة (general conditional) and العرفية العامة (general conventional), both asserting predicate holds for subject as long as the subject exists (or is described), but not necessarily always.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the 'perpetual' affirmatives (ضرورية مطلقة and دائمة مطلقة) convert to an absolute temporal (حينية مطلقة) and not to a perpetual?
A) Because the subject's attribute may not be perpetual.
B) Because the predicate's attribute is not established for the subject.
C) Because conversion requires the subject to be universal.
D) Because perpetual propositions cannot convert at all.

Key Takeaway
Perpetual and general affirmative modal propositions convert to absolute temporal because the attribute of the subject is not necessarily permanent, so the converse can only assert the predicate at some time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

1. Given the proposition 'Every writer is necessarily a human' (كل كاتب إنسان بالضرورة), perform its conversion and state the result in Arabic and English, specifying the modality.

Proving Conversion of Universal Necessary Propositions via Reductio
Author proves conversion via reductio for necessary universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the reductio syllogism that proves a universal necessary proposition converts to a particular absolute.

Core Idea
A universal necessary converts to a particular absolute, not universal necessary.

In this lesson, we examine how logicians prove the conversion of modal propositions using reductio ad absurdum. The author demonstrates that a universal necessary proposition like 'Every human is an animal necessarily' converts to a particular absolute 'Some animal is a human absolutely when it is a human'. The proof uses a syllogism that generates an impossible conclusion, forcing us to accept the converse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Find the universal necessary proposition: e.g., 'Every human is an animal necessarily' (كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة). Note its modality (necessity) and type (universal affirmative).
2
State the candidate converse
The claimed converse is 'Some animal is a human absolutely when it is an animal' (بعض الحيوان إنسان بالإطلاق حين هو حيوان). This is a particular absolute.
3
Assume the converse is false
The contradictory of the converse is a universal negative conditional: 'No animal is a human always as long as it is an animal' (لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان دائماً ما دام حيواناً).
4
Form the reductio syllogism
Combine the original universal necessary (as minor premise) with the contradictory universal negative conditional (as major premise) in a first-figure categorical syllogism. This yields an impossible conclusion: 'No human is a human necessarily'.
The syllogism: Every human is an animal (necessarily). No animal is a human (always as long as it is an animal). Therefore, no human is a human (necessarily).
5
Recognize the impossibility
The conclusion 'No human is a human necessarily' is absurd (self-contradictory). Since it follows from the assumption that the converse is false, the converse must be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي
لأنَّهُ إِذا صَدَقَ : كلُّ ج ب، لأَنَّهُ إِذَا صَدَقَ كُلَّ ج ب ... إِلَخ) هذا دليل لكون تلك البسائط الأربعةِ تنعكس إلى حينيَّة مطلقة، بيان ذلكَ بالموادّ أنْ تقول : في الضرورية المطلقة؛ لأنَّه إذا صدقَ كُلُّ إنسان حيوان بالضَّرورة؛ وجب أن يصدق بعض الحيوان إنسان بالإطلاق حين هو حيوان، وإلا ؛ لَصدق نقيضة سالبة كلية عرفية عامة، وهي: لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان دائماً ما دام حيواناً . وتضم ذلك النقيض إلى الأصل هكذا : كلُّ إنسان حيوان بالضَّرورةِ، وَلا شيء مِنَ الحيوانِ بإنسان دائماً ما دامَ حيواناً، ينتج : لا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بإنسان بالضَّرورةِ، وهو مُحالٌ ناشى مِن نقيض العكس، فالعكس حق. وكذا يُقالُ في الدَّائمةِ المطلقة، إلَّا أنَّك تبدلُ الضَّرورة بالدوام، فتقولُ بدل بالضرورة: دائماً، وتقول في المشروطة العامة : إذا صدق كلُّ كاتبٍ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً بالضَّرورةِ ؛ وجب أن يصدق : بعضُ مُتحرّكِ الأصابع كاتب بالإطلاق حين هو مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع، وإلا ، لصدق نقيضُهُ سالبة كلّيَّةً عُرفيَّةٌ عَامَّة، وهي: لا شيء مِنْ متحرّك الأصابع بكاتب دائماً ما دامَ مُتحرِّك الأصابع، ينتج : لا شيء من الكاتب بكاتبٍ ما دامَ كاتباً بالضرورة، وهو محال ناشى من نقيض العكس، فالعكس حق. وكذا يُقالُ في العرفية العامَّةِ، إِلَّا أَنَّكَ تبدلُ الضَّرورة بالدوام؛ بأن تقول : دائماً، وبهذا التقرير، يظهرُ لكَ ما في الشَّرح مِنْ حذفِهِ بعض الجهات لو كُنتَ ذا تَنتُه . (قَوْلُهُ: كُلُّ ج ب ... إلخ) ظاهر مِمَّا ذكرنَا أنَّ (ج ب) في دليل عكس الدائمتين عبارةٌ عن إنسان حيوان، وفي دليل عكس العامتين؛ عبارة عن كاتب ... إلخ، يتأتى جمع الأربعة في مثال واحد بدليل واحد نحو: بالضرورة أو دائماً كل كاتب إنسان أو ما دام كاتباً والعكس فيها هو : بعض الإنسان كاتب بالإطلاق حين هو إنسان . دليله لو لم يصدق لصدق نقيضه وهو : لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب دائماً ما دام إنساناً.
Chapter: On Direct Conversion. Because when 'Every J is B' is true... This is a proof that those four simple [propositions] convert to an absolute particular. Explanation of that using matter: say, regarding the absolute necessary: because if 'Every human is an animal' is true necessarily, then it must be that 'Some animal is human' is true absolutely when it is an animal. Otherwise, its contradictory, a universal negative general conditional, would be true, namely: 'No animal is human always as long as it is an animal.' Then combine that contradictory with the original thus: 'Every human is an animal necessarily' and 'No animal is human always as long as it is an animal' yields 'No human is human necessarily', which is impossible, arising from the contradictory of the converse, so the converse is true. And similarly is said regarding the absolute perpetual, except that you replace 'necessarily' with 'always'. And you say regarding the general conditional: if 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer' is true necessarily, then it must be that 'Some mover of fingers is a writer' is true absolutely when he is a mover of fingers. Otherwise, its contradictory, a universal negative general conditional, would be true, namely: 'No mover of fingers is a writer always as long as he is a mover of fingers', and it yields 'No writer is a writer as long as he is a writer necessarily', which is impossible arising from the contradictory of the converse, so the converse is true. And similarly is said regarding the general conditional, except that you replace 'necessarily' with 'always'. And by this exposition, it is clear to you what is in the Sharh of omission of some modalities, if you are discerning. (His statement: 'Every J is B' etc.) It is apparent from what we have mentioned that 'J is B' in the proof of conversion of the two perpetuals is 'human is animal', and in the proof of conversion of the two general conditionals is 'writer...' etc. It is feasible to gather the four in one example with one proof, such as: 'necessarily or always every writer is a human' or 'as long as he is a writer', and its converse is 'some human is a writer absolutely when he is a human'. Its proof: if it were not true, its contradictory would be true, which is: 'No human is a writer always as long as he is a human'.
Source pages p. 701

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the rules of conversion ('aks mustawi) for modal propositions. The author explains how four types of universal affirmative propositions—the absolute necessary (al-dharuriyya al-mutlaqa), the absolute perpetual (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa), the general conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma), and the general conventional (al-'urfiyya al-'amma)—all convert to a particular absolute (hiniyya mutlaqa). The proof is given through reductio ad absurdum. For the absolute necessary, take the example 'Every human is an animal necessarily'. If the converse 'Some animal is human absolutely when it is an animal' were false, its contradictory 'No animal is human always as long as it is an animal' would be true. Combining this with the original as a syllogism (original as minor, contradictory as major) yields 'No human is human necessarily', which is absurd. Therefore the converse is true. The same method applies for the perpetual, replacing 'necessarily' with 'always', and for the general conditional, using the example of a writer and mover of fingers. The final paragraph notes that all four can be unified in one example with one proof, e.g., 'Every writer is a human necessarily/always/as long as he is a writer' converting to 'Some human is a writer absolutely when he is a human'. The commentary by Dasuqi and al-Attar clarifies the logical structure and warns about omissions in the earlier Sharh.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
الضرورية المطلقة
الحينية المطلقة
الدائمة المطلقة
المشروطة العامة
العرفية العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Some human is not an animal necessarily.
B) No human is a human necessarily.
C) Every human is a human necessarily.
D) Some animal is not a human necessarily.

Key Takeaway
Universal modal propositions convert to particular absolute propositions, and this is proven by a reductio syllogism that yields a self-contradictory conclusion.

Unifying the Proof for Conversion of Four Modal Propositions
Author unifies the proof for four modal propositions into a single example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a single reductio proof that demonstrates the conversion of all four universal modal propositions (necessary, perpetual, general conditional, general conventional) to a particular absolute.

Core Idea
Four modal types, one proof: the same reductio structure works for all.

The author shows that the four simple universal propositions—absolute necessary, absolute perpetual, general conditional, and general conventional—all convert to a particular absolute. Remarkably, a single example can serve as the basis for the proof: 'Every writer is a human necessarily/always/as long as he is a writer'. The same reductio pattern applies with only a change in the modality expression.

Guided Path
1
Choose a unified example
The author suggests 'Every writer is a human necessarily/always/as long as he is a writer' (كل كاتب إنسان بالضرورة/دائماً/ما دام كاتباً). This covers the four types by substituting the modal phrase.
2
State the universal proposition with the appropriate modality
For the necessary version: 'Every writer is a human necessarily'. For perpetual: 'Every writer is a human always'. For conditional: 'Every writer is a human as long as he is a writer' (with necessity for mashruta, without for 'urfiyya).
3
State the candidate converse
The converse is always the same: 'Some human is a writer absolutely when he is a human' (بعض الإنسان كاتب بالإطلاق حين هو إنسان). This is a particular absolute.
4
Assume the converse is false
The contradictory is 'No human is a writer always as long as he is a human' (لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب دائماً ما دام إنساناً). This is a universal negative conditional.
5
Form the reductio syllogism
Combine the original universal with the contradictory. For the necessary version: minor: 'Every writer is a human necessarily', major: 'No human is a writer always as long as he is a human'. Conclusion: 'No writer is a writer necessarily' (or 'always' depending on modality used). This is absurd, proving the converse.
