Chapter: On Direct Conversion. Because when 'Every J is B' is true... This is a proof that those four simple [propositions] convert to an absolute particular. Explanation of that using matter: say, regarding the absolute necessary: because if 'Every human is an animal' is true necessarily, then it must be that 'Some animal is human' is true absolutely when it is an animal. Otherwise, its contradictory, a universal negative general conditional, would be true, namely: 'No animal is human always as long as it is an animal.' Then combine that contradictory with the original thus: 'Every human is an animal necessarily' and 'No animal is human always as long as it is an animal' yields 'No human is human necessarily', which is impossible, arising from the contradictory of the converse, so the converse is true. And similarly is said regarding the absolute perpetual, except that you replace 'necessarily' with 'always'. And you say regarding the general conditional: if 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer' is true necessarily, then it must be that 'Some mover of fingers is a writer' is true absolutely when he is a mover of fingers. Otherwise, its contradictory, a universal negative general conditional, would be true, namely: 'No mover of fingers is a writer always as long as he is a mover of fingers', and it yields 'No writer is a writer as long as he is a writer necessarily', which is impossible arising from the contradictory of the converse, so the converse is true. And similarly is said regarding the general conditional, except that you replace 'necessarily' with 'always'. And by this exposition, it is clear to you what is in the Sharh of omission of some modalities, if you are discerning. (His statement: 'Every J is B' etc.) It is apparent from what we have mentioned that 'J is B' in the proof of conversion of the two perpetuals is 'human is animal', and in the proof of conversion of the two general conditionals is 'writer...' etc. It is feasible to gather the four in one example with one proof, such as: 'necessarily or always every writer is a human' or 'as long as he is a writer', and its converse is 'some human is a writer absolutely when he is a human'. Its proof: if it were not true, its contradictory would be true, which is: 'No human is a writer always as long as he is a human'.
Source pages p. 701

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the rules of conversion ('aks mustawi) for modal propositions. The author explains how four types of universal affirmative propositions—the absolute necessary (al-dharuriyya al-mutlaqa), the absolute perpetual (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa), the general conditional (al-mashruta al-'amma), and the general conventional (al-'urfiyya al-'amma)—all convert to a particular absolute (hiniyya mutlaqa). The proof is given through reductio ad absurdum. For the absolute necessary, take the example 'Every human is an animal necessarily'. If the converse 'Some animal is human absolutely when it is an animal' were false, its contradictory 'No animal is human always as long as it is an animal' would be true. Combining this with the original as a syllogism (original as minor, contradictory as major) yields 'No human is human necessarily', which is absurd. Therefore the converse is true. The same method applies for the perpetual, replacing 'necessarily' with 'always', and for the general conditional, using the example of a writer and mover of fingers. The final paragraph notes that all four can be unified in one example with one proof, e.g., 'Every writer is a human necessarily/always/as long as he is a writer' converting to 'Some human is a writer absolutely when he is a human'. The commentary by Dasuqi and al-Attar clarifies the logical structure and warns about omissions in the earlier Sharh.

الضرورية المطلقة
الحينية المطلقة
الدائمة المطلقة
المشروطة العامة
العرفية العامة

All four universal modal propositions convert to the same particular absolute, and their proofs can be unified under a single reductio argument.

Proof of Conversion for Perpetual Propositions
Author proves conversion for الضرورة and الدائمة by contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio ad absurdum proof for the conversion of perpetual modal propositions (necessary and absolute perpetual) using a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Conversion of perpetuals is proven by contradiction, not by example.

In this passage, the author demonstrates a logical proof for the validity of conversion of propositions that are true at all times (perpetual). He uses a reductio method: assuming the converse is false leads to an absurdity via a syllogism, thereby establishing the converse as necessarily true.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and its target converse
Start with the original universal affirmative necessary proposition: 'Every human is animal necessarily'. The desired converse is a particular affirmative: 'Some animal is human actually while it is animal'.
The converse is conditioned by 'while it is animal' (al-hiniyya), which is the counterpart mode for perpetual propositions.
2
Assume the converse is false and state its contradictory
For the reductio, assume the converse is false. Then its contradictory must be true: a universal negative proposition with the mode 'always as long as it is animal': 'No animal is human always as long as it is animal'.
3
Combine the original and the contradictory in a syllogism
Use the original as the minor premise and the assumed contradictory as the major premise. The middle term is 'animal'. This yields a first-figure syllogism: minor (Every human is animal necessarily), major (No animal is human always as long as it is animal).
The minor is universal affirmative (A), the major is universal negative (E) — fitting the Celarent mood.
4
Derive the conclusion and recognize the absurdity
The syllogism validly concludes: 'No human is human necessarily'. This is impossible because it denies self-identity. The absurdity arises solely from the assumption that the converse is false.
The syllogism itself is formally valid; the contradiction is in the content, proving the assumption false.
5
Conclude that the original converse is true, and apply the same method to other perpetuals
Since assuming the falsehood of the converse leads to an absurdity, the converse must be true. The same proof works for the absolute perpetual proposition, simply replacing 'necessarily' with 'always'.
The proof is general for any perpetual mode.

Source Text

إذا صدق كُلُّ إنسان حيوان بالضَّرورة؛ وجب أن يصدق بعض الحيوان إنسان بالإطلاق حين هو حيوان، وإلا لصدق نقيضة سالبة كلية عُرفية عامة، وهي: لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان دائماً ما دام حيواناً. وتضم ذلك النقيض إلى الأصل هكذا: كلُّ إنسان حيوان بالضَّرورةِ، وَلا شيء مِنَ الحيوانِ بإنسان دائماً ما دامَ حيواناً، ينتج: لا شيء مِنَ الإنسان بإنسان بالضَّرورةِ، وهو مُحالٌ ناشئ مِن نقيض العكس، فالعكس حق. وكذا يُقالُ في الدَّائمةِ المطلقة، إلَّا أنَّك تبدلُ الضَّرورة بالدوام، فتقولُ بدل بالضرورة: دائماً.
If 'Every human is an animal necessarily' is true, then it must be true that 'Some animal is a human actually while it is an animal'; otherwise, its contradictory, a universal negative general proposition, would be true, which is: 'No animal is a human always as long as it is an animal.' Then you combine that contradictory with the original as follows: 'Every human is an animal necessarily' and 'No animal is a human always as long as it is an animal' yields 'No human is a human necessarily,' which is absurd, arising from the contradictory of the converse, so the converse is true. And similarly it is said regarding the absolute perpetual, except that you replace 'necessarily' with 'always', so you say instead of 'necessarily': 'always'.

Commentary
This passage explains the proof of conversion for the two 'perpetual' propositions: the necessary (al-daruriyya) and the absolute perpetual (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa). The author uses a reductio ad absurdum. First, he states the rule: if a universal affirmative is true with necessity, then a particular affirmative with the condition 'while it is B' (al-hiniyya) is true. The proof: assume the converse is false, then its contradictory (a universal negative with the mode 'always as long as it is B') is true. Combining this with the original premise in a syllogism (original as minor, contradictory as major) yields the absurd conclusion that something is not itself. This shows the assumption was false, so the converse is true. The same method applies to the absolute perpetual, with the mode changed from 'necessarily' to 'always'.

The Four Modalities in Simple Conversion and Their Proof by Contradiction
Definition of four modalities and a proof by contradiction for conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four modalities in simple conversion of categorical propositions and apply the conversion rule using a proof by contradiction.

Core Idea
Conversion preserves truth only when the modality is correctly specified; the four modalities unlock the rules.

Simple conversion is not merely swapping terms – the modality must be preserved or adjusted. The logical tradition identifies four distinct modalities that govern conversion, and the commentators provide a rigorous proof by contradiction to validate the rule. This lesson unpacks those modalities and the proof that guarantees their correctness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original categorical proposition and its modal specification
Look at the given proposition (e.g., 'Every human is animal') and note the attached modality: it could be necessary (ضرورة), perpetual (دائماً), or one of the two time-restricted modalities (ما دام ج with necessity or perpetuity). This is the first step to determine what converts to what.
The modalities are explained by al-Dasuqi: absolute necessary, absolute perpetual, general conditional (necessary as long as subject exists), general customary (always as long as subject exists).
2
State the required converse and its appropriate modality
For a universal affirmative original, the converse is a particular affirmative. Its modality is not the same as the original; it becomes absolute existential (حينية مطلقة) – i.e., 'Some animal is human' actually at the time it is animal. The commentator al-Attar explicitly gives this example.
The converse's modality is weaker: it asserts existence only at some moment, not with necessity or perpetuity.
3
Construct a proof by contradiction: assume the converse is false
If the converse (الحينية المطلقة) is false, then its contradictory – a universal negative general customary proposition (العرفية العامة) – must be true. For the human/animal example, that would be 'No animal is human as long as it is animal' (دائماً ما دام الحيوان حيواناً).
The contradictory of a particular affirmative (with any modality) is a universal negative with a complementary modality.
4
Combine original and contradictory in a first-figure syllogism
Take the original affirmative as the minor premise and the contradictory universal negative as the major premise. This yields a first-figure syllogism (with minor affirmative, major universal). The conclusion is 'No human is human' with the original modality, which is impossible (a thing cannot be other than itself).
Al-Dasuqi points out that the syllogism is in the first figure, requiring the minor to be affirmative and the major universal.
5
Conclude that the original assumption leads to absurdity, so the converse must be true
Since the supposition that the converse is false leads to a contradiction (self-negation), the converse must be true. This proves the validity of the conversion rule under the specified modalities.
This method is standard in logic: if the negation of a statement implies a contradiction, the statement itself is validated.

Chapter: On Simple Conversion with one of the four modalities, i.e., necessarily, or always, or as long as ج. Al-Dasuqi said: 'Moving the fingers' meaning: ج is a term for 'writer', and ب is a term for 'moving the fingers'. They only exemplified with letters rather than matters for two reasons: first, brevity; second, to repel the assumption of restriction to a particular matter. His saying: 'i.e., necessarily... etc.' is an explanation of the four modalities, and his saying: 'i.e., necessarily' means if you mean the absolute necessary, or 'always' if you mean the absolute perpetual. His saying: 'or as long as ج' means necessarily as long as ج if you mean the general conditional, and always as long as ج if you mean the general customary. By this it is known that the modality is the necessary restricted by as long as ج, or the perpetual restricted by as long as ج, not that the modality in the two general [propositions] is 'as long as ج' as is its apparent meaning. Al-Attar said: His saying: 'with one of the four modalities' for example: if 'Every human is animal' is true necessarily or always, then it must be true that 'Some animal is human' actually when it is animal, i.e., at some time of its being animal; otherwise, i.e., if this converse, which is the absolute existential, is not true, then its contradictory must be true, which is the general customary, namely our saying: 'No animal is human as long as animal is animal', and you combine it with the original thus: 'Every human is animal' necessarily or always, and 'No animal is human as long as animal is animal'; it results: 'No human is human' necessarily or always, which is the negation of a thing of itself. His saying: 'i.e., necessarily, or always, or as long as ج' is an explanation of the four modalities, and it is not hidden that the modality in the two general [propositions] is not merely 'as long as ج', and it is as if he conjoined it to an omitted [phrase] related to his saying: 'necessarily or always', its appreciation being 'necessarily or always in respect of essence or as long as ج'; this is what al-Isam said. And Abd al-Hakim said: His saying 'or as long as ج' intended by it the modality common between the two general [propositions], so it is a conjunction to his saying 'necessarily or always'. Then the meaning is... and you combine them to the mentioned principles, it results: 'No writer is a writer' necessarily or always or as long as he is a writer, which is impossible. End quote. Al-Dasuqi said concerning his saying: 'It must be true that some ب is ج when it is ب, otherwise nothing from ب is ج as long as ب' - from this the modality is omitted, i.e., the absolute restricted by the mentioned time, so it would have been better for him to say: 'It must be true that some (ب) is ج with absoluteness when it is (ب)'. And his saying: 'otherwise nothing' means: otherwise the truth of 'some (ب) is ج' etc. is necessary because its contradictory is true, which is a universal negative general customary saying: 'Nothing from (ب) is ج always as long as (ب)'. And the commentator omitted its modality, which is 'always', so he should say: 'So nothing from (ب) is ج always as long as (ب)'. His saying: 'to the original which is every (ج) is (ب)'. His saying: 'Thus every ج is ب with one of... etc.' He made the universal affirmative the minor and the universal negative the major because it is from the first figure, which stipulates that its minor be affirmative and its major universal, so ponder. His saying: 'And nothing from ب is ج' - the modality is omitted from it, and it is 'always'. His saying: 'or as long as ج' has what preceded, so do not neglect. Al-Attar said: With the two essential [modalities] as is common in usage, so what is said that it is a conjunction to an omitted 'essence' is committing what is not needed. His saying: 'It must be true that some ب is ج' because the necessary or perpetual predicate of the essence of the subject or of it according to the description of the subject inevitably is established when the description of the subject is established for the essence of the subject, so the absolute existential is true. (1) His saying: 'In it is what preceded regarding intending to include the necessary or perpetual, and the modality is not just 'as long as ج' alone; the opponent of the concept misunderstood that, so he objected to what did not occur to the mind of the commentator.'
Source pages p. 703

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) on the section dealing with simple conversion (al-ʿaks al-mustawī) in logic, specifically focusing on the four modalities (al-jihāt al-arbaʿ) that govern such conversion. The commentators, al-Dasuqi and al-ʿAttār, explain and correct the understanding of these modalities.

The main text states that conversion occurs with one of four modalities: necessary (ضرورة), always (دائماً), or as long as the subject exists (ما دام ج). Al-Dasuqi clarifies that the letters ج and ب are used as placeholders for terms (e.g., 'writer' and 'moving fingers') to avoid restriction to specific content. He then interprets the four modalities: 'necessarily' can refer to absolute necessary (al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa), 'always' to absolute perpetual (al-dāʾima al-muṭlaqa), 'as long as ج' with 'necessarily' yields the general conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma), and 'as long as ج' with 'always' yields the general customary (al-ʿurfiyya al-ʿāmma). Crucially, he emphasizes that the modality in the two general propositions is not just 'as long as ج' but includes the necessary or perpetual component restricted by 'as long as ج'.

Al-ʿAttār then provides an example with 'human' and 'animal'. If 'Every human is animal' is true with necessity or perpetuity, then its converse, 'Some animal is human' (at the time it is animal), must be true. If this converse is false, then its contradictory, 'No animal is human as long as it is animal', must be true. Combining this with the original statement yields 'No human is human' with necessity or perpetuity, which is impossible (salb al-shayʾ ʿan nafsihi). This is a proof by contradiction that validates the conversion rule: if the original is true with the appropriate modality, the converse must hold with the appropriate existential modality.

Subsequent commentary by al-Dasuqi notes that the modality in the converse should be explicitly stated (e.g., 'always as long as ب'). He also points out that the argument uses a syllogism in the first figure, with the universal affirmative as minor and universal negative as major. Al-ʿAttār further clarifies that the modality in the general propositions is a combination of necessity/perpetuity and the condition 'as long as the subject exists', not merely the condition alone.

Finally, a footnote warns against misunderstanding the modality as only 'as long as ج', which would lead to erroneous objections. This passage thus refines the technical understanding of conversion with modalities, a key concept in immediate inference.

Key Terms
الجهات الأربع
العكس المستوي
الحينية المطلقة
العرفية العامة
ما دام ج

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion with modalities depends on a precise understanding of the four modal types, and the conversion rule can be rigorously proved by contradiction using a first-figure syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Simple Conversion of Special Modal Propositions
Author defines simple conversion and explains how special conditioned and conventional propositions convert.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a special conditioned or conventional proposition into its corresponding momentary absolute not permanent form and verify the conversion by reductio.

Core Idea
Special modal propositions convert into momentary non-permanent compounds.

In this lesson, we explore how specially conditioned (mashruta khassa) and special conventional ('urfiyya khassa) universal affirmatives transform through simple conversion. The result is not a simple absolute but a compound proposition that affirms temporally and denies permanently. We will follow the logical proof provided by the commentators to understand why this conversion holds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is mashruta khassa (necessity with duration condition) or 'urfiyya khassa (perpetuity with duration condition). Both are universal affirmatives restricted to the duration of the subject's description.
2
State the conversion rule
Both types convert into a 'momentary absolute not permanent' (hiniyya mutlaqa la da'ima). That is, the converse affirms the predicate at some time when the subject has its description, but denies that it holds always.
The converse is a compound of an affirmative temporal and a negative general absolute.
3
Decompose the converse proposition
The converse is 'Some P is S when it is P, but not always.' This means there is a moment when the predicate applies, but it is not permanent. The result is not a simple absolute but a conjunction of a particular affirmative temporal and a universal negative general.
The negative part 'not always' is essential to avoid inferring permanence.
4
Verify with an example
Apply the rule to a concrete statement: If 'Every writer is moving fingers as long as he is a writer' (with necessity but not always) is true, then the converse is 'Some moving fingers is a writer when it is moving fingers, but not always.'
The example uses 'writer' and 'moving fingers' as per the text.
5
Understand the reductio proof
Assume the converse is false; then its contradictory would be true, which would force a conjunction of contradictories from the original proposition. This shows the conversion is valid.
This proof establishes that the conversion preserves truth under the modal restrictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي وهو محال ناشئ عن نقيض العكس، فالعكس حقٌّ . (وَ) تنعكس المشروطة والعرفيّةُ (الخَاصَّتَانِ حِينِيَّةٌ) مطلقةٌ (لَا دَائِمَةٌ)، لأنه إذا صدق . الدسوقي: (قَوْلُهُ : وَهُوَ) أي : ما ذكر من النتيجة (محال) : أي : لأَنَّ فيه سَلْب الشَّيء عن نَفْسِه (قَوْلُهُ : الْمَشْرُوطَةُ) هي من الضروريات . (قَوْلُهُ : وَالْعُرْفِيَّةُ) هي مِنَ الدَّوائم . (قَوْلُهُ : وَتَنْعَكِسُ الْخَاصَّتَانِ ... إِلَخ) هذا شروعٌ في عكس المركبات والأربعةِ : المتقدّمة في البسائط، وبقي منها أربعة وهي الوقتية المطلقة، والمنتشرة المطلقة، والمطلقه العامَّةُ والممكنة، وسيأتي الكلام منه على بعضها . (قَوْلُهُ : حِيْنِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ لَا دَائِمَةٌ) وهي ما حُكِمَ فيها بفعليَّةِ النِّسبة في بعض أوقاتِ الوصف، وقيد ذلكَ بِاللادوامِ الذَّات، فهي مُركَّبَةٌ مِنْ مُطلقةٍ حينيَّةٍ، وَمُطلقةٍ عامَّةٍ، إحداهُمَا مُوجبةٌ، وَالأَخرَى سالبة. (قَوْلُهُ : لِأَنَّهُ إِذَا صَدَقَ . . . إِلَخ) وبيان ذلكَ بالموادِّ في دليل عكس المشروطة الخاصَّةِ (1) أنْ تقول : لأنَّهُ إذا صدق بالضَّرورة : كلَّ كاتبٍ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع ما دامَ . العطار: (قَوْلُهُ : حِيْنِيَّةٌ لَا دَائِمَةٌ) وَهِيَ الحينيَّةُ المطلقة مع قيدِ اللادوامِ الذَّاتي. (قَوْلُهُ : لِأَنَّهُ إِذَا صَدَقَ) إلى قولِهِ : (فَيلزَمُ اجتماعُ النَّقيضَيْن)؛ تَوضِيحُهُ أَنَّا إِذا فرضنا بالضَّرورة أو دائماً : كُلُّ كاتب مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع ما دامَ كاتباً لا دائماً ; وجبَ أن يصدق : بعضُ مُتحرِّكِ الأصابع كاتب حينَ هُوَ مُتحرِّكُ الأصابع لا دائماً، أمَّا الحينيَّةُ المطلقةُ ; وَهِيَ الجزء الأوَّلُ مِنَ العكس; فَلِكَونِهَا لازمةٌ للمشروطة العامَّةِ
Chapter: On Simple Conversion. It is impossible arising from the contradictory of the converse, so the conversion is true. And the two special (conditioned and conventional) convert into a qualified momentary absolute (not permanent), because if it is true... Dasuqi: (His saying: 'It is impossible') i.e., what is mentioned from the result (impossible): that is, because it involves negating a thing of itself. (His saying: 'The conditioned') it is from the necessary propositions. (His saying: 'And the conventional') it is from the perpetual propositions. (His saying: 'And the two special convert... etc.') This is the start of conversion of compound propositions and the four: the ones mentioned in the simple ones, and four remain: the absolute temporal, the absolute extensive, the absolute general, and the possible, and discussion of some will come. (His saying: 'Momentary absolute not permanent') it is that in which the actuality of the relation is judged in some times of the description, and it is restricted by the essential non-permanence, so it is composed of an absolute momentary and an absolute general, one affirmative and the other negative. (His saying: 'Because if it is true... etc.') And its explanation by examples in the proof of the conversion of the special conditioned: that is, because if it is true by necessity: every writer is moving fingers as long as... 'Attar: (His saying: 'Momentary not permanent') it is the absolute temporal with the restriction of essential non-permanence. (His saying: 'Because if it is true') up to his saying: ('then the conjunction of two contradictories follows'); its clarification: that if we assume by necessity or always: every writer is moving fingers as long as he is a writer not always; then it must be true: some moving fingers is a writer when it is moving fingers not always. As for the absolute temporal, which is the first part of the converse; it is because it is necessary for the general conventional.
Source pages p. 705

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) and specifically addresses the conversion of special modal propositions: the special conditioned (mashruta khassa) and special conventional ('urfiyya khassa). The rule is that each converts into a 'momentary absolute not permanent' (hiniyya mutlaqa la da'ima), i.e., a proposition affirming the predicate at some time when the subject has the description, but not permanently. Dasuqi explains that the 'conditioned' is from the necessary class, and the 'conventional' from the perpetual class. He then outlines the broader context: there are eight types of modal propositions in simple conversion; four were previously discussed, and four remain (absolute temporal, absolute extensive, general absolute, possible). The definition of 'momentary absolute not permanent' is given: a compound of an affirmative temporal moment and a negative general absolute, i.e., 'some subject is predicate when it is such, but not always.' 'Attar clarifies that the first part of the converse (the affirmative momentary) is necessary for the general conventional when the original is of that type. The proof uses an example: if necessarily 'every writer is moving fingers as long as he is a writer' (but not always), then it converts to 'some moving fingers is a writer when it is moving fingers' (but not always). This is illustrated by a reductio argument: if the converse were false, a contradiction would follow.

Key Terms
عكس مستوي
مشروطة خاصة
عرفية خاصة
حينية مطلقة لا دائمة

Conversion of Special Conditioned and Conventional Propositions
Author states the conversion rule for two special modal propositions and proves it via syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conversion rule for special modal propositions and reconstruct the syllogistic proof that justifies it.

Core Idea
Special conditioned propositions convert to temporal absolute not perpetual, proven by syllogistic contradiction.

In this passage, the author presents a crucial rule of immediate inference: the conversion of two special modal propositions. This rule is not merely stated; it is proved using a sophisticated syllogistic argument. By following this lesson, you will learn both the rule and the reasoning behind it, enabling you to apply similar conversions in Islamic logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition type
Determine if the proposition is a special conditioned (mashrutah khassah) or special conventional ('urfiyyah khassah) modal. Look for the condition 'as long as it exists' and the qualifier 'not perpetually'.
2
State the conversion rule
The author claims that such a proposition converts to a temporal absolute (hiniyyah mutlaqah) that is not perpetual (la da'imah). The convert becomes a particular affirmative (some S is P while it is S, not perpetually).
3
Verify the temporal absolute part
Understand why the convert must be true at some time: because the original is a special case of a general conditioned, and the general conditioned necessarily implies the temporal absolute. Use the principle that the consequence of the general is a consequence of the special.
4
Verify the 'not perpetual' part via contradiction
Assume the 'not perpetual' is false (i.e., it is perpetual). Then its contradictory (a universal perpetual affirmative) would be true. Combine this with the original to derive contradictory conclusions via two categorical syllogisms in the first figure. Observe that the results conflict, so the assumption is false.
5
Reconstruct the syllogistic proof
Write out the two syllogisms explicitly: Premises from the original and the false assumption, and show how each step leads to a contradiction, thereby proving the conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي. وَهُوَ مَحَالٌ نَاشِئٌ عَنْ نَقِيضِ الْعَكْسِ، فَالْعَكْسُ حَقٌّ. (وَ) تَنْعَكِسُ الْمَشْرُوطَةُ وَالْعُرْفِيَّةُ الْخَاصَّتَانِ حِينِيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ لَا دَائِمَةٌ، لِأَنَّهُ إِذَا صَدَقَ بِالضَّرُورَةِ: كُلَّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الْأَصَابِعِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِبًا لَا دَائِمًا؛ صَدَقَ: بَعْضُ مُتَحَرِّكِ الْأَصَابِعِ كَاتِبٌ حِينَ هُوَ مُتَحَرِّكُ الْأَصَابِعِ لَا دَائِمًا. أَمَّا صِدْقُ الْحِينِيَّةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ فَلِكَوْنِهَا لَازِمَةً لِلْمَشْرُوطَةِ الْعَامَّةِ، وَلَازِمُ الْعَامِّ لَازِمٌ لِلْخَاصِّ. وَأَمَّا صِدْقُ لَا دَائِمًا فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ كَذَبَ لَصَدَقَ نَقِيضُهُ مُوجِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ مُطْلَقَةٌ دَائِمَةٌ: كُلُّ مُتَحَرِّكِ الْأَصَابِعِ كَاتِبٌ دَائِمًا، فَتُضَمُّ ذَلِكَ النَّقِيضَ إِلَى الْجُزْءِ الْأَوَّلِ مِنَ الْأَصْلِ فِي شَكْلٍ أَوَّلَ فَتَنْتِجُ: كُلُّ مُتَحَرِّكِ الْأَصَابِعِ مُتَحَرِّكُ الْأَصَابِعِ دَائِمًا، ثُمَّ تُضَمُّهُ إِلَى الْجُزْءِ الثَّانِي فَتَنْتِجُ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْمُتَحَرِّكِ الْأَصَابِعِ بِمُتَحَرِّكِ الْأَصَابِعِ بِالْإِطْلَاقِ، فَتَنَافَتِ النَّتِيجَتَانِ.
Chapter on simple conversion. It is impossible, arising from the contradictory of the convert, so the convert is true. And the two special [propositions], the conditioned and the conventional, convert to a temporal absolute that is not perpetual. Because if it is true by necessity: 'Every writer is moving fingers as long as he is a writer, not perpetually', then it is true: 'Some moving fingers is a writer while it is moving fingers, not perpetually'. As for the truth of the temporal absolute, it is because it is a necessary consequence of the general conditioned, and the consequence of the general is a consequence of the special. And as for the truth of 'not perpetually', it is because if it were false, then its contradictory, a universal affirmative perpetual absolute, would be true: 'Every moving fingers is a writer perpetually'. Then you combine that contradictory with the first part of the original in the first figure, resulting in: 'Every moving fingers is moving fingers perpetually'. Then you combine it with the second part, resulting in: 'Nothing of the moving fingers is moving fingers absolutely'. So the two results are contradictory.
Source pages p. 705

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof that two special modal propositions—the special conditioned (mashrutah khassah) and the special conventional ('urfiyyah khassah)—convert to a temporal absolute (hiniyyah mutlaqah) that is qualified as 'not perpetual' (la da'imah). The author begins by stating the conversion rule directly. Then he provides a proof using an example: the premise 'Every writer is finger-moving as long as he is a writer, not perpetually' (which is a special conditioned proposition) is assumed true. Its convert is 'Some finger-moving being is a writer while it is finger-moving, not perpetually'. The proof has two parts: first, the temporal absolute part ('some finger-moving is a writer while it is finger-moving') is true because it follows from a more general proposition (the general conditioned) that is itself a necessary consequence of the original. Since the consequence of the general is also a consequence of the special, the temporal absolute is guaranteed. Second, the 'not perpetually' part is proved by contradiction. If it were false, then its contradictory, a universal perpetual affirmative ('Every finger-moving is a writer perpetually'), would be true. Combining this with the first part of the original ('Every writer is finger-moving as long as he is a writer') in a first-figure categorical syllogism yields 'Every finger-moving is finger-moving perpetually'. Then combining the same false proposition with the second part of the original (implied: 'Nothing of the writer is finger-moving absolutely') yields 'Nothing of the finger-moving is finger-moving absolutely'. These two results conflict, showing the assumption is impossible. Thus the 'not perpetually' part must be true. The proof relies on the principle that if a proposition's contradictory leads to contradictory consequences, the original is true. The commentary clarifies the logical structure using the standard elements of conversion and syllogism.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي (al-'aks al-mustawi): Simple conversion: swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth, with the condition that the original proposition does not change in quality (affirmative/negative) and that the subject term exists. [Note: In some texts, 'aks includes contraposition; here it is restricted to simple conversion.]
المشروطة الخاصة (al-mashrutah al-khassah): A universal proposition stating that every subject is predicate as long as the subject exists, but not always; e.g., 'Every writer is finger-moving as long as he is a writer'. [Note: Stable term in modal logic.]
العرفية الخاصة (al-'urfiyyah al-khassah): Similar to the conditioned but using conventional (not necessary) connection; e.g., 'Every barber is shaving as long as he is a barber'. [Note: Less common; used alongside mashrutah.]
حينية مطلقة (hiniyyah mutlaqah): A proposition asserting that the predicate actually holds of the subject at some time during its existence; e.g., 'Some finger-moving is a writer while it is finger-moving'. [Note: Stable term for temporal absolute.]
لا دائمة (la da'imah): The negation of perpetuity: the proposition is not true at all times; i.e., there is some time when the predicate does not hold. [Note: Stable term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) No sincere person is a mu'min.

Key Takeaway
Special conditioned and conventional propositions convert to a temporal absolute not perpetual, and this is proven by a syllogistic contradiction.

Simple Conversion for Modal Propositions: The Rule
Defines simple conversion for modal propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule of simple conversion for the particular conditioned and particular customary propositions, and identify the correct temporal modifiers in the converse.

Core Idea
Simple conversion of modal propositions swaps subject and predicate while adjusting the temporal condition.

In this passage, the author defines simple conversion for modal propositions like 'every J is B as long as it is J, not always'. The conversion yields 'some B is J, when it is B, not always'. This rule is essential for understanding how modal propositions behave in logical inference, especially in theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition's type
Determine whether the proposition is particular conditioned (al-mashruta al-khassa) or particular customary (al-'urfiyya al-khassa). The former uses 'by necessity', the latter 'always'.
The passage gives two variants: 'by necessity' for conditioned, 'always' for customary.
2
Note the temporal modifier and the 'not always' qualifier
The original proposition has the form 'every J is B as long as it is J, not always'. The modifier 'as long as it is J' indicates the condition; 'not always' indicates the proposition is not perpetually true.
The 'not always' qualifier is crucial; it prevents the converse from being universally true.
3
Apply the conversion rule
Swap the subject (J) and predicate (B), change the temporal condition to 'when it is B', and retain the 'not always' qualifier. The converse becomes 'some B is J, when it is B, not always'.
The converse is a particular affirmative with a conditional temporal modifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي

بالضرورة، أو دائماً كلُّ ج ب ما دام ج لا دائماً»؛ صَدَقَ «بعض ب ج، حين هوب، لا دائماً».

الدسوقي: ويلزم من ذلك أنَّ مُتحرِّكَ الأصابع متحرك الأصابع لا متحرك الأصابع، وهو محال ناشى مِن نقيض الجزء الثاني من العكس، فيكون الجزء الثاني من العكس صادقاً.

العطار: وَكَذا يُقالُ في دليل عكس العرفية الخاصَّةِ، إِلَّا أَنَّكَ تبدلُ الضَّرورة بِالدَّوامِ، فتقولُ بدل قولنا بالضَّرورة دائماً ، فتأمل.

(قَوْلُهُ: بِالضَّرُورَةِ) أي : إن أردتَ المشروطة الخاصة، وقوله: (أو دائماً)؛ أي: إن أردتَ العرفية الخاصة .

(قَوْلُهُ : ج ب) ظهرَ مِمَّا قرَّرْنَا : أَنَّ (ج) في جميع الدليل عبارة عن كاتب مثلاً ، وأنَّ (ب) في جميعِ الدَّليلِ عبارة عن متحرّك الأصابع مثلاً، وقوله : أولا (۱) : لا دائماً ؛ أي: لا شيء مِنَ الكاتب بمتحرِّك الأصابع بالإطلاق، وقولُه ثانياً : لا دائماً ؛ أي: بعضُ مُتحرِّكِ الأصابع ليس بكاتب بالإطلاق.

العطار: الجزء الثاني منهُما وتقولُ : كُلُّ مُتحرِّكِ الأصابع كاتب دائماً، وَلَا شيء مِنَ الكاتبِ يمتحرِّكِ الأصابع بالفعل، ينتجُ : لا شيء مِن متحرّك الأصابع بمتحرك الأصابع بالفعل، فيلزمُ اجتماعُ النَّقيضَيْنِ ، قال المحشّي : وإنَّما ضُمَّتْ لِكُلِّ مِنَ الجزأين ؛ لأنَّ العكس قضيَّةٌ مُركَّبَةٌ مِن جزأين لازمةٌ لمثلها ، والمركَّبُ اللَّازِمُ لِمركَّب يلزمُ أَنَّ كُلًّا مِن جزأيهِ لازمٌ لِكُلِّ مِنْ جزاي ملزومِهِ .ا.هـ. وليس بشيءٍ؛ إذْ مِنَ المركَّبَاتِ مَا ينعكس إلى بسيطةٍ كَما سيأتي في كلامِهِ، وقال البعضُ : لم يكتفِ بالضَّمِّ الأَوَّلِ معَ ينتجُ سَلْبُ الشَّيْء عَن نفسِهِ لِكُونِ ذلكَ السَّلْبِ ممنوع الاستحالة في المطلقة؛ لِكَونِ مَعناه سلبَ الوصْفِ المفارقِ في الجملةِ كَقولِنَا : لَا شَيءٍ مِنَ الصَّاحِكِ يضاحك بالإطلاق العام .ا.هـ. وليس بشيء أيضاً ؛ لأنَّهُ ليسَ في الضَّمِّ الأَوَّلِ سَلْبُ (۱) (قَوْلُهُ : وقوله : أولاً ... إلخ) لأنَّ اللادوام الأوّل صدره كلّي، والثاني صدره جزئي، وهو يوافق الصدر في الكم ويخالفه في الكيف كما سبق. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
Chapter on Simple Conversion

"By necessity, or always, every J is B as long as it is J, not always" — it is true that "some B is J, when it is B, not always".

Al-Dusuqi: It follows from that that a mover of fingers is a mover of fingers not a mover of fingers, which is impossible arising from the contradictory of the second part of the converse, so the second part of the converse is true.

Al-Attar: And similarly it is said in the proof of the converse of the particular customary, except that you replace necessity with always, so you say instead of our saying "by necessity" "always", so reflect.

His saying: "by necessity" means if you intend the particular conditioned, and his saying: "or always" means if you intend the particular customary.

His saying: "J B" appears from what we have established: that (J) in the entire proof is an expression for e.g. a writer, and that (B) in the entire proof is an expression for e.g. a mover of fingers. And his saying: first: "not always" means: nothing of the writer is a mover of fingers absolutely. And his saying: second: "not always" means: some movers of fingers are not writers absolutely.

Al-Attar: The second part of them, and you say: Every mover of fingers is a writer always, and nothing of the writer is a mover of fingers actually, produces: nothing of the mover of fingers is a mover of fingers actually, so a conjunction of two contradictories follows. The commentator said: "It is combined for each of the two parts because the converse is a compound proposition composed of two parts, necessary for its like, and the necessary compound for a compound necessitates that each of its two parts is necessary for each of the parts of its necessitor." End quote. And it is nothing; for among compounds there are those that convert to a simple, as will come in his speech. And some said: "He did not suffice with the first combination because the negation of a thing about itself is not impossible in the absolute proposition because its meaning is the negation of a separable property in general, like our saying: Nothing of the laugher laughs absolutely." End quote. And it is also nothing; because in the first combination there is not a negation of (1) (His saying: and his saying: first... etc.) because the first not-always has a universal premise, and the second has a particular premise, and it agrees with the premise in quantity and differs in quality as previously. End quote. Al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 707

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the rule of simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) for certain modal propositions. It begins with the statement: If the proposition 'By necessity, or always, every J is B as long as it is J' is true but not always (i.e., the universal affirmative is true conditionally but not perpetually), then its converse 'Some B is J, when it is B, not always' is true. Al-Dusuqi explains that this follows because denying the converse leads to a contradiction: assuming the second part of the converse is false forces the absurd conclusion that a thing is not itself. Al-Attar notes that the same proof applies to the particular customary proposition by substituting 'always' for 'necessity'. Then the text clarifies the variables: J stands for a term like 'writer', B for 'mover of fingers'. The first 'not always' means 'no writer is a mover of fingers absolutely', the second means 'some mover of fingers is not a writer absolutely'. Al-Attar continues the proof by constructing a syllogism: from 'Every mover of fingers is a writer always' and 'No writer is a mover of fingers actually', we deduce 'No mover of fingers is a mover of fingers actually', which contradicts the first result. This proves the converse. Finally, al-Attar critiques a commentator's view that the proof requires combining each part separately, arguing that some compound propositions convert to simple ones, and that the first combination alone is insufficient because negating a thing of itself is not impossible in absolute statements. He supports his critique with an example: 'Nothing of the laugher laughs absolutely' is not contradictory. The discussion highlights the precision needed in modal conversion proofs.

بالضرورة
دائماً
لا دائماً
المشروطة الخاصة
العرفية الخاصة

Proof of Modal Conversion by Contradiction
Provides a proof by contradiction for simple conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof by contradiction that validates simple conversion for modal propositions, and identify the role of the categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
The truth of the converse is proven by showing that denying it leads to a self-contradiction.

After stating the conversion rule, the author proves it using a categorical syllogism that generates a contradiction if the converse is false. Al-Dusuqi and al-Attar explain the reasoning, and al-Attar critiques a commentator's view on the proof structure. This lesson walks through the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Assume the second part of the converse is false
The converse has two parts: 'Some B is J, when it is B' and 'not always'. Denying the second part means asserting 'always' instead. For the particular customary, we assume 'Every mover of fingers is a writer always' (true) and 'No writer is a mover of fingers actually' (true) as premises.
These premises come from the contradictory of the converse's second part.
2
Combine premises into a categorical syllogism
Take the two premises: 'Every mover of fingers is a writer always' (universal affirmative) and 'No writer is a mover of fingers actually' (universal negative). This forms a first-figure categorical syllogism.
The middle term is 'writer', connecting the two premises.
3
Derive the conclusion
From the premises, the conclusion logically follows: 'No mover of fingers is a mover of fingers actually' – a self-negation.
This conclusion is a universal negative saying that nothing is itself.
4
Recognize the contradiction
The conclusion contradicts the first assumption (that the converse is false) because it asserts that a thing is not itself, which is impossible. Therefore, the original assumption must be false, proving the converse true.
Al-Attar notes that some commentators argue about the necessity of combining both parts, but the core contradiction suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي

بالضرورة، أو دائماً كلُّ ج ب ما دام ج لا دائماً»؛ صَدَقَ «بعض ب ج، حين هوب، لا دائماً».

الدسوقي: ويلزم من ذلك أنَّ مُتحرِّكَ الأصابع متحرك الأصابع لا متحرك الأصابع، وهو محال ناشى مِن نقيض الجزء الثاني من العكس، فيكون الجزء الثاني من العكس صادقاً.

العطار: وَكَذا يُقالُ في دليل عكس العرفية الخاصَّةِ، إِلَّا أَنَّكَ تبدلُ الضَّرورة بِالدَّوامِ، فتقولُ بدل قولنا بالضَّرورة دائماً ، فتأمل.

(قَوْلُهُ: بِالضَّرُورَةِ) أي : إن أردتَ المشروطة الخاصة، وقوله: (أو دائماً)؛ أي: إن أردتَ العرفية الخاصة .

(قَوْلُهُ : ج ب) ظهرَ مِمَّا قرَّرْنَا : أَنَّ (ج) في جميع الدليل عبارة عن كاتب مثلاً ، وأنَّ (ب) في جميعِ الدَّليلِ عبارة عن متحرّك الأصابع مثلاً، وقوله : أولا (۱) : لا دائماً ؛ أي: لا شيء مِنَ الكاتب بمتحرِّك الأصابع بالإطلاق، وقولُه ثانياً : لا دائماً ؛ أي: بعضُ مُتحرِّكِ الأصابع ليس بكاتب بالإطلاق.

العطار: الجزء الثاني منهُما وتقولُ : كُلُّ مُتحرِّكِ الأصابع كاتب دائماً، وَلَا شيء مِنَ الكاتبِ يمتحرِّكِ الأصابع بالفعل، ينتجُ : لا شيء مِن متحرّك الأصابع بمتحرك الأصابع بالفعل، فيلزمُ اجتماعُ النَّقيضَيْنِ ، قال المحشّي : وإنَّما ضُمَّتْ لِكُلِّ مِنَ الجزأين ؛ لأنَّ العكس قضيَّةٌ مُركَّبَةٌ مِن جزأين لازمةٌ لمثلها ، والمركَّبُ اللَّازِمُ لِمركَّب يلزمُ أَنَّ كُلًّا مِن جزأيهِ لازمٌ لِكُلِّ مِنْ جزاي ملزومِهِ .ا.هـ. وليس بشيءٍ؛ إذْ مِنَ المركَّبَاتِ مَا ينعكس إلى بسيطةٍ كَما سيأتي في كلامِهِ، وقال البعضُ : لم يكتفِ بالضَّمِّ الأَوَّلِ معَ ينتجُ سَلْبُ الشَّيْء عَن نفسِهِ لِكُونِ ذلكَ السَّلْبِ ممنوع الاستحالة في المطلقة؛ لِكَونِ مَعناه سلبَ الوصْفِ المفارقِ في الجملةِ كَقولِنَا : لَا شَيءٍ مِنَ الصَّاحِكِ يضاحك بالإطلاق العام .ا.هـ. وليس بشيء أيضاً ؛ لأنَّهُ ليسَ في الضَّمِّ الأَوَّلِ سَلْبُ (۱) (قَوْلُهُ : وقوله : أولاً ... إلخ) لأنَّ اللادوام الأوّل صدره كلّي، والثاني صدره جزئي، وهو يوافق الصدر في الكم ويخالفه في الكيف كما سبق. ا.هـ. الشرنوبي.
Chapter on Simple Conversion

"By necessity, or always, every J is B as long as it is J, not always" — it is true that "some B is J, when it is B, not always".

Al-Dusuqi: It follows from that that a mover of fingers is a mover of fingers not a mover of fingers, which is impossible arising from the contradictory of the second part of the converse, so the second part of the converse is true.

Al-Attar: And similarly it is said in the proof of the converse of the particular customary, except that you replace necessity with always, so you say instead of our saying "by necessity" "always", so reflect.

His saying: "by necessity" means if you intend the particular conditioned, and his saying: "or always" means if you intend the particular customary.

His saying: "J B" appears from what we have established: that (J) in the entire proof is an expression for e.g. a writer, and that (B) in the entire proof is an expression for e.g. a mover of fingers. And his saying: first: "not always" means: nothing of the writer is a mover of fingers absolutely. And his saying: second: "not always" means: some movers of fingers are not writers absolutely.

Al-Attar: The second part of them, and you say: Every mover of fingers is a writer always, and nothing of the writer is a mover of fingers actually, produces: nothing of the mover of fingers is a mover of fingers actually, so a conjunction of two contradictories follows. The commentator said: "It is combined for each of the two parts because the converse is a compound proposition composed of two parts, necessary for its like, and the necessary compound for a compound necessitates that each of its two parts is necessary for each of the parts of its necessitor." End quote. And it is nothing; for among compounds there are those that convert to a simple, as will come in his speech. And some said: "He did not suffice with the first combination because the negation of a thing about itself is not impossible in the absolute proposition because its meaning is the negation of a separable property in general, like our saying: Nothing of the laugher laughs absolutely." End quote. And it is also nothing; because in the first combination there is not a negation of (1) (His saying: and his saying: first... etc.) because the first not-always has a universal premise, and the second has a particular premise, and it agrees with the premise in quantity and differs in quality as previously. End quote. Al-Sharnubi.

Proof of Conversion for Conditional Propositions
Author states conversion rule and proves it via contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the converse of a conditional proposition and verify its truth using a contradiction proof.

Core Idea
Conversion of a conditional proposition changes its modal condition, but the proof via contradiction secures its truth.

In this lesson, we examine how to convert conditional propositions—those that hold under a condition—and prove the validity of the conversion. The author of the matn presents a rule, and al-Dasuqi's commentary provides a crisp proof by contradiction. You will learn to apply this proof method to any conditional or common proposition in Islamic logical contexts.

Guided Path
1
State the original proposition
The original proposition is 'Every J is B, as long as it is J, not always'. This is a particular conditional affirmative (mashrutat khassa). Identify the subject (J), predicate (B), condition (as long as it is J), and the modal qualifier (not always).
J and B are variables representing classes. In Islamic logic, these can be replaced by real terms.
2
Formulate the converse
The converse asserts: 'Some B is J, when it is B, not always'. The subject and predicate swap, the quantity changes from universal to particular, and the condition now attaches to the new subject (B).
Notice the modal qualifier remains 'not always' but now applies to the predicate.
3
Assume the negation of the second part of the converse
The second part of the converse is 'when it is B, not always'. Assume this is false. That would mean that whenever something is B, it is always J (i.e., the condition becomes perpetual). This yields a contradiction with the original proposition.
The original says 'not always' for the condition, so assuming 'always' contradicts it.
4
Derive the contradiction using a concrete example
Al-Dasuqi uses the example 'mover of fingers'. Applying the assumption leads to: 'A mover of fingers is a mover of fingers is not a mover of fingers', which is logically impossible. Therefore the assumption is false, and the second part of the converse is true.
The example shows that denying the converse leads to an absurdity, thus confirming the converse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي. بالضرورة، أو دائماً كلُّ ج ب ما دام ج لا دائماً»؛ صَدَقَ «بعض ب ج، حين هوب، لا دائماً». قال الدسوقي: ويلزم من ذلك أنَّ مُتحرِّكَ الأصابع متحرك الأصابع لا متحرك الأصابع، وهو محال ناشى مِن نقيض الجزء الثاني من العكس، فيكون الجزء الثاني من العكس صادقاً. وَكذا يُقالُ في دليل عكس العرفية الخاصَّةِ، إِلَّا أَنَّكَ تبدلُ الضَّرورة بِالدَّوامِ، فتقولُ بدل قولنا بالضَّرورة دائماً، فتأمل.
Chapter: On Simple Conversion. By necessity or always: 'Every J is B, as long as J is not always' is true; 'Some B is J, when it is B, not always' is true. Al-Dasuqi said: And it follows from that that 'a mover of fingers is a mover of fingers is not a mover of fingers,' which is impossible, arising from the contradictory of the second part of the converse; thus the second part of the converse is true. And similarly it is said in the proof of the converse of the particular common proposition, except that you substitute 'always' for 'necessity', and you say instead of our statement 'by necessity' 'always', so reflect.
Source pages p. 707

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the conversion rule for conditional (mashrutat khassa) and particular common ('urfiyya khassa) propositions. The author first states the rule: given a proposition like 'Every J is B, as long as it is J, not always' (i.e., a particular conditional affirmative), its converse is 'Some B is J, when it is B, not always'. Then al-Dasuqi explains the proof by contradiction: if we assume the second part of the converse is false, we derive that 'A mover of fingers is a mover of fingers is not a mover of fingers', which is logically impossible. Therefore, the assumption is false, and the second part must be true. The same reasoning applies to the 'urfiyya khassa, but with 'always' instead of 'by necessity'. The student should note the use of a concrete example (mover of fingers) to illustrate the abstract logical form.

المشروطة الخاصة
العرفية الخاصة
الضرورة
الدوام

B) A contradiction
C) A tautology
D) An immediate inference

Key Takeaway
Conversion of a conditional proposition is valid and can be proven by contradiction: denying the converse leads to an absurdity, affirming its truth.

Defining Temporal Modalities: al-Hiniyya and al-Ladawam
Defines temporal modalities and addresses conversion objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-hiniyya al-mutlaqa and al-ladawam, and determine the quantity of their converse when subordinate.

Core Idea
Temporal modalities depend on whether the predicate holds at the subject's existence or permanently.

In this passage from Dasooqi's commentary on Tadhheeb al-Mantiq, two key temporal modalities are defined: al-hiniyya al-mutlaqa (absolute temporal) and al-ladawam (non-permanence). These are crucial for understanding how propositions behave in conversion (al-‘aks). You will learn their definitions, their logical relations, and how to handle an apparent paradox about their quantity when converted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of al-hiniyya al-mutlaqa
Locate the phrase in the Arabic that defines al-hiniyya al-mutlaqa. It is given as 'بعض ب ج، حين هو ب' meaning 'Some B is J while it is B'. This means the predicate holds only during the existence of the subject.
This modality is often omitted for brevity, as noted by the commentator.
2
Identify the definition of al-ladawam
Find the definition of al-ladawam: 'بعض ب ليس ج بالإطلاق' meaning 'Some B is not J absolutely'. This is a permanent negation, without temporal restriction.
The passage explains its falsehood would imply a universal affirmative permanent.
3
Understand the necessity relation
The author states that al-hiniyya is necessary for the conditional and general conventional propositions. The analogy of animal and human shows that what is necessary for the general is also necessary for the specific.
4
Analyze the objection about conversion quantity
One might object that al-ladawam, as a restriction on a universal affirmative, should convert to a universal negative (since universal negatives convert universally). Understand why this seems logical.
The objection is based on the general rule of conversion.
5
Follow the answer and its critique
Dasooqi answers that the universal negative conversion only applies when the proposition is independent. Here, al-ladawam is subordinate to a universal premise, so it converts to a particular negative. Al-Attar critiques that the quantity follows the premise, not subordination.
The correct answer: al-ladawam in the original is universal, in the converse becomes particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحينية المطلقة، وهي: بعض ب ج، حين هو ب؛ فلكونها لازمة للمشروطة والعرفية العامتين، ولازم العامتين لازم الخاصتين. وأما اللادوام وهو: بعض ب ليس ج بالإطلاق؛ فلأنه لو كذب؛ لصدق: كل ب ج دائماً. (قوله: أما الحيينة) أي: أما صدق الحينية. وقوله: (بعض ب ج حين؛ أي: بعض (ب) ج بالإطلاق حين ... إلخ، فحذف جهتها، وكذا يقال في قوله قبل: صدق بعض (ب ج) ... إلخ). (قوله: ولازم العامتين ... إلخ) وذلك كما في الحيوان والإنسان، فإن اللازم للحيوان كالتحرك لازم للإنسان. (قوله: وأما اللادوام) أي: وأما صدق اللادوام في قضية العكس. (قوله: وهو بعض ب ليس بالإطلاق إن قيل: اللادوام في الأصل إشارة إلى سالبة كلية لما مر أنه إشارة إلى مطلقة موافقة في الكم مخالفة في الكيف لما حصل قيداً له، وهو قيد لموجبة كلية، فيلزم أن يكون عكسه سالبة كلية لما تقرر أن السالبة الكلية تنعكس سالبة كلية. فالجواب: أن محل ذلك ما لم تضم وتكون تابعة لغيرها، وإلا فتنعكس سالبة جزئية، وهنا تابعة لكلية الصدر. (قوله: لصدق ... إلخ) أي: لصدق نقيضه موجبة كلية دائمة، وهي: كل (ب ج) دائماً. العطار: الشيء عن نفسه، فإن نتيجة الضم الأول: كل متحرك الأصابع متحرك الأصابع دائماً؛ كما صرح به الشارح ومعلوم أن هذا ليس فيه سلب الشيء عن نفسه؛ بل إثباته لنفسه، وليس من قبيل المحال بل من اللغو في القول، فذلك احتيج إلى الضم الثاني لتحصيل نتيجة سلب الشيء عن نفسه. (قوله: وهنا تابعة ... إلخ) فيه أن اللادوام في قضية العكس صدره جزئية لا كلية عكس ما يقول، فالصواب في الجواب أن يقول: إن اللادوام في الأصل صدره كلية فكان إشارة إلى كلية وفي العكس صدره جزئية فكان إشارة إلى جزئية إذ هو يتبع الصدر في الكم ويخالفه في الكيف.
As for the absolute temporal (al-hiniyya al-mutlaqa), it is: Some B is J while it is B; because it is necessary for the conditional and the general conventional, and the necessary of the two general ones is necessary for the two specific ones. As for the non-permanence (al-ladawam), it is: Some B is not J absolutely; because if it were false, then 'Every B is J always' would be true. (His statement: 'As for al-hiniyya' means: as for the truth of al-hiniyya. And his statement: 'Some B J while' means: Some B is J absolutely while... etc., so its modality is omitted. Similarly is said in his earlier statement: 'truth of some B J' etc. His statement: 'And the necessary of the two general ones... etc.' That is like in animal and human, for the necessary of animal, such as movement, is necessary for human. His statement: 'And as for al-ladawam' means: as for the truth of al-ladawam in the proposition of conversion. His statement: 'And it is some B is not absolutely' if it is said: al-ladawam in origin refers to a universal negative because it was mentioned that it refers to a absolute proposition agreeing in quantity but differing in quality for what occurred as a restriction for it, and it is a restriction for a universal affirmative, so it follows that its converse is a universal negative because it is established that the universal negative converts to a universal negative. The answer: that the place of that is when it is not joined and subordinate to another, otherwise it converts to a particular negative, and here it is subordinate to a universal premise. His statement: 'would be true... etc.' means: its contradictory would be true, a universal affirmative permanent, which is: Every B is J always. al-Attar: the thing from itself, for the result of the first syllogism is: Every moving-finger is moving-finger always; as the commentator explicitly stated, and it is known that this does not involve negating a thing from itself; rather affirming it of itself, and it is not impossible but rather idle talk, so the second syllogism was needed to obtain the result of negating a thing from itself. (His statement: 'And here it is subordinate... etc.') contains that al-ladawam in the proposition of conversion has a particular premise, not universal contrary to what he says. The correct answer is to say: al-ladawam in origin has a universal premise, so it referred to a universal, and in conversion it has a particular premise, so it referred to a particular, for it follows the premise in quantity and opposes it in quality.
Source pages p. 708

Commentary
This passage defines two temporal modalities used in modal logic and conversion: al-hiniyya al-mutlaqa (absolute temporal) and al-ladawam (non-permanence). The author first defines al-hiniyya al-mutlaqa as a proposition of the form 'Some B is J while it is B', meaning the predication holds only during the existence of the subject. This modality is necessary for the conditional and general conventional propositions, and what is necessary for the general is also necessary for the specific. Then he defines al-ladawam as a proposition of the form 'Some B is not J absolutely', meaning the negation holds without temporal restriction; its falsehood would imply the universal affirmative permanent 'Every B is J always'. The commentator Dasooqi expands on these definitions, explaining that the modality of al-hiniyya is often omitted for brevity, and gives an example: just as movement is necessary for animal and therefore for human, the relation holds. He then addresses an objection: one might think that al-ladawam refers to a universal negative, but since it is a restriction for a universal affirmative, its converse ought to be a universal negative. The answer clarifies that this is only the case when the proposition is not subordinate to another; here, because it is subordinate to a universal premise (the original converted proposition), the converse becomes a particular negative. A marginal note critiques this answer, asserting that the quantity of al-ladawam in conversion follows the premise's quantity, so the correct answer is that in the original it is universal, in conversion it becomes particular. Finally, al-Attar comments on the need for a second syllogism to achieve the negation of a thing from itself, as the first syllogism only affirms a tautology (e.g., every moving-finger is moving-finger always), which is not impossible but merely trivial. This passage uses examples from natural philosophy, such as animal and human, to illustrate logical relations. The key takeaway is the careful handling of temporal quantifiers in conversion, which prevents contradictions.

Key Terms
الحينية المطلقة
اللادوام
العكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No B is J while it is B.

Key Takeaway
The conversion of al-ladawam from a particular negative to a particular negative when subordinate prevents a logical contradiction.

How Simple Conversion Can Lead to Contradiction
Demonstrates using two syllogisms that certain conversion produces contradictory conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two syllogisms that prove a modal conversion leads to contradiction and identify the contradictory conclusions.

Core Idea
Simple conversion of a modal universal produces a tautology and its negation.

This passage demonstrates a powerful technique: using syllogistic reasoning to test the validity of a logical operation. The author shows that if you apply simple conversion to a certain original proposition and then combine the converse with the original's parts, you get two contradictory conclusions. This reveals that the conversion cannot be valid, as it would lead to the impossible conjunction of opposites. Follow along as we break down the two syllogisms step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and its two parts
Find the two statements that compose the original: the universal affirmative modal 'Every C is B always' and the universal absolute negative 'No C is B absolutely'.
The original is a complex proposition that combines two different modality types.
2
Form the converse of the original
The converse is 'Every B is C always' (since the original is a universal affirmative modal). This is the minor premise used in both syllogisms.
The conversion preserves truth only if the modality is appropriate; here it is assumed for the argument.
3
Run the first syllogism
Combine the converse with the first part of the original: 'Every B is C always' and 'Every C is B always as long as C' yield 'Every B is B always'. This is a tautology.
The syllogism is in the first figure: middle term is C, major term is B, minor term is B.
4
Run the second syllogism
Combine the same converse with the second part: 'Every B is C always' and 'No C is B absolutely' yield 'No B is B absolutely'. This directly contradicts the first conclusion.
The second syllogism is also first figure: middle term C, major term B, minor term B, with a negative major premise.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter: On Simple Conversion. And you combine it as a minor premise to the first part of the original, which is our statement: 'Necessarily or always, every C is B as long as it is C', resulting: every B is B always. Then you combine it as a minor premise to the second part of the original, which is our statement: 'Nothing of C is B with absolute generality', resulting: nothing of B is B absolutely, so there follows the conjunction of the two contradictories. (His statement: 'as a minor premise') means: in the state of being a minor premise. And his statement: 'from the original', which is necessarily: every (B is C) as long as (C is) not always. (His statement: 'and it is') i.e., the first part is our statement. And his statement: 'results: every (B is B)'; i.e., necessarily every (B is B). And his statement: 'then you combine it'; i.e., the universal affirmative absolute permanent which is the contradictory of the second part of the converse. (His statement: 'then you combine it') i.e., the mentioned proposition which is the contradictory of the second part of the converse. (His statement: 'so there follows the conjunction of the two contradictories') i.e., because 'nothing of B is B' necessitates 'not some B is B absolutely', and it contradicts 'every B is B always'. (His statement: 'the two contradictories') intended: the two mutually exclusive ones, for the result of the first syllogism is a universal affirmative, and the second is a universal negative, so intended by the two contradictories is the first result obtained from combining. al-Attar: (His statement: 'then you combine it') i.e., the minor premise of the first syllogism, which is 'every (C is B)', so the arrangement of the syllogism is: every (B is C) and nothing of (C is B), so the result: nothing of (B is B). And you have known the benefit of this second combination. And perhaps one might imagine that the pronoun in 'then you combine it' refers to the nearest mentioned. (His statement: 'i.e., necessarily... etc.') what is in the commentator: it results 'every B is B always' by making permanence the modality of the result, so what reason is there to mention necessity together with the modality of permanence when they do not occur together in a single proposition?! End quote from al-Sharnubi.
Source pages p. 709

Commentary
This passage is a syllogistic proof that if a certain conversion scheme is applied, it leads to a contradiction. The chapter on simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) presents a thought experiment: take a universal affirmative premise (the original) and its converse. The original consists of two parts: (1) 'Every C is B always, as long as it is C' and (2) 'No C is B absolutely'. The author then combines the converse of the original (a universal affirmative 'every B is C' with the modality 'always' or 'necessarily') as a minor premise to the first part, producing 'every B is B always' (a tautology). Then, the same converse is combined with the second part, producing 'no B is B absolutely' (a self-negation). These two conclusions are contradictory (one says every B is B always, the other says no B is B absolutely). Thus, the original conversion leads to a contradiction, proving that such a conversion is invalid. The commentator Dasooqi clarifies the arrangement: the first syllogism uses the universal affirmative modal premise, and the second uses its contradictory (the absolute universal negative). Al-Attar rephrases the second syllogism as: every B is C, and no C is B, therefore no B is B. He also notes that the pronoun in 'then you combine it' might be misunderstood. A marginal note by al-Sharnubi questions why the commentator mentions both 'necessity' and 'always' as modalities in the same proposition, since they are incompatible. This passage is a classic example of using syllogistic reasoning to demonstrate a logical impossibility, thereby justifying restrictions on conversion. The example uses abstract terms (B, C) but the reasoning is applicable to any subject, such as in fiqh: if every intoxicant is forbidden always, and no intoxicant is wine absolutely, then the converse would lead to a contradiction, showing the need for careful modal distinctions.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
اجتماع النقيضين
الجزء الأول/الثاني من الأصل

Simple conversion of a universal affirmative modal leads to contradiction when combined with a contradictory modality, proving the conversion is invalid.

Conversion of Temporal and Existential Propositions to the General Absolute
Lists conversion results for modal propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which modal propositions (temporal, existential, absolute) convert to the general absolute and explain why composite propositions convert to simple ones.

Core Idea
Composite modal propositions lose their temporal or existential restriction when converted, yielding the unrestricted general absolute.

This passage from the Tadhhib commentary clarifies how certain modal propositions—temporal, existential, and absolute—convert. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying immediate inference in Islamic logical analysis, particularly in legal and theological argumentation where modal nuances matter. The main text states the conversion results, and the commentary explains why composites convert to simples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the propositions listed in the main text
Find the terms: 'al-waqtiyyatan' (the two temporal: waqtiyya and muntashira), 'al-wujudiyyatan' (the two existential: la-da'ima and la-daruriyya), and 'al-mutlaqa al-'amma' (the general absolute).
Ensure you distinguish the composite from the simple forms.
2
Determine their conversion result
All these propositions convert to a general absolute (mutlaqa 'amma). The commentator emphasizes this and adds that the absolute temporal and absolute muntashira also convert to general absolute.
The result is a simple proposition, not a composite.
3
Understand why composites convert to simples
The commentary states that the meaning of composites is derived from simples, so there is no need to preserve the composition in the converse. The converse merely expresses the underlying simple meaning.
This principle applies to all four composite affirmatives (except the two possible propositions).
4
Resolve the textual ambiguity about case endings
Al-'Attar corrects the parsing: 'waqtiyatan' is nominative (conjunct to 'al-da'imatan'), and 'mutlaqatan 'ammatan' is accusative (conjunct to 'hinhiyyatan mutlaqatan' from an earlier line). This ensures the text is read correctly.
The marginal note prevents misreading the conversion rule.

Commentary
This passage specifies the conversion results for certain modal propositions. The main text states that the two temporal (waqtiyya and muntashira), the two existential (la-da'ima and la-daruriyya), and the general absolute all convert to a general absolute proposition. The commentator (Dassuqi) explains that the general absolute is a simple proposition. He also notes that the time-delimited absolute (al-waqtiyya al-mutlaqa) and the spread absolute (al-muntashira al-mutlaqa) similarly convert to a general absolute. The reason composite propositions convert to simples is that the meaning of composites is derived from simples, so the composition is not preserved in the converse. Al-'Attar then clarifies that the phrase 'general absolute' is a predicate for a preceding phrase about the contradictory of the two temporal, but he corrects this: the main text and the primary commentary do not contain 'contradictory' because the context is conversion, not contradiction. He also corrects the case endings: 'waqtiyatan' is nominative, not accusative, as it is conjoined to 'al-da'imatan', and 'mutlaqatan 'ammatan' is accusative, conjoined to 'hinhiyyatan mutlaqatan' from a previous line. The marginal note from al-Sharnubi points out that the author likely intended 'al-waqtiyyatan' to include both temporal and spread types, making the rule cover all eight simple and seven composite affirmative propositions, except the two possibility propositions which do not convert.
