Conversion of Modal Propositions into Absolute General
Explanation and proof of conversion of four modal propositions to absolute general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a temporal or existential proposition into an absolute general proposition and justify the conversion using a reductio argument.

Core Idea
Four modal composites convert into one simple: the absolute general.

In this lesson, we examine a critical refinement in the logic of conversion. The author establishes that time-bound, spread, non-permanent, and non-necessary propositions all convert into the absolute general. This is a crucial result because it shows that the modalities are not preserved: the converted proposition loses its temporal or existential qualification. The proof uses a syllogistic reductio, which we will walk through step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four modal propositions
The passage lists two temporal (waqtiyya and muntashira) and two existential (la da'ima and la daruriyya). Note their definitions: waqtiyya asserts at a specific time, muntashira at some time, non-permanent not always, non-necessary not necessarily.
2
State the conversion claim
Each of these four converts into the absolute general (mutlaqa 'amma). That means the result is a simple universal or particular categorical without any temporal or modal qualification.
3
Understand the objection
An objector points out that the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative, not a universal negative. So the proof that uses universal negative as contradictory might be flawed.
4
Examine the author's response
The author replies that the universal negative implies the particular negative because it is more specific; therefore the proof still holds. The universal negative is sufficient to derive the contradiction.
5
Walk through the reductio proof
Assume the conversion is false, so its contradictory is true. Combine that contradictory with the original premise in a first-figure syllogism, derive an impossible conclusion (e.g., 'No moon is a moon'), thus the conversion must be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَ) تنعكسُ (الوَقْتِيَّتَانِ)؛ أي: الوقتيَّةُ والمنتشرة (وَالوُجُودِيَّتَانِ)؛ أي: اللادائمةُ ، واللأضروريَّةُ ، وَالْمُطْلَقَةُ العَامَّةُ ؛ مُطْلَقَةٌ عَامَّةٌ)؛
فَإِنْ قِيلَ : إِنَّ النَّتيجتين ليس بينَهُما تناقض ؛ لأنَّ الموجبة الكلية نقيضُهَا السَّالبة الجزئيَّةُ ؛ لا الكلِّيَّة، وهنا جُعِلَ نقيضُها سالبة كلّيَّةٌ، إلَّا أَنْ يُقال: يلزم من وجودِ السَّالبة الكلِّيَّةِ وجودُ السَّالبة الجزئية ؛ لأنّها أخصُّ منها .
(قَوْلُهُ : وَالْمُطْلَقَةُ الْعَامَّةُ) هذهِ من البسائط ، ومثلُها (۱) الوقتية المطلقة، والمنتشرة المطلقة تنعكس مطلقةً عامةً .
(قَوْلُهُ: مُطْلَقَةٌ عَامَّةٌ وإِنَّما عُكِسَتِ المركَّبات الأربع بسائط؛ لأنَّ المعنى من المركَّباتِ مُستفادٌ مِنَ البسائط، فكانَ التَّركيب حينئذ لا حاجة له،
لأنَّه إِذَا صَدَقَ كُلُّ ج ب بإحدى الجهات الخمس المذكورة، فبعض ب ج بإحدى الجهات الخمس المذكورة. وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو: لا شيء من ب ج بتلك الجهة، فيضم ذلك النقيض إلى الأصل، وينتج محال، فيثبت العكس.
(قَوْلُهُ: كُلُّ ج (ب) أي : كلُّ قمرٍ مُنخسف في المثال المذكور .
(قَوْلُهُ : بِإِحْدَى الْجِهَاتِ ... إِلَخ) هيَ الضَّروريّةُ المقيَّدةُ بوقت معيَّن في الوقتية. والضَّروريَّةُ المقيَّدةُ بوقت ما في المنتشرة .
بيان ذلك بالمواد في الوقتية أن تقول : لأنه إذا صدق بالضَّرورة: كلُّ قمر مُنخسف وقت الحيلولة لا دائماً؛ صدق: بعض المنخسفِ قمر بالإطلاق، وإلا ، لصدق نقيضُة سالبة كلية دائماً، وهو: لا شيء مِنَ المنخسف بقمرٍ دائماً ، فتضم ذلكَ النَّقيض كُبرى إلى الجزء الأوَّلِ مِنَ الأصل بأنْ تقول: هكذا بالضَّرورة كلُّ قمرٍ مُنخسف وقت الحيلولة، وَلَا شيء مِنَ المنخسفِ دائماً، دائماً، ينتج : لا شيء مِنَ القمر بقمر وهو محال نشأ من نقيض العكس، فيكون العكسُ حقًّا.
And the two temporal propositions are converted—meaning the time-bound and the spread—and the two existential propositions—meaning the non-permanent and the non-necessary—into the absolute general; absolute general. For if it is said: The two conclusions are not contradictory because the universal affirmative's contradictory is the particular negative, not the universal. Here its contradictory is made a universal negative, unless it is said: From the existence of the universal negative follows the existence of the particular negative because it is more specific. His statement: 'and the absolute general'—this is from the simples, and like it the absolute time-bound and the absolute spread convert into absolute general. His statement: 'absolute general'—the four composites are converted into simples because the meaning from the composites is derived from the simples, so composition then has no need. Because if every J is B in one of the five mentioned modalities, then some B is J in one of the five mentioned modalities. Otherwise, its contradictory would be true, namely: Nothing that is B is J in that modality, so that contradictory is adjoined to the original, and an impossibility results, so the conversion is established. His statement: 'every J is B' means: every moon is eclipsed in the given example. His statement: 'in one of the modalities... etc.'—they are the necessary qualified by a specific time in the temporal, and the necessary qualified by some time in the spread. An explanation of that by examples in the temporal: You say: Because if it is true by necessity: Every moon is eclipsed at the time of opposition not permanently; then it is true: Some eclipsed is a moon absolutely; otherwise, its contradictory would be true, a universal negative permanent: Nothing that is eclipsed is a moon permanently. Then you adjoin that contradictory as a major premise to the first part of the original, saying: Thus, by necessity: Every moon is eclipsed at the time of opposition, and nothing that is eclipsed is a moon permanently, permanently; it yields: Nothing that is a moon is a moon, which is impossible, arising from the contradictory of the conversion, so the conversion is true.
Source pages p. 710

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) by al-Dasuqi and al-‘Attar on the conversion of four complex modal propositions into a simple absolute general proposition. The author begins by stating the rule: the two temporal (time-bound and spread) and the two existential (non-permanent and non-necessary) all convert into the absolute general. This is a claim about immediate inference (al-‘aks al-mustawi). An objection is raised: the method used earlier assumes that the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a universal negative, but in standard logic the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative. The author answers that the universal negative implies the particular negative because it is more specific, so the proof remains valid. The commentator then notes that these composites convert into simples because the meaning of a composite is derived from simples, so there is no need for composition in the result. The proof is given using a reductio argument: assume every J is B in a modality (e.g., necessity, temporal), but if the conversion 'some B is J' were false, then its contradictory 'no B is J' in the same modality would be true. When this is combined with the original proposition in a syllogism, it yields an impossible conclusion, thus proving the conversion. The example uses the temporal modality: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of opposition not permanently' converts to 'Some eclipsed is a moon absolutely'. If not, then 'Nothing eclipsed is a moon permanently', which combined with the original gives 'No moon is a moon', which is impossible. The proof uses a categorical syllogism in the first figure with a universal negative major premise and a universal affirmative minor premise, which is valid only if the negative is taken as permanent, but the contradiction shows the conversion holds.

منتشرة
وجودية
مطلقة عامة

Key Takeaway
The conversion of temporal and existential propositions to absolute general is a valid immediate inference, proven by reductio, that simplifies modal premises for syllogistic use.

Proof of Conversion for Temporal Propositions
Proof of simple conversion for temporal statements via contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof by contradiction that validates the simple conversion of temporal universal affirmative propositions.

Core Idea
Denying the converse of a temporal universal leads to an impossible self-contradiction.

Simple conversion (عكس مستوي) normally applies to categorical propositions. But what about propositions with temporal qualifiers? The commentator extends the rule to such modalities by showing that if the converse were false, a logical impossibility follows. This lesson unpacks that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
State the conversion rule for temporal propositions
The rule: if a universal affirmative proposition in any of the five temporal modes is true, then its converse (particular affirmative) is true absolutely (without temporal qualification).
2
Identify the temporal example (waqtiyya)
The example: 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of opposition (not always)' – this is a universal affirmative with a specific time bound.
The predicate is 'eclipsed at the time of opposition' and the subject is 'moon'.
3
Assume the converse is false
For proof by contradiction, assume the converse 'Some eclipsed thing is a moon' is false. Then its contradictory 'No eclipsed thing is a moon always' must be true.
This negation is a universal negative with 'always' qualifier.
4
Construct the syllogism
Take the first part of the original proposition ('Every moon is eclipsed at the time of opposition') as the minor premise, and the negation of the converse as the major premise. This yields a first-figure syllogism: All moon are eclipsed at opposition; no eclipsed thing is a moon always; therefore, no moon is a moon always.
The conclusion is absurd because it says 'No moon is a moon' – a contradiction in terms.
5
Draw the contradiction and validate the converse
Since the premises (original part and negation of converse) are both true (by assumption), the absurd conclusion shows that the assumption is false. Hence the original converse is true: 'Some eclipsed thing is a moon' absolutely.
This method works for expanded-temporal (مُنْتَشِرَة) by replacing the specific time with 'at some time'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي
لأنَّه إذا صَدَقَ كلّ ج ب بإحدى الجهات الخمس المذكورة، فبعض ب ج بالإطلاق.
بيان ذلك بالمواد في الوقتية أن تقول: لأنه إذا صدق بالضَّرورة: كلُّ قمر مُنخسف وقت الحيلولة لا دائماً؛ صدق: بعض المنخسفِ قمر بالإطلاق، وإلا، لصدق نقيضُة سالبة كلية دائماً، وهو: لا شيء مِنَ المنخسف بقمرٍ دائماً ، فتضم ذلكَ النَّقيض كبرى إلى الجزء الأوَّلِ من الأصل بأنْ تقول: هكذا بالضَّرورة كلُّ قمرٍ مُنخسف وقت الحيلولة، وَلَا شيء مِنَ المنخسفِ دائماً، دائماً، ينتج : لا شيء مِنَ القمر بقمر وهو محال نشأ من نقيض العكس، فيكون العكسُ حقًّا، وكذا يُقالُ في المنتشرةِ، إِلَّا أَنَّكَ تبدلُ الوقت المعيَّنَ بوقت ما. ويُقال مثل ذلك في الوجوديتين والمطلقة العامة.
ج بالإطلاق، وإلا فلا شيء من ب ج دائماً، وهوَ معَ الأصل، ينتجُ : لا شيء من ج ج دائماً، وإنَّه محال.
Section: On Simple Conversion. Because if 'Every J is B' is true in one of the five mentioned modes, then 'Some B is J' is true absolutely. Explanation of that using the temporal matter: if necessarily 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of opposition, not always' is true, then 'Some eclipsed thing is a moon' is true absolutely; otherwise, its contradictory 'No eclipsed thing is a moon always' would be true. Then you combine that contradictory as major premise with the first part of the original, saying: 'Necessarily every moon is eclipsed at the time of opposition' and 'No eclipsed thing is a moon always' yields 'No moon is a moon', which is impossible, arising from the contradictory of the converse, so the converse is true. Similarly for the expanded-temporal, except you replace the specific time with 'at some time'. And the same is said for the existential [universal affirmative] and the absolute general. [Then:] 'J [is] absolutely, otherwise no B is J always', and this together with the original yields 'No J is J always', which is impossible.
Source pages p. 711

Commentary
This passage explains the method of proving the rule of simple conversion (al-‘aks al-mustawi) for temporal propositions. The rule states: if a universal affirmative proposition in one of the five modalities is true, then its converted form (particular affirmative) is true absolutely. The commentator uses the example of the moon's eclipse at the time of opposition. To prove that 'Some eclipsed thing is a moon' must hold, he supposes the negation of the converse: 'No eclipsed thing is a moon always'. Combining this negation with the original proposition 'Every moon is eclipsed at the time of opposition' in a first-figure syllogism (with the negation as the major and the original as the minor) yields the absurd conclusion 'No moon is a moon'. Since the premise set is true, the negation of the converse must be false, hence the converse is true. The same reasoning applies to the expanded-temporal (muntashira) by replacing the specific time with an indefinite time. The commentator also mentions that this method extends to the existential (wujūdiyya) and absolute general (muṭlaqa ʿāmma) propositions. The argument is a proof by contradiction (burhān khulfī) showing that denial of the converse leads to logical impossibility.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
الوقتية
المنتشرة
الوجودية
المطلقة العامة

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion of temporal universal affirmatives is proven by reductio: denying the converse leads to a self-contradictory syllogistic conclusion.

Ibn Sina's Denial of Conversion for Possible Propositions
States that the two possible propositions do not convert on Ibn Sina's view, with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Ibn Sina denies the conversion of possible propositions and contrast his view with al-Farabi's.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's conversion rule for possible propositions depends on the actual existence of the subject.

This lesson examines a key disagreement between Ibn Sina and al-Farabi regarding the conversion of possible propositions. The passage states that, on Ibn Sina's view, the two types of possible propositions (general and specific) do not convert because the subject term must actually be true of its individuals at the time of judgment. Understanding this condition is essential for correctly applying conversion in modal logic, especially in Islamic sciences where such inferences appear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible propositions
Recognize that 'the two possible propositions' (al-mumkinatān) refer to the general possible (mumkina ʿāmma: 'possibly all') and the specific possible (mumkina khāṣṣa: 'possibly some'). These are modalized forms of universal and particular propositions.
2
Locate Ibn Sina's condition
Find the phrase 'which requires the actual existence of the description of the subject for its individuals'. This means that for a proposition like 'Every human is a writer' to be considered, each subject (human) must actually be human at the moment of judgment.
The example uses 'sperm-drop' (nuṭfa) – which is not yet human.
3
Apply the condition to the conversion
If the original proposition is true under Ibn Sina's condition, its conversion ('Some writers are human') would require that the predicate (writer) actually applies to its individuals. But a writer might exist who is not actually human (e.g., a sperm-drop if it became a writer later?), so the conversion fails.
The key is that the subject term in the converted proposition must also satisfy actual existence.
4
Compare with al-Farabi's view
Al-Farabi does not require actual existence; he allows the subject to be merely conceived. Therefore, the sperm-drop would be included as a human, and conversion would be valid for him.
5
Summarize the ontological implication
Logical rules like conversion are not purely formal; they depend on substantive philosophical assumptions about what it means for a term to be true of individuals.
This dispute reflects broader debates about the nature of possibility and necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا عَكْسَ لِلْمُمْكِنَتَيْنِ) العامَّةِ والخاصة على مذهب الشَّيخِ. مثلاً : كلُّ إنسانٍ كاتب، فعلى مذهبِ الشَّيخِ المشترط لثبوتِ وصفِ الموضوع لأفراده بالفعل؛ لا يتناولُ النُّطفة، فإنَّها لم يثبت لها الإنسانيَّةُ بالفعل، وأمَّا على مذهب الفارابي؛ فإنَّه يتناول النُّطفة.
And there is no conversion for the two possible propositions (general and specific) according to the doctrine of the Master [Ibn Sina]. For example: 'Every human is a writer'. According to the Master's doctrine, which requires the actual existence of the description of the subject for its individuals, it does not include the sperm-drop, because humanity has not actually been established for it. As for al-Farabi's doctrine, it would include the sperm-drop.
Source pages p. 712

Commentary
This passage declares that, on Ibn Sina's view, the two types of possible propositions—the possible general (mumkina ʿāmma) and the possible specific (mumkina khāṣṣa)—do not have a valid conversion. The reason relates to Ibn Sina's condition that the subject term must actually apply to its individuals at the time of judgment. For a universal affirmative possible proposition like 'Every human is a writer', the subject 'human' must actually be predicated of each individual. But in the case of a sperm-drop (nuṭfa), it is not yet actually human; hence the statement does not cover it. Under this condition, if the proposition were true, its conversion to 'Some writers are human' would not hold because the subject 'writer' might refer to individuals that are not actually human at the time. Al-Farabi, however, allows the subject to be merely conceived or hypothetical, so conversion would be permitted. This discussion highlights the importance of ontological commitments in logical rules.

Ibn Sina's Condition for Converting Possible Propositions
Commentary explains Ibn Sina's condition for converting possible propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Ibn Sina's condition of actuality to determine whether a possible proposition converts validly.

Core Idea
Conversion of possibility requires the subject description to be actual.

Ibn Sina imposes a critical condition on the conversion of possible propositions: the description of the subject must be actually true of it. This lesson unpacks that condition and shows how it affects the validity of the converse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original possible proposition
Take a proposition of the form 'Every J is possibly B' (e.g., 'Every traveler is possibly exempt from fasting'). The subject description is 'traveler', which must be actual for the proposition to be meaningful.
2
Check the actuality of the subject description
Ibn Sina requires that the description 'J' be actually true of the subject. In the example, each traveler is actually a traveler now.
3
Construct the converse and test it
The converse would be 'Some of what is actually B is possibly J'. For the traveler example: 'Some of what is actually exempt from fasting is possibly a traveler'. This may be false because a person exempt due to travel may later cease to be a traveler.
Ibn Sina's condition is about actuality in the original proposition, not in the converse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي. فإنَّه يشترط في وصف الموضوع أن يكون ثابتاً للموضوع بالفعل. فعلى هذا يكون مفهوم: كل ج ب بالإمكان: أن كل ما هو ج بالفعل ب بالإمكان. ومن الجائز أن يكون ب بالإمكان ولا يخرج من القوة إلى الفعل أصلاً، فلا يصدق في عكسه: بعض ما هو ب بالفعل ج بالإمكان. وأما على مذهب الفارابي فجائز انعكاسهما كنفسهما.
Section: On Simple Conversion. For it is required in the description of the subject that it be actually attributed to the subject. So on this basis, the concept of 'Every J is B with possibility' is: 'Everything that is actually J is possibly B'. And it is possible that B is with possibility and does not exit from potency to actuality at all. Then its converse, 'Some of what is actually B is possibly J', is not true. As for the school of al-Farabi, their conversion is permissible like themselves.
Source pages p. 713

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion rule for possible propositions and presents the disagreement between Ibn Sina and al-Farabi. The commentary opens by stating the condition for conversion: the description of the subject must be actually true of it. Thus, when you say 'Every J is possibly B', it means that every individual that is actually J is possibly B. For example, 'Every donkey is possibly ridden by Zayd' means every actually existing donkey can be ridden by Zayd. Now, for the converse to be true, we would need 'Some of what is actually ridden by Zayd is possibly a donkey'. However, it is possible that Zayd's being a rider of donkeys never becomes actual—he may never actually ride a donkey. Therefore, on Ibn Sina's view, the converse is false because actuality is required in the subject of the converse. Thus, the possible proposition does not convert to a similar possible. In contrast, al-Farabi holds that both the general and specific possible propositions convert to exactly the same modality—general possible remains general possible, and specific possible remains specific possible—because the possibility itself is the condition, not actuality. This is an important point of divergence in the logic of conversion.

Key Terms
عكس مستو
ممكنة
بالفعل

D) The modal term must be actual.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's condition of actuality means that a possible proposition does not always convert to a similar possible proposition; the converse may be false if the predicate never becomes actual.

Ibn Sina vs. al-Farabi on Conversion of Possibles
Commentary contrasts Ibn Sina's and al-Farabi's positions on conversion of possibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare Ibn Sina's and al-Farabi's positions on the conversion of possible propositions and identify the key difference regarding actuality.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina requires actuality; al-Farabi allows conversion preserving modality.

Ibn Sina and al-Farabi disagree on the conversion rule for possible propositions. While Ibn Sina imposes an actuality condition that can invalidate the converse, al-Farabi holds that the converse retains the same modal status. This lesson explores the dispute and its logical implications.

Guided Path
1
Restate Ibn Sina's condition
Ibn Sina requires the subject description to be actual (bi-l-fi'l). For 'Every J is possibly B', the converse 'Some B is possibly J' is false if B never becomes actual.
2
State al-Farabi's view
Al-Farabi holds that the converse of a possible proposition is itself possible without any further condition. Both the general and specific possible convert to the same modality.
3
Identify the crux of the dispute
The disagreement hinges on whether actuality of the subject description is needed for the converse. Ibn Sina says yes; al-Farabi says no—possibility itself suffices.
4
Test with an Islamic example
Take 'Every traveler is possibly exempt from fasting' (Ibn Sina: converse may be false if exemption never actual; al-Farabi: converse is true as possible).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي. فإنَّه يشترط في وصف الموضوع أن يكون ثابتاً للموضوع بالفعل. فعلى هذا يكون مفهوم: كل ج ب بالإمكان: أن كل ما هو ج بالفعل ب بالإمكان. ومن الجائز أن يكون ب بالإمكان ولا يخرج من القوة إلى الفعل أصلاً، فلا يصدق في عكسه: بعض ما هو ب بالفعل ج بالإمكان. وأما على مذهب الفارابي فجائز انعكاسهما كنفسهما.
Section: On Simple Conversion. For it is required in the description of the subject that it be actually attributed to the subject. So on this basis, the concept of 'Every J is B with possibility' is: 'Everything that is actually J is possibly B'. And it is possible that B is with possibility and does not exit from potency to actuality at all. Then its converse, 'Some of what is actually B is possibly J', is not true. As for the school of al-Farabi, their conversion is permissible like themselves.
Source pages p. 713

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion rule for possible propositions and presents the disagreement between Ibn Sina and al-Farabi. The commentary opens by stating the condition for conversion: the description of the subject must be actually true of it. Thus, when you say 'Every J is possibly B', it means that every individual that is actually J is possibly B. For example, 'Every donkey is possibly ridden by Zayd' means every actually existing donkey can be ridden by Zayd. Now, for the converse to be true, we would need 'Some of what is actually ridden by Zayd is possibly a donkey'. However, it is possible that Zayd's being a rider of donkeys never becomes actual—he may never actually ride a donkey. In contrast, al-Farabi holds that both the general and specific possible propositions convert to exactly the same modality—general possible remains general possible, and specific possible remains specific possible—because the possibility itself is the condition, not actuality. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'Every human is possibly a knower' (كل إنسان يحتمل أن يكون عالماً). According to al-Farabi, what is the converse? According to Ibn Sina, is that converse valid? Provide reasoning using the actuality condition.

Create a brief jadal-style exchange: one person defends Ibn Sina's condition, another objects using al-Farabi's view. Use an Islamic example such as 'Every believer is possibly forgiven'.

Conversion of Modal Possible Propositions: al-Shaykh vs. al-Farabi
Commentary explains why al-Shaykh denies conversion of two modal propositions while al-Farabi affirms it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the conversion of modal possible propositions and contrast the positions of al-Shaykh and al-Farabi using a concrete example.

Core Idea
Modal conversion depends on whether the subject's attribute is actual or merely potential.

This lesson explores a key disagreement in Islamic logic: do modal possible propositions convert? Al-Shaykh (Ibn Sina) says no, al-Farabi says yes. The commentary by al-Dasuqi and al-Attar unpacks the reasoning using an example of a donkey that is only potentially Zayd's mount. By understanding the terms of this debate, you will master the conditions for valid conversion of modal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal type of the original proposition
The original proposition is 'Every donkey is Zayd's mount potentially'. This is a general possible (al-mumkina al-āmma) because the possibility is not restricted to a specific time or actualization.
In modal logic, 'potential' here means it is possible that the subject has the predicate, but it may never become actual.
2
Distinguish the two positions
Al-Shaykh requires the subject to be actual for conversion to be valid. Since 'donkey' here is only potential (not actual), the converse 'Some of Zayd's mount actually is a donkey potentially' would require an actual mount that is a donkey, which does not exist. Al-Farabi does not require actuality; he converts potential to potential.
Al-Dasuqi explains that al-Shaykh's condition stems from the subject's attribute being potential, not actual.
3
Apply the example to see the failure
Assume Zayd only rides horses. The original claim 'Every donkey is Zayd's mount potentially' is true (since all donkeys could possibly be his mount). But the converse 'Some of Zayd's mount actually is a donkey potentially' is false because his actual mounts are horses, not donkeys.
Al-Attar's commentary provides this concrete scenario to illustrate the logical asymmetry.
4
Generalize the condition for modal conversion
If the subject term (C) is only potential and never actual, the conversion from 'Every C is B potentially' to 'Some B actually is C potentially' fails because the converse requires an actual B that is C. However, if both terms remain potential, as al-Farabi holds, conversion holds: 'Some B potentially is C potentially'.
The key is whether the modality of the subject is preserved or changed from potential to actual.

CHAPTER: ON THE SIMPLE CONVERSION. And it is possible that (B) is potential and never emerges from potency to act, so the converse 'Some of what is (B) actually is (C) potentially' is not true. As for according to al-Farabi's doctrine, their conversion like themselves is permissible. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'And it is possible that (B) is' means that the description of (B) is established for its individuals potentially, i.e., in potency. His statement: 'and does not emerge' means: while (B) potentially does not emerge from potency to act. His statement: 'so the converse is not true... etc' means: because his actual mount is not the donkey, so the meaning of 'Every (C) is (B)' is: every donkey is Zayd's mount. His statement: '(C)' means: donkey potentially. His statement: 'like themselves' means: the general possible proposition converts to a general possible, and the specific possible proposition converts to a specific possible. Al-Attar: This proposition [means] that everything actually characterized by donkeyship is Zayd's mount potentially, and it is possible that the potential mount never emerges from potency to act at all, so then the converse 'Some of what is Zayd's mount actually is a donkey potentially' is not true. For this reason, al-Shaykh held the opinion that the two possible propositions do not convert. And his statement: 'because he did not stipulate in the description of the subject its actual existence for the subject, but was satisfied with potentiality, so the meaning of Every (C) is (B) is: Everything that is (C) potentially is (B) potentially, and it converts to: Some of what is (B) potentially is (C) potentially.' Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'for the subject' means: for its individuals. His statement: 'was satisfied with potentiality' means potency. His statement: 'the meaning of Every (C) is (B)' means: with potentiality, so he omitted its modality. Al-Attar: His statement: 'so the meaning of: Every C is B... etc' means: the meaning of our statement for example: Every donkey is Zayd's mount potentially, assuming that Zayd never rode except the horse and never rode a donkey at all, is that everything characterized by donkeyship potentially is Zayd's mount potentially, and the mentioned proposition converts to our statement: Some of what is Zayd's mount potentially is a donkey potentially, and that is what is desired.
Source pages p. 714

Commentary
This passage discusses a key issue in the conversion of modal propositions: the difference between al-Shaykh (Ibn Sina) and al-Farabi regarding whether two types of possible propositions (general and specific possible) convert. The passage begins with a heading indicating a new chapter on simple conversion. The main text states that if the subject term (B) is only potentially true of its individuals and never becomes actual, then the converse proposition 'Some of what is actually B is potentially C' is false. On al-Farabi's view, both possible propositions convert like themselves (i.e., general possible to general possible, specific possible to specific possible). Al-Dasuqi's commentary explains each part: 'B' means the predicate is potentially true, 'does not emerge' means it remains potential, and the meaning of 'Every C is B' in this context is that every donkey is Zayd's mount (but only potentially). The reason the converse fails is that Zayd's actual mount is not a donkey, so the actual mount class does not include a donkey. Al-Attar adds an example: Suppose 'Every donkey is Zayd's mount potentially' is true, but Zayd never rode a donkey (only a horse); then the converse 'Some of what is Zayd's mount actually is a donkey potentially' is false because there is no actual mount that is a donkey. Thus al-Shaykh denies conversion of the two possible propositions. On the other hand, if one does not require actual existence for the subject, as al-Farabi does, then the meaning of 'Every C is B' is that whatever is C potentially is B potentially, and it converts to the same modal type. The commentators clarify that the modality is omitted but implied. This highlights how the truth conditions of modal conversions depend on whether the subject's attribute is taken in actuality or potentiality.

الممكنة الخاصة
العكس المستوي
الشيخ

Conversion of Universal Negative Permanent Propositions
Author states the conversion rule for permanent universal negatives with proof by contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rule for permanent universal negatives, justify it by contradiction, and identify why necessary negatives convert only as permanent absolute.

Core Idea
Two permanent universal negatives convert to a permanent universal negative; necessary negatives convert only as permanent absolute.

This lesson unpacks a key conversion rule in modal logic: universal negatives that are permanent (either absolutely permanent or necessary) convert to a permanent universal negative. We will see how commentators Al-Dasuqi and Al-Attar clarify the rule and provide a rigorous proof by contradiction, showing why necessary propositions do not retain their modality after conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two permanent negatives
Al-Dasuqi specifies that 'the two permanent ones' are the absolute permanent (dāʾima muṭlaqa) and the absolute necessary (ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa). Both assert that the negative relation holds perpetually.
In some texts, 'da'im' may refer only to temporal permanence; here it includes necessary.
2
Understand the conversion rule
The rule states: If a universal negative is either absolutely permanent or absolutely necessary, its converse (swap subject and predicate) is a universal negative that is permanently absolute (not necessarily permanent in the original modality).
The converse is always a permanent absolute, not necessarily necessary.
3
Examine the counterexample for necessary conversion
Al-Dasuqi gives an example: If Zayd never rode a donkey, 'Nothing of Zayd's mount is a donkey necessarily' is true, but 'Nothing of the donkey is Zayd's mount necessarily' is false (donkey could be ridden). Thus the necessary does not convert as necessary.
The modality weakens from necessary to absolute permanent.
4
Follow the proof by contradiction
Al-Attar proves the rule: Assume the converse is false; then its contradictory (some stone is human) is true. Combine this with the original (no human is stone) in a syllogism: Some stone is human (minor), No human is stone (major) → Some stone is not stone, which is impossible. Thus the converse must be true.
This proof uses the principle that a true proposition cannot lead to an impossibility.
5
Apply to new Islamic examples
Practice converting and proving rules with examples from fiqh and kalam, e.g., 'No intoxicant is permissible' converts to 'No permissible is intoxicant' (permanent absolute).
Always check modality: if original is necessary, the converse is only absolute permanent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَا يَنْعَكِسُ مِنَ الْمُوَجَّهَاتِ السَّالِبَةِ
(وَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ تَنْعَكِسُ الدَّائِمَتَانِ دَائِمَةً)؛
الدَّسُوقِيُّ: (قَوْلُهُ: وَمِنَ السَّوَالِبِ تَنْعَكِسُ الدَّائِمَتَانِ) أَيِ الدَّائِمَةُ الْمُطْلَقَةُ، وَالضَّرُورِيَّةُ الْمُطْلَقَةُ. (قَوْلُهُ: دَائِمَةً) أَيْ دَائِمَةً مُطْلَقَةً. وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ تَنْعَكِسِ الضَّرُورِيَّةُ كَنَفْسِهَا؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَطَّرِدُ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ الشَّيْخِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يَصْدُقُ عَلَى مَذْهَبِهِ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنْ مَرْكُوبِ زَيْدٍ بِالْحِمَارِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ، إِذَا كَانَ زَيْدٌ لَمْ يَرْكَبِ الْحِمَارَ أَصْلًا، وَعَكْسُهَا كَنَفْسِهَا: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْحِمَارِ بِمَرْكُوبِ زَيْدٍ بِالضَّرُورَةِ، وَذَلِكَ لَا يَلْزَمُ.
الْعَطَّارُ: (قَوْلُهُ: الدَّائِمَتَانِ) إِلَى قَوْلِهِ: (بَعْضُ (ب) لَيْسَ (ب) وَأَنَّهُ مُحَالٌ)، مِثَالُهُ: إِذَا صَدَقَ بِالضَّرُورَةِ أَوْ دَائِمًا: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ، وَجَبَ أَنْ يَصْدُقَ دَائِمًا: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْحَجَرِ بِإِنْسَانٍ. وَإِلَّا أَيْ: وَإِنْ لَمْ يَصْدُقْ هَذَا الْعَكْسُ، لَصَدَقَ نَقِيضُهُ، وَالْعَكْسُ دَائِمَةٌ سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ، فَيَكُونُ نَقِيضُهَا مُطْلَقَةً عَامَّةً مُوجَبَةً جُزْئِيَّةً، وَهِيَ قَوْلُنَا: بَعْضُ الْحَجَرِ إِنْسَانٌ بِالْإِطْلَاقِ، وَيُضَمُّ هَذَا النَّقِيضُ إِلَى الْأَصْلِ بِأَنْ يُجْعَلَ صُغْرَى، وَالْأَصْلُ كُبْرَى.
What converts from the negative modal propositions: And from the negatives, the two permanent ones convert as permanent. Al-Dasuqi: His statement: 'And from the negatives, the two permanent ones convert' means: the absolute permanent and the absolute necessary. His statement: 'as permanent' means as absolute permanent. And they did not convert as necessary like themselves because it is not universal according to al-Shaykh's doctrine; for it is true according to his doctrine: 'Nothing of Zayd's mount is donkey by necessity', if Zayd never rode a donkey at all, and its converse like itself: 'Nothing of the donkey is Zayd's mount by necessity' is not entailed. Al-Attar: His statement: 'the two permanent ones' to his statement: 'some B is not B and that it is impossible'. Example: If it is true necessarily or permanently: 'Nothing of the human is stone', then it must be true permanently: 'Nothing of the stone is human'. Otherwise, i.e., if this converse is not true, its contradictory would be true, and the converse is a permanent universal negative, so its contradictory is a general absolute affirmative particular, which is our statement: 'Some stone is human absolutely'. And this contradictory is joined to the original by making it the minor premise, and the original as the major premise.
Source pages p. 715

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion rule for negative modal propositions: two types of permanent universal negatives (the absolute permanent and the absolute necessary) convert to a permanent universal negative. Al-Dasuqi notes that the necessary proposition (e.g., 'Necessarily, nothing of Zayd's mount is a donkey') does not convert as necessary because according to al-Shaykh, if Zayd never rode a donkey, the original is true but the converse 'Necessarily, nothing of the donkey is Zayd's mount' is false (since the donkey could have been ridden). So it only converts as a permanent absolute. Al-Attar provides a proof: if 'Nothing of humans is stone' is true necessarily or permanently, then its converse 'Nothing of stone is human' must be true permanently. If it were false, its contradictory 'Some stone is human' would hold. Combining this with the original in a syllogism (with original as major and contradictory as minor) leads to a contradiction (some stone is not stone), showing the converse must be true. The passage is a clear application of the rule of conversion by contradiction.

Key Terms
الدائمتان (al-dāʾimatān): Two types of permanent propositions: the absolute permanent (dāʾima muṭlaqa) and the absolute necessary (ḍarūriyya muṭlaqa), both asserting perpetual truth. [Note: In some texts, 'da'im' may refer only to temporal permanence; here it includes necessary.]
السالبة (al-sāliba): A negative proposition (e.g., 'No S is P'). [Note: Stable term.]
نقيض (naqīḍ): Contradictory of a proposition; for a universal negative, the contradictory is a particular affirmative. [Note: Standard usage across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because conversion of necessary propositions is always invalid.
B) Because if Zayd never rode a donkey, the original is true but the converse is false (donkey could be ridden).
C) Because necessary propositions only convert to particulars.
D) Because the converse is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Permanent universal negatives convert to permanent universal negatives, but if the original is necessary, the converse is only permanently absolute, not necessary.

Conversion of Possibility Propositions in 'Aks Mustawi
Author defines conversion of affirmative possibility proposition and explains rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the 'aks mustawi rule to convert universal affirmative possibility propositions from Islamic sciences and explain the condition of possibility.

Core Idea
Conversion of possibility propositions preserves possibility, not actuality.

In this lesson, we examine how logicians treat conversion of propositions where the predicate is only possibly true of the subject. The passage explains that for universal affirmative possibility propositions, the converse is a particular affirmative possibility. Understanding this rule prevents errors in arguments where modality is overlooked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Find the universal affirmative possibility proposition (كل ج ب بالإمكان). Note that the modality is possibility, not actuality.
2
Understand the condition
The predicate does not have to be actually true of the subject; it suffices that it can possibly apply. The commentator equates possibility with potentiality (قوّة).
3
Apply the conversion
Swap subject and predicate, reduce quantity to particular, and keep the modality as possibility. The result is: بعض ب ج بالإمكان.
The rule for affirmative universals normally yields a particular affirmative converse.
4
Verify with the example
The passage implies: 'Every donkey is possibly ridden by Zayd' converts to 'Some of what is possibly ridden by Zayd is a donkey.' Check that the possibility modality is preserved.
5
Note the commentator's clarifications
The commentator explains that 'for the subject' refers to its individuals, and possibility means potentiality. He also notes that the modality may be omitted when context shows it is possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي الْعَكْسِ الْمُسْتَوِي

لأنَّه لم يشترط في وصف الموضوع ثبوته للموضوع بالفعل، بل اكتفى بالإمكان، فيكون مفهوم كلُّ جب: «إِنَّ كلَّ ما هو ج بالإمكان ب بالإمكان وتنعكس إلى: بعضُ ما هو ب بالإمكان ج بالإمكان».

(قَوْلُهُ: لِلْمَوْضُوْع) أي: لأفرادِهِ.
(قَوْلُهُ: اكْتَفَى بِالْإِمْكَانِ) أي: القوَّة.
(قَوْلُهُ: مَفْهُوْمُ كُلِّ ج (ب) أي: بالإمكان، فقد حذف جهتها.
Section on Conversion (al-‘aks al-mustawi). Because it is not required in the description of the subject that the predicate is actually true of it; rather, it suffices with possibility. So the meaning of 'Every J is B' is: 'Everything that is J by possibility is B by possibility,' and it converts to: 'Some of what is B by possibility is J by possibility.' (His statement: 'for the subject' means: for its individuals. His statement: 'it suffices with possibility' means: potentiality. His statement: 'the meaning of every J (B)' means: by possibility; thus its modality has been omitted.)
Source pages p. 715

Commentary
This passage defines the conversion (عكس مستوي) of a universal affirmative possibility proposition. The author explains that in such a proposition, the subject does not need to actually possess the predicate; it is enough that the predicate can possibly apply to the subject. For example, 'Every donkey is possibly ridden by Zayd' means that anything that is a donkey can possibly be ridden by Zayd. Its converse is 'Some of what is possibly ridden by Zayd is a donkey.' The commentator (al-Dasuqi) clarifies that 'for the subject' refers to its individuals, and 'possibility' means potentiality. He also notes that in the phrase 'the meaning of every J (B)', the modality is omitted because the context shows it is possibility.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the converse of 'Every J is possibly B'?
A) Every B is possibly J
B) Some B is possibly J
C) No B is possibly J
D) Some B is necessarily J

Key Takeaway
Universal affirmative possibility propositions convert to particular affirmative possibility propositions, not to universal ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition: 'Every example of riba is possibly prohibited by analogy (كل مثال للربا يمكن تحريمه بالقياس).' Write its correct converse using the 'aks mustawi rule for possibility.
?
Identify a proposition from fiqh that uses possibility modality and convert it. Check that you keep the quantity as particular and modality as possibility.
